Oh, sons, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last on the science of medicine, for its completion I have achieved its end, and through its explanations and declarations, it seems to me that I should complete it for you with this treatise, which is part of the operation by hand, that is surgery.
For the operation by hand is prostrate in our region and in our time totally deprived, so much so that the science of it nearly perished, and its vestiges are removed; and nothing remained of it except some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which changed hands and errors and hesitations befell them until its intentions are closed and its strength and art are elongated.
And it seems to me that I should revive it with the ordering of this treatise on it according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and so that it may come with the forms of cautery instruments and other instruments of the work as it is by addition of the declaration, and by preparation of it, which thing is necessary.
But the reason why a good practitioner with his hand is not found in our time is because the art of medicine is long, and it is fitting for its practitioner before that to be exercised in the science of anatomy which Galen recounted until he knows the uses of the members and their forms, and their conjunction and separation; and has the knowledge of the bones, nerves and muscles, and their number, and their emergence; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their exit.
And for this Hippocrates says that with a name it is much, but in the work little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of that in the introduction of this book; for who is not knowledgeable of what we have said about anatomy, is not exempt from falling into error by which men will die, as I have seen many of those, for some are trained in this science and throw themselves into it without knowledge and without experiment.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced doctor incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he cut some arteries of the neck so that great blood flowed until the woman fell dead between his hands.
And I saw another doctor who took that he would extract a stone from a man who had advanced in his age, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, for which that man on the third day died.
And I was already called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient I knew about him that he would die.
And I saw another doctor who took a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and it happened to a castrated black man who had a fracture in his thigh; and the doctor prepared with his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with pillows and splints with strong binding, and did not leave the wound to breathe.
And afterward he untied it according to his desires, and afterward he retied it, and afterward he left it for some days and ordered that we should not untie the binding; and it stayed thus until the thigh and the foot became abscessed, and it came to ruin.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to untie the binding, and tranquility was achieved and thus his pains diminished.
But however corruption was already confirmed in the limb, for which I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another doctor who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated after some days, until its malignancy was magnified with its lord.
Which thing is because the hot cancer is pure of melancholic humor, then it is not fitting that it should be approached with iron at all, unless it be on a member which could suffer to be entirely removed.
And for this, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation by hand be divided into two divisions: that is, into operations which are associated with health, and into work which is perilous in the major dispositions.
And I have already excited this in every place of this book in which came an operation in which there is error and fear; for which it is necessary for you to guard this and leave that, so that fools do not find a way to speak and to dishonor you.
And thus take your weapons with solicitude and with the protection of God, and your patients with happiness and with firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God leading to health and to praiseworthy succession, and leave the terrible diseases of difficult healing, and remove your weapons from that which makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not evade you: for more remains to your glory, and higher in the world and lastly to your sanctities.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: "Do not treat bad disease, so that you are not named bad doctors".
And I have already divided this book according to three chapters.
The division of the book begins, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered from the head to the feet, on the forms of instruments and on the iron tools of cauteries, and on that which is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on cutting and perforation, and on cupping glasses, and on wounds, and on extraction of arrows and similar to those, all chaptered and ordered on the forms of instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and on curing of torsion and similar to those; chapter ordered from head to feet, and on the forms of instruments.
It is fitting that I say the quality of the benefits of that cautery and of its harms, and in which complexion cautery is prohibited.
Thus I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and of its harm is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle by science, and hidden secret; and already a great multitude of wise men have spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and for me a little of their discourses suffices, fearing prolongation.
And thus I say that cautery confers to every complexion universally which is with matter or without matter, except two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter they disagreed; for some said that cautery is beneficial to it, and others said the contrary of that, that cautery is not suitable in disease which is by quality and by dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is unsuitable that hot and dry disease be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And he said who says the contrary of that, that by cautery benefit is made in every hot and dry disease that comes in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and its humidities to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say by the discourse of that, that experiment has already sometimes revealed this to me.
But however it is not fitting that he come upon this who is already proven and exercised in the chapter of cautery by last exercise, and has known the diversity of the complexion of men and the disposition of diseases in themselves, and their causes, and their accidents, and the space of time of those.
But in the other complexions, there is no fear upon you, and especially in cold and humid diseases, for already all the doctors have agreed upon those, and they have not disagreed on the benefit with cautery in those.
And know, sons, that of the secret of curing by cautery with fire and of its goodness, this is above cautery with burning medicine, because the fire is simple, the action of which does not reach except to the member which is cauterized and does not harm another member joined with it except with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches to that in the members which are distant from it, and very easily brings disease of difficult healing to the member and very easily kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except that it is superfluous. And it is already known to us by experiment and by length of service of study with the art on the truth of things; and for this I am excused from the length of the discourse. And if it were not that it is not allowed in my book in this I would set down for you a hidden secret with fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies and of its expulsion by which it expels diseases, with demonstrative philosophical discourse, which would be sharpened in your thoughts.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time in which it is suitable to do cautery and they set the best time as spring.
And I say that cautery is suitable at all times, because the harm by accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is attracted by the cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains or for necessary, strong and fistulous pains, which do not suffer delay because it is feared that malice may come from them, which is greater than the little harm entering through time.
And this does not fall in your thoughts, oh sons, this is not estimated except by the vulgar people and foolish doctors, that is that for that disease which is healed by cautery there is not always a return, and they set that consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they think for this, because cautery is nothing but in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humidities which are the cause for which the pains come.
But however cautery surpasses medicine by the velocity of its validity and virtue of its operation and greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that his disease returns at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his disease, and his strength, and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and that which has come to him in his acquisition in foods and similar things; unless the disease in which cautery is administered is a subtle disease in a member of few superfluities and humidities, such as cautery of tooth for pain and similar to that, it is possible that in him that pain does not return, but this happens in small cases.
And more vulgar discourse is that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is righteous discourse, not to that which they understand in it, for they believe that there is no cure that profits with medicine nor with other thing after cautery.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer the manners of cure in some diseases, and those medicines do not confer, and afterward at the end of the matter one administers cautery, and it profits to that. And then from here it falls that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the intention which the vulgar and many of the foolish doctors understand.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they said this only because of the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not make pus, though this is not absolutely true, for I have already proved this and have found that it does not do this except in some bodies without the others, and cautery with that is good and better than with iron as they said; but however when you make cautery with fire with gold, it is not declared to you, for it is ignited according to the quantity that you want by the redness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you add ignition over it, the gold melts by the fire, and some sheets fall in the fire by which the artificer falls by the occupation of that.
And for this, cautery is made with iron by us more easily and more proximately to rectitude of operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and have ordered those from the head to the feet, so that they lighten the one demanding that which he wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization confers to the humidity and coldness over the brain, accident of which are two causes, that is cause of headache and multitude of rheumatic flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and multitude of sleeping, and of tooth pain and of the throat, and universally of all disease that comes by coldness, such as paralysis, stupor and apoplexy, and similar to those diseases.
The form of this cauterization is this, that firstly you command the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine cleansing the head, for three or four nights, according to what the strength of the patient and his age and his custom require.
And afterward command him to shave his head with a razor, and afterward make him sit between your hands on a square bench and that he place his hands on his chest.
And afterward place the root of your palm near the pulse over the root of the nose between his eyes. But however, when your middle finger has reached, mark that place with ink, and afterward ignite the olive-shaped cautery of which this is the form:
And afterward place that over the place marked with ink, in a manner that descends, with which your hand should be constrained little by little, and you revolve that.
And afterward lift your hand lightly and look at the place, and if you see already some quantity of the bone of the head to be uncovered, like the quantity of a grain of vetch, then raise your hand, and if not return your hand with that iron, with another of that, if it is cooled, until that which I have said to you is seen of the bone.
And afterward take a little salt and dissolve it over the place and leave it for three days.
And afterward place cotton soaked in butter over it and leave that over it until the scab from the fire is destroyed. And afterward treat the place with tetrapharmacon ointment until it is healed.
And they already said that when the wound remains open, the better it expels the pus, which is better and more helpful.
And some of them said that the skin should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some part of the bone's surface is burned.
And afterwards, what is burned of the bone should be scraped, and then it should be cured.
And others said that with the cautery one should reach to the back, until it makes a strong impression on the bone until the bone falls, and this is the form of small alphecati: And they estimate that vapors from the head expire from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and afterwards it should be cured until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men by way of fear; and leave that, in my opinion, it is better with health, when it is; because the head is weakened by the solution of continuity of it as we have testified in other members, and especially when the patient's head is of weak nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and I use that and work with it safely.
Second Chapter.
On the cautery of the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs in the whole head and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses jars and beds and head purges and plasters and cats and puppies and hens and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we recount and none of these things benefit him, then consider that if the patient's head is of strong creation in its nature; and it is not weak and finds very great cold, then cauterize that with another cautery below that one a little. And afterwards cauterize that over the horn of his head with cautery until the skin is separated. Cut the skin and let it be uncovered from the bone the quantity which we have recounted and cauterize that with a cautery in the back part of the head in the place which is called vas, — and that is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And ease your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, because the patient finds very great pain different from the pain of all the other cauteries of the head.
And I will say this cautery in its place, because it is appropriate that the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle place of the head is cauterized; and this is the form of it.
Third Chapter.
On cauterization of non-chronic migraine.
When sudden pain occurs in part of the head, or pain extends to the eye, evacuate the head with purifying medicines, and administer the other cure, which I have described in the division of illnesses; and if all doesn't help, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with acute burning medicine, or with iron.
But cautery with burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, peel it and cut off its ends on both sides. And then cut the painful area in the temple with a wide lancet until a space is made under the skin, until it is hidden.
And then tighten it with a stopper for proper constriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; and then untie it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then place cotton soaked in butter until the area forms pus.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron is done in this manner: heat for cautery which has this form:
and it is called nail-keys, because its head is in the form of a nail in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it is a small protrusion.
And then place it on the painful area, and hold your hand — so that the artery which is below is not burned — and turn the iron little by little with speed.
And if the quantity that is burned through the thickness of the skin is about half of it, raise your hand so that the artery which is below is not burned, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it for three days; and then place cotton moistened with butter, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the end of the knife-like second cautery, and guard against cutting the artery in this properly non-chronic migraine.
Chapter four.
On cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat migraine with what we have said about the preceding cure, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the cure is not effective, and you see that the disease is not alleviated by what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or cautery with fire, then it is necessary that you heat the knife cautery until it becomes white-hot, then mark the location of the pain with a line half the length of a finger, or thereabouts; and place your hand once and press it until you cut the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful of the continuation of the jaw, which moves during chewing, and that the muscle is not burned and the nerve which moves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the protection and caution of blood flow from the artery that is cut, because in cutting it there is fear, especially for one who ignores what he does and is not studious and experienced; and leave the operation at the beginning.
And I will come with a reminder of the management of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this cautery is not sufficient for this disease and you see the patient's body can tolerate it, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on constriction of artery with cautery having two knives, and this cauterization is better and more conferring.
Chapter 5.
On cauterization of ear pains.
When pain comes to the ear from cold and is treated with laxative things and with the other cure which I have said in the division, and the pain is not removed, then heat the cautery, and this is the one which is called "puncta", of which this is the form:
And after puncturing after the heating, and let it be moved around the entire ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circuit of each together if the pain is in each. Extend yourself with the cautery from the root of the ear little by little after you have marked the place with a probe.
And if the cautery of ten points in the whole ear, and almost that, and afterwards treat the places until they are healed.
Chapter 6
On the cauterization of torticollis (or facial paralysis).
Torticollis which is cured by cauterization is only of the types which come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that occurs due to dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of torticollis with jars, head purgatives, and gargles, and your treatment doesn't work, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauterizations: one at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the sick part, because softening only comes in the part that appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize with the cautery at the right of the highest extremity of the ear a little above the corner of the head, and another at the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the thumb, and apply your hand with the cautery until half the thickness of the skin is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife-like instrument, the form of which is set before. But it is more subtle than that, a little like you see; and it should be a knife in place of excessive thickness a little.
And afterwards, treat the place with what has been prescribed before until it is healed.
Chapter 7
On the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said and your medication doesn't work and the patient doesn't have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauterizations: above all the corners of his head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery at the nape of the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as said above.
And it is already cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is more behind; and afterwards it should be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
On cauterization for forgetfulness caused by phlegm.
It is appropriate to give the patient at the beginning large pills that purify the brain.
And afterwards shave the patient's entire head and place on the back of their head a plaster of cedar described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cautery. And do this according to the same order which I have already mentioned.
And then if by these he is healed it is good; and if not, then make three cauterizations on the rear part of his head, which should descend from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And place between each cautery the thickness of a finger, and afterwards treat the cautery with what has proceeded.
But if you want addition and the patient can tolerate it, then make a cautery in the middle place; and if you want more addition, then cauterize above the two horns.
Afterwards treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form given before.
Chapter 9.
On cauterization for paralysis and softening of the whole body.
It is appropriate that you proceed in purifying the head with pills and with what we have said; and afterwards shave the patient's head, and afterwards cauterize it with cautery in the middle of the head, and with cautery above all the horns of the head, and with cautery on the rear part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body of many and the patients tolerating and the illness is strong, then cauterize with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and lastly with cautery until much of the skin is burned, and lift your hand. And afterwards treat that until it is healed, and this should be an olive-shaped cautery.
Chapter 10.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And so it is appropriate that you purify his brain at the beginning with pills and with the other treatment which we have mentioned in the division when the patient is large and is tolerating the administration of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not suffer the medicines, then administer gargles and chewing substances that purify the brain, before this for many days, with improvement of the foods.
And afterwards shave the patient's head, and afterwards cauterize it with one cautery in the middle of the head according to the preceding manner, and with another cautery on the back of his head, and cauterize above each horn of his head. And if the patient is strong and is tolerating, then cauterize with the cauteries which I have mentioned, in having paralysis and softening of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped as preceded.
But if the patient is a child, then place a subtle cautery according to this manner.
Chapter 11
On the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cautery we mentioned for paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is an excess tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give to drink that which purifies the brain according to what was previously mentioned in the division.
And then shave the patient's head, and then make a proper circular bandage of linen, and then place it in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated squarely, and be held from all sides.
And then apply a liniment; and it is best made of old sheep's butter. And heat it over the fire for moderate warming, which the aforementioned can tolerate when it is applied to him. And then pour it in the middle of his head in the circle, and leave it until it has cooled.
And do this with the patient once every week, with the other good regimen until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize him making small points according to his condition, unless you hold the hand with a cautery ordered thus: this is a type of cautery which moistens the brain equally.
And then place cotton soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter 12
On the cauterization of water descending in the eye.
When the beginning of water in the eye is declared to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient to drink that which purifies his head, and protect it from all humidities.
And make him sweat in a bath for some days.
And then command him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries over the two temples if it is the beginning of water coming out in both eyes, or on one side if it is the beginning of water in one eye.
Cut with cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin; and let the cauteries be far in the width of the temples.
And guard against blood flow when it is cauterized; and if you see blood flow from it, stop it with some medicine, which is possible for you.
And I will come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the cutting of those and caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck under two bones with two cauteries produced at the back.
Chapter 13
On cauterization of chronic tears.
When tears from the eyes are chronic and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the visible part of the head outside, and it is verified that this is cold phlegmatic superfluity, then cauterize it with the cautery which we have described in the chapter on the beginning of water falling in the eyes, and with cautery in the middle of the head and with two cauteries on the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries on the neck below two bones. And if you need additional treatment, then cauterize it on all sides of the corner of the eye above the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14
On cauterization for foul smell of the nose.
When you treat it with what we have said in the division and your medication does not help, then prepare and give the patient the cochias to drink for three nights; and after shaving his head, cauterize it with a medium cautery with an olive-shaped cautery. And after, cauterize it with a nail-shaped cautery with two cauteries above the two eyebrows below the hair a little.
And be careful of the artery that you do not cut it.
And this is the form of the nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter 15
On cautery for softening of the eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery with this lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you wish, cauterize it above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in each part, and let them be distant from the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too much with the cautery, but according to the amount that a third part of the eyelid is burned.
Chapter 16
On cautery of the eyelid when the hairs are turned inward and prick the eye.
When the eyelashes are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cautery on them is done in two ways, for cautery is either with iron or with burning medicine.
If with cautery of it with fire, command the patient before this to leave his hairs as those which clean them, until they are lengthened and evened; and if they prick him towards their growth, then you tighten his eyes with a band so that it is not moved until the hairs have grown.
Then when they will have grown and evened, then place the patient's head in your lap, and after mark on his eyelid with ink, with a mark which is in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And after, place cotton soaked in egg white or in mucilage of psyllium on the eye.
And after, heat the cautery of which this is its form:
And then cauterize over the figure which you have marked, little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin is burned, which is like a myrtle leaf, all appearing properly.
And a sign of the health of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs of it raised towards the puncture of the eye.
And then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and afterwards place cotton with butter on it until the scab is removed. And afterwards treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if something returns through the hairs after their growth and the eyelid is softened, then do the cautery again over all of it just as you had done at first.
But if the hairs are on the lower eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is evened out and so that the hairs do not prick the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine is this: that you command the patient to leave the hairs until they are prolonged and evened out, and afterwards make the shape of a myrtle leaf from a piece of cloth.
And then take known soap and quicklime, one ounce each, or thereabouts, and then mix these things well, and do this quickly so that it does not cool; and then spread this over the paper which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place it over one eye or both; and place cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and let the patient's head be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it a little and do not move it until the patient feels the burning of the medicine, for the burning is in it like fire.
And so when he perceives that burning sensation, then leave the medicine and move it with your finger; so when the burning stops, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider if you see the eyelid is already raised as it is washed from the constriction with fire or with incision, for then it is good; and if not, return some of the medicine to the place where the medicine has not impressed and does not darken until your operation is evened out and the eye is contracted.
And afterwards place cotton with butter on it until the skin of the burn is removed, and afterwards treat it with deafinicon ointment or with another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, you must in your operation guard with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after some days is softened and something of the hairs pricks the eye, then repeat your medicine on that place of the eyelid which is softened, and properly as you did at the beginning, and afterwards surround it until it is healed.
And this is the shape of a myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size and smallness, and so according to this your contraction is made.
And the way of rectitude will not be hidden from the one who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
On cauterization of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment doesn't work, then it is appropriate that you cauterize it in this manner: command the patient to rest their head on your lap, and have some assistant hold their head between your hands in such a way that it is not moved or shaken with their head.
And then place cotton soaked in egg white or in psyllium mucilage over their eye.
And then heat the cautery which has this form:
Which should be concave, like the shape of a cane or a vulture's feather, from one end with which the cautery is made.
And if you want, it should be penetrating up to the other end. And if you want it to be solid like a probe.
But however, the one that is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the corruption from it.
And then place the cautery over it which should be well heated, and hold your hand a little towards the cautery of the eye to the part of the nose, so that your hand does not err or the patient is not disturbed, and then place the cautery in the corner of the eye and destroy it.
And so if at the beginning of your cautery it reaches to the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like this for three days, and then place cotton with butter over it and around it with drying ointment until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it is not healed, then place sharp corrosive medicine over it until the bone is uncovered and is scraped according to what is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another manner of cautery for fistula which some first said: attend to the place of the fistula and split it and then place in that fissure subtle chamomile of which this is the form: And melt in it the quantity of 1 ounce of melted lead; and hold your hand with the chamomile with good retention, and the patient should not be moved so that the melted lead does not reach their eye; and it is appropriate that you place over the patient's eye cotton soaked in egg white or in water, because lead hardens the place of the fistula and heals it by marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a hole in the nose and reduce the fistula to the course of the nose according to what will come in its proper place.

Chapter 19
On cauterization of fistula in the mouth.
When an abscess occurs at the root of the uvula or in the palate or at the roots of the teeth and then forms pus, and then breaks, and then becomes chronic, and pus runs and a fistula is formed, and then you treat it and it doesn't heal, then it's necessary that you heat a cautery according to the size that can be received in the fistula, and then put that heated instrument in the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth, doing this once or twice at the end; and then treat it with what we have said about the cure until it is healed.
And so if the infection is cut off and it is healed, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to uncover the place and remove the corrupted bone according to what will come in its chapter.
Chapter 20
On cauterization of the teeth and softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines and it doesn't help, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery whose form will come after this, so that you place the cannula over the tooth and introduce into it the heated cautery quickly, and hold your hand a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth.
And then lift your hand, and then repeat the cautery several times as you wish.
And then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth is secured and the softened gum is tightened and the corrupted moisture is dried out.
Chapter 21.
On cauterization for tooth pain.
When tooth pain is due to coldness, or there is cold or worm in it, or treatment with medicine doesn't work, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with cow dung, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with dung is when you take cow dung and boil it in an iron spoon, then take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the boiled dung, and place it quickly on the painful tooth until it cools.
And then repeat this several times until the power of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cotton in the aforementioned dung, and place it on the aching tooth, and place a heated iron on it until the iron reaches the depth of the tooth's cavity.
But cauterization with fire is when you use a tube of copper or iron, and there should be some thickness in its core so that the intensity of the fire doesn't reach the patient's mouth.
And then heat the cautery, the shape of which will come, and place it on the tooth and hold it with your hand until the cautery cools; and do this several times because the pain will be removed either that day or the next.
And it's appropriate that after the application of this cautery, the patient fills their mouth with good butter and holds it for an hour and then spits it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize with whichever end you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the tube:
Chapter 22.

And afterwards treat it with ointment and probes and cloths until it is healed.

Chapter 26.
On cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors come into the stomach until they extract it from its complexion and rheumatisms are multiplied in it, and if you continue the ways of curing and they do not confer, then it is appropriate that the patient be on his back supine, and extend his hands and his thighs, and after cauterize him with three cauteries: with one cautery below the chest by the quantity of one finger with a nail cautery, and with two cauteries below the two sides of the cautery, until the figure of the cautery comes triangulated; and separate between them so that they are not joined which will make putrefaction.

Chapter 27
On cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain occurs in the liver due to cold, humidity, or excessive gas until it is thrown out of its normal composition by excessive excretion, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it doesn't work, then the patient should lie on his back, and mark with ink three cautery points in this form and this same quantity on the liver below the hypochondria, where the patient's body rests.
And let the space between each cautery be the width of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the straight length of the body.
And do not press too hard with the cautery.

And it is possible for you to cauterize these cauteries with a knife-like cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And be careful not to proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the thickness of all the skin be burned so that the whole body is burned and it reaches to the intestines, because the skin there is thin; and know this.
Chapter 28
On perforation of liver abscess with cautery.
When an abscess or apostema occurs in the liver and you want to know if that apostema is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient finds heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver, with the pain there is great sharpness and you see that the doctors are already fatigued with its cure.
Therefore, it is appropriate that the patient be turned on his back; and then mark the location of the apostema with ink; and then heat the cautery which is similar to a ray, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the skin is burned, until it reaches the peritoneum, until pus comes out.
And afterwards, treat it by curing the other apostemas until it is healed.
And it is not appropriate that this form of cautery be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is prolonged, and in whose hands many diseases have been known; but this with experience sometimes be bold on this operation, but however leave that to us, and it is better.
Chapter 29.
On cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that cautery of the roots of aristolochia for pleurisy should be done in this manner: take from the roots of long dry aristolochia a root as long as can be found, and let it be of the thickness of a finger.
And then submerge it in oil and heat it in the fire, and then cauterize with a cautery in that place which is between the continuation of the clavicle with the neck; and with two small cauteries below the guideline, declining a little to the part that is under the beard, and with two cauteries in that which is declining between the fifth and sixth rib, declining a little to the back parts; and with another cautery in the middle place of the chest; and another over the stomach, and with three cauteries of the back parts; and with a cautery in that place which is between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries of the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between two shoulders.
And it is not suitable that you press your hand with the cautery, but let it be manifest on the skin in work.

But in this perforation with cautery there is not without fear, for one dies soon or a fistula occurs in the place for which there is no healing.
Chapter 30.
On the cauterization of the spleen.
When you cure the disease of the spleen with what we have said about the cure in the division and your cure doesn't work, then cautery is used according to three methods, all of which are proper, of which one is that you make three or four cauterizations along the length of the spleen according to the shape of the liver cauterizations, the shape of which has preceded.
And between each cautery should be the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cautery should be the manner which we have said in the liver cautery equally.
And do not press your hand with the cautery; and the patient should be lying on their back.

And your raising with which you raise the skin should be according to the length of the body, so that cauterizations are made according to the width of the body.
And then introduce the two heated prongs, very hot, in that area until you penetrate the skin on the other side with them.
And then extract the cautery; and thus four cauterizations are made.
And if you want, cauterize with a cautery having three prongs.
And afterwards you treat the cautery after it has stopped discharging pus for many days, for it is more beneficial than the other preceding things in the treatment.
Chapter 31.
On cauterization of dropsy.
Cautery is not appropriate for dropsy except in specific cases.

And the depth of the cautery should be close to the thickness of the skin.
And afterward, leave the cautery open so that it drains for a long time.
And do not evacuate the patient from treatment after the cautery with what is appropriate so that two intentions are added to him, and healing will be hastened with help.
The shape of the cautery with which the belly is cauterized should be nail-like according to the form that is described above, and the one with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped according to its preceding form.

And after, treat it with ointments until it is healed.

Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of hunchback (spinal deformity).
This disease often occurs in young children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it affects their structure in the wing area with elevation and movement.

And so when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery in this form: so that the cautery covers every part of the vertebra equally.

Chapter 44.
On cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pains are caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pains come to the feet, it is the custom of physicians to call this gout properly.
And so when gout from a cold cause is treated with a method of cure and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is that you cauterize it after evacuation around the joint of the foot with many cauterizations.
And let it be an olive-shaped cautery of which this is its form:
If it is necessary to make a point on the surface of the foot, then do it with a cautery point. If the pains rise up to the knees or other joints, which often happens, then cauterize above each knee with three or four cauteries on all sides with this olive-shaped cautery.

If the pains rise up to the knee and back, then administer the cautery which we have mentioned in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, and it is called gout, then puncture in the circuit of the bones as they revolve, puncture in those two lines.
If pain remains in the fingers, then puncture in the circuit of the bones above each joint and on the palm of the hand.
If the pains rise after some days to the elbows or muscles, then cauterize each of them on all parts of the upper arms.
Do not evacuate the patient from good regimen and reception of medicines, for if good regimen is done and phlegm is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God helping.
Chapter 45.
On cauterization of ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin and the intestines descend before the hernia to the genitals, and this is beginning nearby, then it is appropriate that you order the patient to stop his eating on that day and to use soothing things so that the split descends from his belly.
And after, let the patient sit between your hands on his back, and command him to hold his breath until it is expelled or the hernia or intestines appear.
And after, return that with your fingers and mark with a probe the rupture above the pubic bone with a sign the end of which is similar to half a circle at the upper parts of the body.
And after, ignite the cautery of which this is its form:
until it is very red and throws sparks. And after, reduce the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and after place his hand over the place so that the intestine does not come out.
And you have already opened between the thighs of the patient and placed a cushion below him, and another servant is on his legs, and another on his chest, who holds his hands.
And after, place the cautery on that mark with your hand with the cautery standing straight, and hold it until with it you reach the pubic bone up to the middle of the pubis; and repeat with it another time if you have not reached the bone on the first attempt.
Observe carefully the exit of the intestine at the time of your cauterization, so that you don't burn that intestine, which could cause death or other great harm to the patient.
Know that when you don't reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not effective.
It is appropriate that the cautery for children be subtle according to their size and measure.
Afterwards, treat the cauterized area for three days with (unknown substance) until the fire scar is removed.
Then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
The patient should lie on their back for 40 days until the area and wound are sealed.
It is appropriate to adjust their nutrition according to the length of the healing time, which soothes their belly, and so that the intestine doesn't come out due to the effort of healing.
And after they want to get up after 40 days, then administer a properly made bandage, and keep it for another 40 days.
And let them be reduced in work and in filling with food and drink, and from loud voices, for when they use this regimen of complete healing, they will be healed.
I will tell you about the cure for ruptures in other parts of the belly, and this is at the beginning, and if you don't want them to draw out, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let what is extracted from the skin be about a third of it; and afterwards treat it as we have said, and it is not added.
And some earlier (practitioners) have said that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this form, after it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed that goes across the width at the upper side of the rupture, and another line above; and lower your hand with a nail cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46
On cauterization of torsion.
When pain occurs in some limbs due to falling or impact and that pain persists for a long time and is not cured with medicines, then it is appropriate to cauterize with a point cautery.
And let the cautery for each limb be according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of its torsion and pain.
So if it is healed by the first cautery, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is customary for those pains to be punctured from limb to that limb which is close to it, so it is appropriate that you follow them with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers sometimes benefit greatly from cautery, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so when you want to cauterize that, consider if the leprosy is starting and you have treated it with what we have said in the division and it doesn't descend, nor stay, and you fear that the corruption will encompass the patient's entire body and complexion, then cauterize him on the head with 5 cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and the second below it, towards the forehead at the hairline; and both over two horns; and one behind, on the nape or neck.
And move your hand with the cautery a little until it makes a small impression on the bone, and from it the scales are displaced, so that the expiration of thick vapors is alleviated from him; and cauterize over the spleen as previously described.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and appears manifestly, then it is appropriate that you cauterize him with those cauteries which we have mentioned on the head; and with cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheeks, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with a large one on the vertebrae near the tail, and one on the tail, and with two on the hips, and with one on each hip, and with two on the knees and one on each knee, and with two on the elbows, and with two on the muscles, and with two on the tenderness of his chest; and cauterize him on all the joints of the fingers of the hands and feet, and on each heel of his feet, and on the bones of his hands.
And be careful of the nerves which are on the heel that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery on the pubic bone and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you add, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as healthy people do, because his body is already numbed.
And it is appropriate that the cauteries be large or small according to the limbs and joints as preceded by the types of instruments.
And afterwards, treat the cautery with flour of bitter vetch cooked with honey and with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On the cauterization of numbness.
When any limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to what the size of the limb or its smallness merits.
And let your cautery enter the thickness of the skin a little. And afterwards treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes some numbness is cauterized which occurs in the hands and feet, and in the vertebrae of the back and towards the exit of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is removed.
But do not presume to do this unless one is wise in the anatomy of the limbs and the egress of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
On cauterization of albaras which is morphea.
When albaras is old and does not respond to any of the medicinal ointments, then you cauterize over it with a deep cautery, a little according to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness and its color are removed, and its color is changed.
And afterwards treat it with flour of bitter vetch and lentils with rose oil and with leaves of knotgrass, and with pigeon blood or swallow blood, of each take one part and mix them all, and then spread it on a cloth and place it on the spot until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
On cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around its circumference as it is revolving.
And the wise men said that it should be cauterized with the last cautery in the middle; but I have not seen this, because I fear it may ulcerate, and I have already seen this sometimes.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 51.
On cauterization of abscesses.
When any abscess is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the excess causing it, or due to the age of the patient, for he is old in decrepitude, of little blood, or due to the time, and you want to hasten the maturation of the abscess; then cauterize around its circumference with many small puncturing cauteries; and afterwards leave it for it will hasten in maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery of which this is its form: and place it in the middle of the abscess until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that place which is lower, so that the body of the arm is relieved; and afterwards treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
On cauterization of corrosive (ulcers).
Corrosive is a moving corruption in the limb, which corrodes it just as fire consumes dry wood.
So if you see that corrosive in a place which tolerates cautery with fire, then you heat many small nail-like cauteries, and large ones according to what is suitable in that place where the corrosive is.
And afterwards cauterize it from all sides until you eradicate all the corrosion and nothing remains of it.
And afterwards leave it for three days, and place on the cauterized places ground sulphur mixed with oil until all the scabs are eradicated and all the corruption.
And afterwards treat it with flesh-generating ointments.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh grows in which there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery over what remains in the corrupted places.
And corrosive is sometimes treated with sharp medicine, for that is put in place of cautery. But nevertheless, cautery with fire is of easier benefit.
And I have already spoken of its treatment with sharp medicine in the division, and so take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
On cauterization of warts and other similar things.
Many times there occurs in the lower parts of the foot a disease called a wart, and it is a large, split thing, which harms the foot; and cauterization for these is done in two ways, that is cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
But cautery with fire is when you heat the concave cautery resembling a vulture's feather, which is made of iron according to the size that contains the wart on all sides, and has thin edges.
And then place that heated instrument on the wart, and then revolve your hand with the cautery around the wart until the cautery reaches the depth of the wart. And leave it for three days until it begins to discharge pus. And then plaster it with wild mallows crushed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for it will be eradicated from its roots.
And then treat the area with flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if they are not converted, and many times occur on the surface of the body, mainly on the hands and feet, then it is appropriate that you administer cautery with water to them, and it is that you take a cannula of copper or iron or vulture's feather, and place it on the wart; and then pour a small quantity of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand, and you revolve it with compression of your hand completely, and the edge of the cannula should be pressed into the root of the wart, and the hot water will find a way to the depth of the root of the wart; and the patient should wait a little for the stinging of the hot water for an hour, and then remove it, for the wart with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with them one after the other until you come upon all of those that are on the body.
And then treat them after the roots of the warts are eradicated with those ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On cauterization of tremors.
When someone experiences tremors due to cold in the nerve or due to fever from a cold cause, or from another similar cause, then it is appropriate that you cauterize it with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one cautery; and with a cautery on their chest, and on their stomach, with an olive-shaped cautery.
And their tremor subsides and hastens the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
On cauterization of white spots occurring on the body.
When white spots appear on the body, and they are split pustules which are made of thick corrupt matter; then it is appropriate at the beginning when they appear, that it be cauterized over every head of the fistula with a subtle cautery with myrtle wood, the extremity of which is extended with fire; or with roots of long aristolochia; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes there is cautery at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner; and they are not increased, and the superfluity causing them is disrupted, and heals from them.
But however, it is appropriate that this be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
On cauterization of blood flow accidents due to incision of arteries.
Many times, there is a flow from an artery that is cut towards the wound which happens outside, or from the incision of abscesses or cautery of the limb, and similar things, and it is difficult to remove it.
So when this happens to someone, then you hurry with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger on it, pressing it many times until the flow of blood beneath your finger is stopped, and does not come out.
And then place in the fire the small and large olive-shaped cauteries, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then take one of these, small or large depending on the location and the wound where the artery is broken, quickly over that vein after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery until that blood is cut off and restricted.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those prepared in the fire, and do not stop doing this one after another until the blood is restricted.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that no other harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to restrict the blood in those, especially when the artery is large, except by one of four ways: either with cautery as we have said, or with extraction when it is not extracted, because when it is extracted the extremities are better burned and the blood is restricted, or that it be tied with thread or with a ligament by strong tightening, or that medicines be placed on it whose virtue is to restrict the blood; and they should be tightened with a cushion with decent constriction.
But he who administers decent restriction of it with binding and with tightening with cloths or by placing burning things and similar, does not succeed except rarely.
But if this happens to someone and neither the doctor nor medicine is present, then work by placing the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and attend to the great constriction of it until the blood is removed from the upper part of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from the artery; and over the artery let it be sprinkled with very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and further consider what need there is for cautery or medicine.
On incision, perforation, cupping, bloodletting, wounds, and similar things.
We have already spoken in the first chapter about all illnesses in which cautery with fire is appropriate and with burning medicine, and the occasions and causes of it, and the necessary instruments with the forms of cautery; and I have ordered the chapters from the top of the head to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that it may be easier for the one seeking this, when they have asked about it.
And before I begin this, it is important, son, that you learn that in this chapter is by terror that which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of this, it is necessary that there be great caution in it, for in the operation which is in this chapter, many times there occurs evacuation which is with blood with which life depends on the sprinkling of the vein or incision on the abscess or opening of the exit or purging of the wound or extraction of arrow or incision of stone and similar things. And of all these are accompanied fear and trembling and in their hands falls death.
And I warn you that upon you does not fall that in which there is hesitation, for sometimes there fall to you kinds of men with many kinds of illnesses. And of them is someone who is angered by his illness and death is light for him, and this is because of the vehemence of what he finds of his illness, and because of the length of his illness, and with the illness is fear which signifies death.
And of those is he who gives you his possessions and says, you hoping with that health, and his illness is pernicious.
And therefore it is not appropriate that you consent to that one who comes to you of whom this is the manner; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your rapacity; and do not presume anything about this except after certain knowledge which verifies this for you, and to which praiseworthy success comes.
And use in the curing of all your patients prognostication and indication of that to which health is converted, for in that is help for you in acquiring fame and memory, praise and glory.
O, son, may God inspire in you His understanding and not remove from you direction and protection, for that is in His hand.
And I have ordered this chapter distinguishing by chapters according to what preceded in the chapter on cautery, from the cure of the head to the feet, so that it may be easier for you what you ask about this and of which you have need.
Chapter One.
On the treatment of water which is contained in the head of infants.
Know that this illness often occurs in infants during infancy and when the nurse tightens the infant's head without care.
And sometimes it occurs for hidden reasons which are not known.
But I have only seen this illness in infants; and for all those I saw, death hastened, and so it seems to me that treatment for them is abandoned.
And I have seen an infant whose head was filled with water, and his head was always so enlarged that he could not hold it up by himself due to the size of his head, and moreover the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture is either accumulated between the skin and the bone, or is accumulated under the bone above the brain membrane.
For the operation on that, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with a cut in the width, and let the length of the cut be about two knots until the moisture flows, and this is the shape of the instrument:
And then if the moisture is more increased and the swelling is larger, then there are two incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone, a sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand on the inside parts and this is not hidden from you. It is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with three cuts in this manner:
and after the incision, extract all the moisture; and afterwards tighten the cuts with cloths and small feathers; and then apply wine and oil on top until the fifth day, and afterwards untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and tighten the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little moisture until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another manner of incision, where you consider where the magnitude of the swelling appears and the collection of water, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and then attend with the incision where the swelling and the repletion of water appear, and make the cut as best as possible for you.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood and the patient dies from that blood flow with evacuation of moisture.
Second Chapter.
When someone has catarrh or rheum in the eyes or chest, and it is chronic, and medicines do not cure it, then the last cure for this is the incision of those two arteries.
And so it is appropriate when you want to cut those that the patient shaves his head, and afterwards rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and afterwards the patient's neck should be tightened with a cloth; and afterwards consider where the vein pulses; and the locations of each above the places which are near the depressed ears, and they are hidden except in some men.
And afterwards mark the artery with ink, and afterwards cut each with the spatula from each sharp part with an incision reaching to the bone, and let this be across the width of the head.
And if you want, insert the spatula under the artery and cut it to the incisions in the upper parts.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers joined, because when a vein is cut the blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which leaps out in frequent spurts.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then it is appropriate that you measure from the ear the space of three fingers, and afterwards mark with ink, and cut to the bone.
And what is appropriate to let of blood is a quantity of six ounces, and very easily more or less is let from it, and all this is according to the quantity which appears from the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
And afterwards examine the wound, and then if any part of the membrane remains on the bone, then cut it so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And afterwards tighten the wound with pledgets and with linen bandages, and afterwards treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
On the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or acute catarrh, due to acrimonious humors and by quality in the muscles of the temples, or violent chronic headache and similar to those, and is treated with methods of medical cure and this does not help, then we have tried in these illnesses extraction of the arteries in the temples, or cauterization of them as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of those is that the patient shaves the hairs which are on the temples; and attend to the apparent artery in the temple for it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden except in few men due to the violence of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then let the patient's neck be tightened with a cloth; and afterwards rub the place with a rough cloth or steam the place with hot water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And afterwards you peel the skin with it with ease until you reach the artery; and afterwards throw in the hook and extract it to the upper parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are below it, from all parts.
But if the artery is thin, then wrap it with the end of the hook, and afterwards cut from it a part according to the quantity by which its ends are lengthened and are contracted, and there will not occur a flow of blood, because if they are not cut the blood will not rise at all.
And afterwards, evacuate the blood of wine ounce by ounce up to three.
And if the artery is large, then it is advisable that you tie it in two places with a strong double thread of silk or with long cords, and do not rush to putrefaction before the consolidation of the wound and the occurrence of blood flow.
And afterwards, cut away the excess which is between the two ties; and do this at that hour or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone with a knife-like cautery until the extremities are cut, and it will remain in place of that same work.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has fever or is of a heated complexion; for cautery is one of those things that help with ending humidities, and thus is better in assistance.
And it is advisable after the extraction of the arteries that you fill the place with old cotton, and powders are placed on it decently made, and afterwards it is cured with dry medicines making the flesh grow and with some until it is healed.
And if while you work another time blood flow occurs from the artery, then you hurry to restrict that blood, either with cautery, or by filling the place with cotton, and tighten your hand until the blood is restricted. And if none of those things is present to you, then place your finger on the place until the blood is congealed, and pour water of extreme coldness on the place until the acuteness rests, and let it be tightened as is appropriate.
And what is easier in extraction of artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and deposit each, very hot, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between their extremities is elongated, for they are not entirely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two knives be together. But if they are less sharp than two knives it is better, because if they are as sharp as two knives it approaches each cold, and they do not cut the flesh with speed.
Therefore when in those there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is held in them and the flesh is easily cut away.
And this work is better in all work and easier.
And let the space which is between two knives be the quantity of the thickness of the finger.
Chapter four.
On the treatment of acute and acidic tears of the eyes.
When there are acidic tears, and it is the course of those veins which are above the outer skull, and no cause of medical cure helps in this, and you see the patient's face already reddening and they feel in their forehead a movement as if it were the movement of ants, and their eyes are weakened, so moist that their eyelashes are stuck together, and the eyelids are heated by the acidity of the tears, the ancients had methods for the cure of these by this operation, and they named this cure with a sword.
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on their forehead, and afterwards incise three equally distant fissures on the forehead according to the length of the forehead. And let the length of the fissure be about two fingers: let one fissure be equally distant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the muscles which are in the tear duct, and the third on the other side.
And guard yourself from cutting the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jawbones. And let the space of each incision be the quantity of three fingers joined.
And have a piece of sponge prepared or very dry cloths with which you dry the blood.
And afterwards insert the spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the temple area to the second incision. Flay with it all the skin which is between two incisions with the membrane which is over the bone, and afterwards do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And afterwards withdraw this spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a knife, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp side towards the flesh attached to the skin, and the smooth side against the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle fissure, and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to what appears of the skin, and afterwards do this in the middle fissure until the other fissure.
And after an equal quantity of blood flows, press the places of the pieces of congealed blood.
And afterwards place in each fissure some old cotton, and place over those powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that no hot abscess occurs.
And on the third day, undo the bandage and administer a large infusion of hot or lukewarm water, and afterwards treat this with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5
On the treatment of tears due to catarrh and for eyes affected by internal parts of the head.
When someone has acute and continuous catarrh, and you see the patient's eyes small and weakened, and their gaze is already debilitated, and the eyelids are ulcerated and losing hair, and there is sharp pain deep in the head and consequent sneezing, then know by these symptoms that the matter and catarrh come only from many veins proceeding from the inner parts.
And the best cure for these is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make an incision in the middle of the forehead, a little in width (and begin with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be down to the bone; and keep your hand away from the muscles of the temples which move during chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or dry cloth and then fill between the two lips of the fissure with old cotton or linen cloths. And then tighten above with powders soaked in wine and oil so that no hot abscess occurs.
And when you untie this and see that the abscess has already diminished, then it is appropriate to rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it, and then treat it with a drying regimen which makes flesh grow, as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and make a plaster of them and apply it to these wounds for the growth of flesh.
Chapter 6
On the treatment of what falls into the ears.
Everything that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to stone, such as iron and glass, or it is from a plant such as chickpea or bean, or it is a fluid thing such as water and vinegar and things like them, or some animal.
And so when a stone falls into the ear or another similar thing of those things that do not stick or grow in the ear, and place the ear in the sun, and if you see the stone, then drip some violet oil or sesame oil into it, and then administer its extraction, and make them sneeze with condizi, and block the nostrils during the onset of sneezing. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and extend the ear to the upper parts, for many times it is expelled with these things.
And if it is not expelled, then administer its extraction with the instrument called subtle gesti epicicarios, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with gesti, good; and if not, then administer its extraction with a subtle hook of little duplication.
And so if it is not expelled by this, then make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole and close around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open except the tube. And then extract with your breath with strong suction, for many times with what we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small quantity of illuminated halka or prepared vecz with which birds are caught, and place it at the end of a ray, and then wrap cotton decently placed over it. And then insert that into the ear hole with ease, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And so if it is not expelled with all the things we have recounted, then you hasten to cut it before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the manner of cutting it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to their strength, and then make them sit between your hands and turn the ear to the upper parts, and make a small fissure at the root of the ear towards its fat in the well-pressed place of it. Let the fissure be of a lunar shape until it reaches the stone, and, once the stone is found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then close the fissure at that time with speed, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and are swollen, then administer the cure and extraction of it; but if it does not obey you to come out, then take a thin, subtle spatula in this form: and use it to cut away that grain which has fallen into the ear.
Do not do this when you think that it has already been moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it in many pieces, and then extract those with tweezers or with subtle gestures, or with incision as we have said, for the extraction of it is eased.
