But for water which has entered the ear, it is advisable that the patient use sneezing with caution at the beginning, and fill his ear with some old cotton, and lie on the ear in which the water is, and if by this it is expelled, it is good; and if not, then take many thin and light stones according to the length of the finger, and heat them in the fire a little, and let the patient insert one of these into the opening of his ear and jump on one foot on that side and strike with another stone on the stone which is inside the ear. Thus, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Thus, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And the water will be expelled thus: that you take paper or reed, and put one end of it in the ear and extend the other end with fire until most of it is burned.
And then put in another reed and do this so many times until all the water is expelled, and extract it with a tube as is said above about the extracting stone.
But in the extraction of a beast entering that ear, if it is small-bodied, like a flea and similar to it, treat it as we have said in the division; but if it is of large body which appears to the sense of sight, then administer its extraction with gestures and tweezers, and its matter is easier than all the other things that fall into the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm engendered in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment does not succeed, then it is advisable that you look at the ear in the sun.
And thus if something of the worms appears there, then extract it with gestures or with subtle tweezers, but if nothing appears, then take a tube narrow below and wide above, of which this is its form: And insert the subtle end into the ear according to the quantity which the patient can suffer; and then suck with it by strong suction, and do this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then you block and close the circuit of the tube with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already tested in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear be with this instrument of which this is its form: And make it of silver or copper, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and flat above.
And thus it should be with a pushing to what is in the concavity of the tube immediately where it will be necessary. And if you want, take the ray and wrap cotton decently at its end, and then throw oil into the concavity of the hearing, or juice of some herb, or what you want of these medicines in the tube — and that is in the ear —, and then insert the ray with cotton above and squeeze it with your hand with temperate compression of it until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the hearing and the patient feels the oil inside.
And let what is poured into the ear be already heated with fire a little. And guard yourself that the thing which you will distill in the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not suffer this.
Chapter 7
On treating blockage of the ears.
Sometimes some children are born from their mothers' wombs with unopened passages, and this also happens in some men, blockage in the ear canal due to injury or flesh growing there; and this blockage sometimes occurs deep in the ear hole which cannot be seen, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs in the deep part, which cannot be seen, is in the most difficult conditions to heal.
And in that which can be seen, it is appropriate that you place the patient's ear in the sun and look into it. And then if you see an apparent blockage, open it with a thin spatula of which this is the shape:
Let its end be where the width is small, of which one part should be sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be smooth on both sides so that it does not harm the ear.
And then if the blockage is due to flesh growing in the ear or in its canal, then take it with thin hooks and cut it with great care until all the flesh is removed.
And if the blockage is deep in the ear, then take a thin smooth probe and heat it slightly with fire, and afterwards put it in the ear canal.
And then if you have felt the blockage with that which the patient finds easily, due to difficulty in hearing, press on it gently; and be careful not to wound the nerves of the ear.
And afterwards place in the ear a linen cloth according to the width of the hole which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for some days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take some lint and soak it in rose water and sprinkle crushed tallow on it and use it; and if you see the ear is already swollen, then it is appropriate that you remove the lint and replace it with other lint which you will wrap in a cerote made with rose oil, until the hot swelling subsides; and afterwards treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which stops the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8
On the treatment of growths which occur on the eyelids.
Growths which occur on the eyelids of the eyes are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
So it is appropriate that you hold these with tweezers or a hook, and cut them from their roots with a spatula, and afterwards place crushed tallow on the areas.
But if pain with bleeding comes, then you cauterize them with a subtle lenticular cautery.
And the best cauterization is after the incisions and many times we return when something remains of its roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9
Treatment of swellings that come to the eyelids of the eyes.
Many times there occurs in the eyelids of the eyes something which is similar to a grain in its strength and hardness, and for this reason it is called a grain.
And it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the lower and upper eyelid.
So the operation on them is that you consider then if the grain is proceeding in it in what appears of the eyelid and is moved to every part; and its cause is light.
So make an incision over it along its width, and afterwards scrape it from every part gently until it comes out, and afterwards suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
And if the wound is large, then you close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it because the ointment restores it and helps it heal.
And if the swelling is declining towards the inner parts of the eyelid towards the inner surface, then turn the eyelid inside out and suspend the swelling with a hook, unless you need to make an incision, and press it from all sides, and if the eyelid is elongated by the incision, this does not harm in any way.
And after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with that which heals until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the treatment of axcirnah in the upper eyelid of the eye.
Axcirnath is a growth which is generated in the layers of the upper eyelid, and mostly happens to children, weighing down their eyes and causing them to have cataracts in the morning; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears rush to them.
And for this reason you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and their lower eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, which are already covered with swelling, and the presence of accernac is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel accernac up to them.
And one way of operation is that the patient places his head in your lap, and then places a linen cloth and makes it warm, and makes a circle of it according to the quantity which contains arthernach from all parts, and then places it over it and presses with your fingers all around so that arcernac is gathered in the middle of the circle, and then splits in the middle of that moisture with a cutting spatula with a fissure along the width, and the fissure should not be larger than the fissure made in phlebotomy.
But in depth, it is necessary that you split the entire skin until you reach arcernach, because in most dispositions arcernach proceeds through the fissure in the form of white fat.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped between your fingers to the outer parts and revolve between the right and left hand until it has dispersed.
And be careful not to add to the fissure so that with the spatula you do not reach the eye and damage it.
But if arternach does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, but you add a little to the fissure with ease until arternach proceeds; and then extract it as we have said; and then submerge linen cloths in water and vinegar and place them over the area and tighten them with powders.
And there are some who grind salt and place it in the concavity of the fissure so that what remains of that moisture [is removed].
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
On the ways of tactimi of the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes hairs are born added below the natural hairs and are old, then those harm the eye and cause kinds of diseases such as constant tears and softening of eyelids and film until it is a cause of destruction of the eye called tactinir of the eye, and it is cured in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with sharp medicine as said before in the chapter on cautery; or tactini is done by incision and suture; or by reeds as I will say.
And so it is fitting that you place the patient's head in your lap; then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, it is good; and if not, insert a needle into it in which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with thread above, and this should be — near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow — near the hairs, and pull the thread to the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it with the ray.
And then make an incision on the inner part of the lower eyelid near the hairs, cutting with a spatula from the larger tear duct to the smaller one; and then extract the bile and place small cotton or cloth pads under the eyelid, and then mark above the eyelid with ink, and let it be in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be according to the amount of elevation of the eyelid, for this varies among men, as there are those who need a large amount to be cut from the eyelid according to how much the eyelid is softened.
And then cut with the spatula along the two lines which you have marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct. And let your incision be close to the natural hairs, about the thickness of a grain of rye.
And then put the hook in one of the two corners of the skin, and then flay it all, and then join the two lips of the cut with thin wool thread or gut, and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what is left of the threads over the two eyebrows with some sticky substances, if you want to do this, and if not, don't worry about it.
And then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then treat it.
And if you want, leave the wound without suture, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, for the eyelid is raised towards the sealing of the wound and the aggregation of light; but suture is better. And so this is what the ancients said about eyelid surgery.
But in any case, be attentive to the patient, and it is a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And there is another way in eyelid surgery, and it is that you mark on the eyelid a shape like a myrtle leaf, and then lift the eyelid with three hooks separated and brought together in this form:
But if it is not possible to hold the hooks, and this is not equal, then take a needle with thread and put it in the middle of the shape, and insert the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and join the threads between your fingers equally, and then with these lift your hand with equal elevation, lift all the skin on which is marked as we have recounted.
And then join the two lips of the wound with suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if swelling occurs near your incision or your cut, calm that swelling with wax plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are turned inward, then it is necessary that you use on it what we have said about incision; but suture in each case is more skillful.
Eyelid surgery with reeds is in this form: and it is that you turn the eyelid and cut the shape that is inside as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two reeds or two thin splints whose length is according to the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the spatula. And you have already cut in the extremities of each part, where you hold the threads.
Then gather each with subtlety over what is excess of the eyelid of the eye, and constrict the reeds of each part with strong tightening, and leave it for some days, for the skin by the tightening will die and will be denied and will rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then cut it with scissors, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Thus when it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two reeds which you will make according to this form: and it should have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12
On removing sharp hairs from the eye with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and sharp hairs in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle and thread it with fine silk thread, and then you join the ends and tie each one in a very subtle manner. And the length of the threads should be about one palm.
And then you compose another fine thread below it shortly, and tie the ends of it if you want.
And then have the patient rest their head on your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is clear, because it is a subtle and small operation.
And then insert the needle at the root of the gathered hair which needs to be removed, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair up and insert it into the alixati (possibly a type of tool or treatment), and let there be one or two or three hairs and no more.
And then pull your hand with it until the hair is expelled with the alixolati in the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if the alixalati is pulled and the hair is not expelled, you will pull the alixati to the lower parts with the thread which you have composed with it, until the alixalati is expelled from the upper hole.
And examine it with the vision of the eye, because then you will expel the alixati and the thread, and rub each and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you add a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is raised with that, or you leave it until it is lengthened after some time, and then you remove it.
Chapter 13
On the treatment of ehacratir (possibly a condition) which comes in the upper eyelid.
When ehacratir comes to the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound or incision or cauterization, and it is called leporine, and this ahercrati is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the healing of a wound or incisions or cauterization, or by similar poorly treated conditions.
But the manner of the operation on it is that you split that healed area and separate its edges, and place between them a strip of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not appropriate that you use for its treatment things which dry out, constrict; because if you do this, the alixacratir returns worse than it ever was before.
Use on it things that soften such as fenugreek, and soak with water in which marshmallow and flaxseed are cooked, or diachylon ointment, which is liquefied with some of the oils, and submerge the strip in it, and treat with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you strive with all skill that it not be healed according to the form it was at the beginning.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but some rectification of ehalixacrati so that it does not return to the form of the place as it was before.
Chapter 14.
On the treatment of ehacrati which is made in the lower eyelid.
This alxacrati which is made in the lower eyelid, which is truly called alxacrati; and some of it is natural, other accidental.
But accidental is made by wound or by cut or by cautery or by other similar things.
And the way of the operation is that you take a needle in which there is a double thread, and stick it in the flesh, and make it pass from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two extremities of the flesh.
And then extend the flesh to the upper parts with the needle and sew it with the needle, and cut with a wide spatula. And so if the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good; and if not, take the ray and place it in the split figure, and with that turn the eyelid, and make two fissures in the side of the lower eyelid.
And let the extremities of the two fissures be from two angles of the incision, which you have made until they meet, and let an angle be made from them until when they come together a figure is made of them.
And this figure is a Greek letter: the lambda.
And then remove that flesh by the quantity that is the sharp side below it, that which follows the eyelid.
And then join the separated parts and sew them with the wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of softening medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if alxacrati occurs by fissure or suture or cautery, then it is appropriate that you split with a flat fissure over the hairs of the eyelid as previously described. And then separate with the poultice between two lips as we have said.
In the summary of the discourse on the treatment of alxacrati when it is above or below, that runs in the operation, according to how it is prepared from the form of alxacrati, and that is in the form of many diversities.
And the wise craftsman always prepares a device in each way which is possible to him until he makes the figure return to the natural form, or close to it, and that patient according to what is convenient to him of the operation and of the instruments in the major dispositions.
Chapter 15.
On the adherence of the eyelid with the conjunctiva or with the cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many men so that the upper eyelid is solidified with the white of the eye, and is prohibited from the ease of movement and its natural works, then it is appropriate that you introduce the extremity of the ray under the eyelid, and raise it up, or extend it with the hook.
And then you cut the adherence with a subtle spatula, which should not be with sharpness of incisions, rather slightly blunt so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and move the spatula so that it does not harm the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision be almost skinning that adherence with ease until the eyelid is returned according to its natural form and all the adherence is healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash; or distill in it zunia dissolved in water.
And then separate between the eyelid and the eye with a linen poultice, and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye, and after the third day use consolidating things until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
On the incision of the nail and the eminence of flesh from the tear ducts.
Know that the nail is of two types, for it is either nervous, and is similar to a hard, subtle ciphac; or it is non-nervous, similar to congealed white humidity, which when iron touches it, or is forced to take it with a hook, is cut off and the hook is not fixed in it.
And both species do not start except from the major tear duct until it gradually covers the sight and prohibits the light and movement of the eye.
But the manner of working in the incision of it is that the patient rests his head on your lap, and then opens his eyes, and you raise the eyelid with your hand, and then collect the nail with a hook of little duplication and extend it to the upper parts. And then take a needle and insert in it a horse hair or ox or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or double the end of the needle little by little and fix it in the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the side of the nail which follows the pupil. And thus you follow it with the hair to the back of it.
And then you cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a subtle spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that by cutting it, a continuous flow of tears does not occur.
And sometimes the nail with the sole hook extended or with thread, as we have said, and then be scraped with a smooth spatula of which this is its form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the corneal tunic and that rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
After the incision, throw in the eye a little crushed salt or cief made of bloodletting, and leave it until the next day; and then treat it with what is suitable until it is healed.
And if the nail is not nervous and you cannot insert the needle, nor is the hook fixed in it, then many times this species of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the division.
And if you want to cut it, then you open the patient's eye and take a subtle smooth spatula of weak sharpness according to this form: with subtlety. And if you see that it is rarefied and has fled and takes root in it, then distill from your mouth into the eye cief made of bloodletting or with red cief or with crushed salt, and close the eye until the next day.
And then repeat the operation on it until all of it is removed, unless there is a hot abscess in the eye, for then you leave it, and treat the abscess at the beginning until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with reason until it is healed.
The eminence of the tear duct flesh, if it harms the eye greatly, should be suspended with a hook; and cut off a part of it, and do not finish the incision so that a flow of tears does not occur.
And then distill red cief and bloodletting into the tear duct until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
On the incision of alguazardenic and of that which is born of flesh joined in the eye.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is born, and it is placed over until it covers the pupil, or approaches or presses on the eyelids and strongly compresses the eyelids to the outer parts and resembles a pomegranate flower.
But the operation on it is that the patient rests his head in your lap; and then open his eyes and collect all that flesh with hooks suitable for this, and hold it with forceps or tweezers; and then cut the flesh at the beginning until all is finished with the scalpel, and be careful of the eye that you do not damage it during your operation.
And let your incision with which you cut those be either with a spatula which we have described in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which the growth is collected according to the form that will come after this chapter.
Therefore when your incision is complete and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with ground salt, or drip red powder and similar corrosive medicines into it, and place cotton with egg white on the outside of the eye until it is secured from hot inflammation.
And if any cause of alguardenici remains and hot blood dominates, and you fear hot inflammation, then leave the eye and cure that inflammation with what reduces that inflammation, and then repeat the operation on it until it is healed, and similarly do for the joined flesh which occurs in the eye of this type.
But however, it is fitting that you avoid all curing of the weak eye by natural or accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member is able to endure that operation.
Chapter 18.
On the collection of sabel in the eye.
Sabel are red veins which are woven over the eye and prohibit its operation of sight, and weaken the eye with length of days.
Therefore it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is sabel is strong and in it there is no other malady than sabel, then you collect that sabel.
And it is that you command the patient to rest his head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with one hook or with two according to your judgment; and let that hook be of subtle duplication according to this form:
or they are two hooks gradually in one body according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with subtle forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood hour after hour, until you see those veins to be removed from the eye and are rolled back.
And take care that you do not damage the eye with the extremities of the spatula; and let your operation be at midday before the sun, and be very firm in your operation that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have completed, then you drip green or red cief into the eye so that with its sharpness it corrodes what remains of the sabel.
But if that is not possible for you to collect all in that hour, then you plaster all with that which reduces its pain, and leave it for some days until its pain is settled and it is secured from hot inflammation.
And then repeat on it in the same manner until it is healed.
And this is the form of the scissors:
Chapter 19.
On the reduction of arizati to the nose.
When you cure arizati with cautery or with sharp burning medicine as described in the preceding account of it and it is not healed, then there is no other solution but to cut into the abscess towards its maturity, and extract all the humidity which is in it and the pus until the bone is exposed.
And then when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is called a rough head, and is made of Indian iron, and the head is round like asir which is made punctated by punctation of a file or aliskalfati by subtle punctation (as shown in the following form).
And place that on the corrupted part of the bone, and then revolve it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and afterwards restore the place with drying and astringent substances.
Then if the place is consolidated and good flesh has grown in it and the flow of pus is stopped for 40 days, and it is not renewed and does not form an abscess and no accident occurs in it, then you know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is no solution but to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, in this manner: that you uncover the second bone with iron or with sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a drill of this form:
Let the extremity of the iron be triangular, and its shaft be thin and tapered to the extremity as you see here.
And then place that on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal; and move your hand a little from the eye, and then revolve your hand with the drill until it penetrates all the bone, and the patient feels this because he finds wind coming out when he holds his hand.
Then restore the place with astringent drying medicines as I have taught you.
Then when flesh grows and the place is hardened, then the matter which was flowing to the outer parts returns to the nose, so this is easier for the patient.
Chapter 20.
On the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety and no harm or diminution occurs in the vision, then it is appropriate that you relax the patient, and then that you follow him, and then that you place cups on the neck without scarification and suck with good suction; and then place on the eye a liniment which is made of acacia and aloe and incense and sarcocolla, and then tighten the plaster on the eye with many cloths.
And the manner of the cloths is that you take many clean linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded in four pleats; and make many of these.
And then place them one on top of the other according to the quantity which the eye needs. And then tighten over them with a hard cloth with strong constriction; and repeat the cups with suction without scarification; and then remove the cups and leave the eye tightened for the day and night.
But if meanwhile the binding is softened, then it should be very tightly bound.
And then remove the binding after the day and night as we have said.
And if you see the eye has already been reduced, it is good, and if not, then repeat the plaster with the cloths and the constriction and the cups until it is healed.
Chapter 21
On the incision of the protrusion of the uveal tunic.
When a disruption occurs in the uveal tunic, and afterwards it protrudes and comes out of the eyelids like a grape, and the shape of the eye is distorted because of this, and you want to cut it, then the operation on it is as we have described, that you insert the needle at the root of the uvea from the lower part to the upper part, and then put another needle with a double thread from the lacrimal part, and make it penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then cut the place of the double thread and lift with it the part of the uvea in the circle of the needle, and tighten it a lot.
And then extract the needle and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye and leave it until the thread falls off with the uvea.
And then treat the eye with something that comforts it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all parts with a circle of cloth until the eye gathers a large protrusion, and then let it be perforated with a thin spatula that comes to the depth of the eye, and let the albuminous fluid run, and the eye descends and enters immediately.
And then tighten until it is healed.
Chapter 22
On the treatment of alconati.
This disease that is called alconati is nothing but pus which is gathered in the eye resembling descending water and is not that.
But the manner of working on it is that you make the patient sit on the prepared and upright chair, and then take his head in your hands from both sides and move it until you see the pus reach the lower parts, and then let it be dried and not descend, and let the light be removed, and the patient see things as he saw.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water.
But if the descent is not prepared for the pus with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a thin spatula and split with it over the sifting membrane a little towards the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the crown (up to the empty place), until the pus is expelled.
Therefore when it is expelled, then you instill in the eye hot water in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then let it be treated with other things until it is healed.
Chapter 22
On the treatment of water descending from the eye.
We have already spoken of the types of water in the division and which is suitable to perforate with a declaring speech, then that according to health.
Then it is appropriate that you make the patient sit between your hands on a square and bind his healthy eye, very tightly.
And then place near the eyelid of his eye your left hand, if the eye in which the water is is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take the needle, if the eye is left; or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
And then place the tip of the needle near the crown through the thickness of the ray in the white of the eye from the part of the lesser lacrimal, and then push the needle with strength and turn your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the white of the eye and feel that the needle has already reached an empty cause.
And it is appropriate that the measure of the path of the needle to the depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, and is the crown of the eye; for you will see with that pupil with the vision of the eye through the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the needle up to the place where the water is, and then you draw it down once after another, and therefore if the water descends immediately, then you see the patient that on which he opens his sight immediately, and the needle in his eye.
And then rest a little; when the water has descended, then you remove it again before the almond is thrown out.
And when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and rotate it with your hand little by little, and then dissolve in water a little clear salt of gem and with that wash the eye inside; and then put outside the eye cotton or tow or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white and bind with that the healthy eye.
But we and those of our time put over it ground cumin with egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you when entering the eye due to its hardness — because there are some men whose eye is very hard —, then it is necessary that you take the spatula which is called alberid, of which this is the form:
And then perforate with it that conjunction, and do not push your hand into the hole, and it is only so that you make a subtle way for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, a decent sleeping place should be prepared for the patient in which he sleeps on his back in a dark house, and he should be prohibited from all movements.
And put in his nature that by which his nature may be soothed, and do not move his head to the right or left side at all.
And let the bandage be according to its disposition until the third day.
And then untie it in that dark house and test the weak sight, and make him see some things, and then return the bandage until the 7th day.
And it is not appropriate that you do this at the time of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Rather, it is appropriate that you alienate this so that the water can lightly rise through great and vehement regard.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you untie the eye before the 7th day, and rectify it with that which reduces the abscess.
Then you free his sight and put over his face a veil by which his vision may be guided below for some days, he being in the dark house.
And then let him be gradually removed from that house and converse in his affairs.
And know that in alchatea the disciple is not excused from vision sometimes, and then you be bold in the operation.
And it has already come to us from some of those who are in Artach, and some said that it was done in Ayrac magdaam perforated with which the water is sucked.
But however I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read this in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the species of magdaan so that he who ignores them may be over them:
And let them be made of copper or iron properly, and let their extremities be with this triangular subtlety, the sharp extremity as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
When in the nose are born diverse flesh joined of which there is something which is assembled like a scorpion of many feet, or like an octopus fish; and this disease is called polypus because it is in the likeness of that fish represented. And of it is cancerous flesh, flat, of dark color; and of it is that it is lion flesh not of dark color.
So in that which is in these lion flesh not fraudulent nor cancerous, it is appropriate that you make the patient sit between your hands and placed in the sun, and open his nostrils, and prick your nail into those flesh. And then extract that to the outside parts, and then cut what you have grasped of those with a sharp, subtle spatula from one part until you know that all the flesh has already been removed; that if any of it remains of which the removal is possible, then you scrape that with one of the subtle instruments with ease until none of it remains. But if it dominates with blood or hot apostema, then you apply to it what is appropriate; or if it is of malignant apostemas, then you cook and cauterize it until the blood is removed and all the cavities are eliminated.
And afterwards, pour into the nose after the incision vinegar and water or wine. But if the nose is open and humidity proceeds from it to the palate, then know that it is already healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside it flesh has grown in the highest bones of the nostrils to which the removal has not reached.
Therefore then it is appropriate that you introduce a thread to which there is some thickness, and tie many knots in it, and place between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and instruct the patient and insert one end of the thread into the nostril with the ray or with another thing which is possible for him.
And after that has been done, let him inhale his odor until it reaches the channels of the nostrils, and comes out above his throat, for many times children in schools do something similar to this action, and it is an easy thing for one who wants to do it.
And afterwards place the two ends of the thread, one end that comes out above the nose and the other that remains in the nose, and afterwards administer sawdust of flesh with the knots that are in the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is already with the knots of the thread.
And afterwards extract the thread, and place in the nose after the abstraction of blood a plug, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
And afterwards at the end of the matter, place in the nose a lead cannula for some days until it is healed.
But if it needs a cure that dries out, use that.
And this is the form of the instrument for making head purgatives with which oils and medicines are distilled in the nose:
And make this of silver or brass, which is similar to a small open lamp, just like a small basin, and the passage similarly.
And if you want, make a closed cannula just like a cane, and the cannula of the instrument for making head purgatives and let it be flat, uncovered; to which there is a handle in the back of it, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it or what you want of the juices and of the suckable things.
Chapter 25.
Of moles born in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a mole is born in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in some days until its appearance is very ugly.
And for this it is appropriate that you cut it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. And afterwards place on the location either cautery or burning medicine which stays in place of it.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is stony, hard, of dark color and of little feeling, then do not touch it with iron because it is a cancerous apostema; for many times to one who cuts this apostema it happens that through it great malice comes upon the one who had it.
And if the apostema is of soft touch and is not of dark color, and you see that incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and cut it without fear, and cure the location with that which dries out and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
On suturing the nose, lips, and ear when continuity is disrupted by wound or similar.
Know that when a break in continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, it contributes little to that operation except in some men.
Therefore, when something like this happens to someone, consider if it's a fresh wound with its blood, and then join the two edges of the wound with sutures, and afterwards treat it until it heals.
And if the edges of the break in continuity are already separated, and each has become healed, then you should scrape each part of its visible skin until blood comes out. And afterwards join the two edges with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle dragon's blood and ground incense over them, and place a piece of diaphoenicon plaster, or some other consolidative plaster, over the powder, and leave it bound for two or three days, and afterwards unbind it and change the plaster and leave it until the threads fall out by themselves, and afterwards treat with ointment until it heals.
But the manner of suturing is that you close the break in continuity either with needles as we have described in the suturing of the belly, or close the break in continuity with thread as you have been taught.
Chapter 27.
On extraction of those things that come in the two lips in the inner parts.
Many times it happens to many men in the inner parts of their lips some hard swellings similar to vetch seeds, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore you should turn the lips and cut each of those hard spots from all sides, and after the excision fill the place with ground fat until the blood is stopped, and afterwards wash it with vinegar and treat the place with something astringent, until it heals.
Chapter 28.
On incision of flesh added to the gums.
Many times flesh grows on the gum which the ancients called albulus, so you should suspend it with a hook or hold it with forceps and cut it towards its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
And afterwards place ground fat or other astringent or drying powders on the spot.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it a second time, and cauterize it, for after cauterization it will not return.
Chapter 29.
On scraping teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth of the inner and outer parts and between the gums rough, ugly scales are gathered, and sometimes they are blackened and become yellow or green until corruption comes to the gum because of this and the teeth become ugly.
And so you should have the patient sit between your hands and place his head in your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those scales and things like sand appear until none of it remains, and do this for the blackness and the yellowness and the others until they are cleaned.
So if what is on them from the beginning of the scraping is removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the scraping on them the next day and the second and the third until it comes to the final state that you want.
And know that teeth need many scrapers of diverse forms and many shapes according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the scraper with which the tooth is scraped from the inner part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of scrapers should be prepared by you which you will see soon:
Chapter 30
On the extraction of teeth.
It is advisable that you treat the tooth for its pain with all skill and delay any extraction of it, for there is nothing that can replace it once it is extracted, as it is a sublime substance until there is justification for its extraction.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which is the painful tooth, for many times the pain deceives the patient and they think it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and afterwards the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the work of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which is the painful tooth, and you know it well, then you should scarify around the tooth with a spatula which has some virtue, until it is separated from the gum on all sides, and afterwards move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at the beginning little by little until you loosen it.
And afterwards, place strong, large forceps on it for a good grip or strong hold, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold it well and it doesn't move, and then pull the tooth straight out in a way that you don't break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And if it is not thrown out, then take one of those instruments, and place it beneath it on all sides with ease and attempt its removal as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is hollow or decayed, then you should fill that hole with cloth and tighten it with the end of a fine rod so that it is not broken at the time of your constriction on it with forceps.
And you should work carefully with scarification around all parts of the gum and be very careful with your strength that you don't break it and leave a part of it, and return to the patient due to the harm that may be greater than their first pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity in their extractions, without administering what we have recounted; for many times they bring great illnesses upon men which are more difficult to be broken, and all their roots remain, or some of them, or that you extract it with some bones of the jaw as we have often testified.
And after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with hot wine or vinegar with salt.
And if bleeding occurs because of this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of zegi and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that zegi doesn't help, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and fine pliers with which the tooth is moved at the beginning should be of long extremity, with a short and thick handle, so that they are not bent when you must work with them.
And they should be as you see with a thick handle so that when you tighten with them they are not bent, with short extremities.
And they should be made of Indian iron, decently made with straight extremities, and at their extremities should be teeth of which some enter into the others and tighten by strong decent constriction, and sometimes their extremities should be in the shape of the beak of a bird which is called ciconia, that is stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
On the removal of teeth and extraction of bones from broken jaws.
When you must remove a tooth which is broken and some part remains, then you should place cotton with butter on the spot for a day or two until the area is softened; and afterwards introduce pliers or forceps whose tips resemble the beak of a bird called stork or crane, the shape of the forceps:
Their tips should be made inside like a file or rasp.
And if it doesn't come out with these forceps or pliers, then you should dig around the root and uncover all the flesh and introduce the instrument called atali, which is a small lever whose shape is this:
And it should have a short, slightly thick end, and not be beveled so that it doesn't break.
So if by this the root is extracted, good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are the forms: the shape of the first instrument is of triangular tip in which there is some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is that it should be of triangular and more subtle tip.
And I have already been helped with this instrument having two prongs of which this is the form after the following: and with other instruments which preceded in scraping of teeth; for help is made with this instrument which resembles a large hook, and this is the shape:
of the triangular tip curved or short, which should have some thickness, and not be beveled so that it doesn't break.
And furthermore know that there are many dental instruments and similarly other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artisan in the art finds for himself according to what the operation and diverse diseases signify to him; for there are diseases for which the ancients did not speak of nor set a proper instrument, due to the forms of diverse species.
But if the jawbone or other bones of the mouth are broken, or if they rot, then let it be treated carefully in its place with what is convenient of the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of tooth roots, and help yourself with a tool of which this is the shape:
And let there be some thickness in it little by little, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it doesn't escape or flee until the bone is completely removed; and afterwards restore the place with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape it of that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of teeth protruding above the others.
When the teeth are protruding not according to their natural course, their shape is ugly, and especially when it happens to women, and especially to venerable maidens; so it is fitting that you consider: if a tooth is born next to another tooth, and it is not possible to close or align it, then uproot it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you cut it with this instrument of which this is the shape; and it is similar to a pig's snout, and should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And let your cutting with which you cut it be over many days due to the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other tooth is disturbed.
And if it is a protruding tooth exposed to filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of which this is the shape:
And let it all be of Indian iron and the handle likewise; and let it be of many small points just like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth should be filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is disturbed and falls. And afterwards smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some razors.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and hurts the tongue, then it is fitting that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and evened out, and it is smoothed so that the tongue is not hurt nor the speech corrupted.
Chapter 33.
On restoring loose teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth become loose due to impact or falling, and the patient cannot bite on what they eat, and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and your treatment has not worked, then so that they don't fall out, it is ingenious to fasten them with gold wire and not silver, because silver turns green and then rots after some days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness that it can enter between the teeth; and the form of the repair is that you take the wire and take double of it between two healthy teeth, and then increase with two twists of the wire between two loose ones, whether one or many, until the thread reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving until the part where you began, and tighten your hand with ease and wisdom until it is not loose.
And let your tightening with which you tighten the wire be towards the roots of the teeth so that it doesn't escape.
And then cut the excess ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and gather them in each place with gestures, and turn them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth, so that they don't injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightened as it remains.
But if it is untied or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in such a manner for all its time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the repair of two healthy teeth and two loose ones, as you see these tied:
And when it happens that one tooth or two return after the falling of each in their places, and they are regrown as we have recounted, and that they remain.
And only a good and wise craftsman does this.
And sometimes something is adapted from cow bones, and is made from them in the form of a tooth, and is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and is tightened as we have said, and remains and serves with that for a long time.
Chapter 34.
On cutting the ligament which comes in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament which comes under the tongue sometimes is natural with which a man is born, or accidental and happens due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the mouth of the patient, their head being in your lap; and raise their tongue, and then cut that nervous ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its retention.
But if in it there is any hardness or knot, and this has been due to the healing of a wound, then you throw a hook and cut it across its width until you have cut the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And take care that a fissure is not made in the depth of the flesh and that you don't cut an artery there and cause a flow of blood.
And afterwards let the trace of the incision be washed with rosewater or with vinegar or with cold water; and then place under the tongue a wick; that is, a bandage which the patient should hold under the tongue each night so that it doesn't heal again.
But if a flow of blood occurs, place crushed vitriol on the place; but if the blood wins, then cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And after that, treat with other things until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula under the tongue.
Sometimes a swelling occurs under the tongue resembling a small growth, preventing the tongue from its natural operation; and it can quickly grow until it fills the mouth.
And the procedure for it is to open the mouth of the patient sitting in front of the sun and examine the swelling.
And if you see it is dark or black in color, hard, which the patient cannot feel, then do not touch it because it is cancer.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then you put the hook in it, and cut it with a fine spatula, and cut it from all sides; and if blood comes during your operation, then you place crushed vitriol on it until the blood is stopped.
And then return to your work until you have removed it completely, until it is washed with vinegar and salt; and treat it with the other cure for this until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
On the extraction of swellings from the two glands and of that which is born in the throat and of other swellings.
When they occur in the throat, these are glands which are like cotton, which happen outside and are called two ears, which when you treat them with what we have said in the division and it is not healed, then you consider if the swelling is hard, of dark color, with little feeling, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron because of the blood flow; instead leave it until it is mature, and then either you will pierce it, or it will break on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is thin, then it is appropriate to be cut.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, if the swelling has already rested from its heat with complete rest or if it has diminished with some reduction; and then make the patient sit in front of the sun, and their head should be in your lap, and open their mouth, and the servant taking between his hands should press their tongue down with a very noble instrument of which this is the form:
And it should be made of silver or brass, and be thin like a knife.
So when you press the tongue with it and the swelling is shown to you, and your gaze falls upon it, then take a hook and fix it in the almond, and pull it to the outside parts as much as you can. Besides this, pull something from the membranes, and then cut it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Besides this, pull something from the membranes, and then cut it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it is like forceps, but its extremities are curved, so that the orifice of each of them is in the direction of the other, very sharp, which should be of blue-dyed iron.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then cut it with a spatula of which this is its form: And it should be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same manner of incision, and after the incision the patient should gargle with cold water
But if blood flow occurs, gargle water of decoction of pomegranate peel or myrtle leaf and similar to that of astringent things until the blood flow is restricted, and then cure it until it is healed.
And sometimes other swellings are born in the throat besides those two glands, and cut those according to what I have said in the cutting of two tonsils equally.
And I have already treated a woman for a swelling which was born in her throat, which swelling tended to a dark color, of little feeling, which had already closed her throat, and the woman was breathing with a narrow passage, and it had already prohibited her from eating and drinking water, and she had already come to death if it had remained for one more day or two.
And the swelling had already grown so much that it had put two branches until they came out through two holes.
Therefore I hurried carefully and stuck in one of their nails, and pulled it, and a large portion was pulled from it, and then I cut that from where I understood the other hole, and then I opened its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and then I stuck the nail in that abscess.
And then I cut off a large part of it, and only a little blood ran from it. Therefore the throat of that woman was freed, and she immediately began to drink water, and then took food, so I did not cease to cut that abscess many times for a long time, and an abscess succeeded in place of that one, which I cut until it was prolonged.
Therefore I ingeniously had and cauterized its throat, so it stopped from addition; and then I walked around and did not know what God did with that after me.
Chapter 37.
On cutting the uvula when it is swollen.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is swollen and long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a bubble.
But when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and your cure does not work, and likewise you already see that the hot swelling has already been removed from it and it is subtle, then it is appropriate that you cut that which is gathered round; and if it has that length, and is bloody, of dark color or black, and has no feeling, then it is appropriate that you attempt to cut it, because in that there is fear for the patient.
And when you see that according to the manner that I have said of its whiteness and length, that you make the patient sit and press his tongue with an instrument whose form proceeded and then stick the nail in the uvula, and pull it down and cut it with one of two instruments which are mentioned in the cutting of two tonsils.
And it is appropriate that you do not cut it except what is added above the natural term without addition, because if you cut more of it, you will harm his speech.
And after the cutting, use what we have recounted in the cutting of two tonsils, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the patient was frightened by cutting it, it is appropriate to use ingenuity in cauterizing it without fear, and the manner of cautery in it is only with sharp water, and it is that you place the head of the patient in your lap, and then press his tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix quicklime with it, and place it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the concavity of the instrument of which this is the form:
Let the extremity of it be a concavity in which the medicine is placed just as the concavity of a ray spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and the patient lies on his side so that saliva or mucus runs from his mouth through the intrinsic medicine, so that nothing of it descends on his throat and harms it.
And then hold your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for the space of half an hour until you see it become blackened and sedated by the biting of the medicine.
And if you want, take cotton and wrap it on the extremity of the ray, and submerge the cotton in the medicine, and introduce the ray with the cotton in a cannula from above until the cotton adheres in that cannula, and do this so many times until you achieve what you want of the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it because it will dry and fall after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and after the cauterization wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter, and clean with it what is around it of the medicine, and let it be washed with cold water, and let it be cured outside with embrocation, and inside with gargles until it is healed.
And this kind of cure is safer than cutting and further from fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with something more subtle than cautery and incision, for it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calament, hyssop, origanum, rue, Arabic sticados, chamomile, bruised and similar to those herbs, and add all these things, or some of them, in a pot, and let them be submerged in vinegar, and boil; and let the pot be well sealed with proper luting, and let there be in the middle of the pot a hole, over which is placed a concave instrument of copper in this manner; let it be made of silver or copper.
And let the extremity be introduced into the mouth of the patient until the vapor rises to the uvula over the cannula, until the uvula is very darkened.
And afterwards repeat over it many times until it is dry, and be careful not to do this cure at the beginning of the appearance of abscesses, for it would add too much to the abscess, and it is not appropriate for you to do this, except towards the declaration of the abscess of heat.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a cane and compose at its extremity an eggshell, so that the mouth of the patient is not burned, for the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this cure is good with health.
Chapter 38.
Of extraction of thorns and of that which adheres in the throat and of other things.
Many times there adheres in the throat either a fish bone or some other thing.
Then it is appropriate that you extract that which of those is apparent, upon which falls the sight, after you press the tongue with an instrument before the sun, so that to you is declared that which is in the throat.
And that which does not appear and is hidden to you in the throat, it is appropriate that you make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very easily the thing attached to the throat will be thrown out by vomit; or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or take a piece of dry sea sponge, and tie it strongly with thread, and afterwards let the patient swallow it, for when it reaches the place where the thorns are, it is extracted, then they are thrown out. And also take an eel and tie it well with thread, and let it be put inside and suddenly extracted; or take a leek and suddenly put it in and suddenly take it out.
But if they are not thrown out with these things which we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form:
And let it be a little thicker than a ray, and at its extremity let there be a concavity which you introduce into his throat with ease.
And let him have his head elevated upwards, and be careful of touching his throat bone, so that it reaches the thing and with it that pushes the bone or the thorn, or that the physician does it with his hand.
But however that the patient introduces it is better, for his knowledge of the place of the attached thing and pushes downwards, and pulls his hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the quantity which is prepared for him until it is thrown out.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is treated with what we have said in the division of the cure with medicines and it doesn't work, then examine the patient's throat in the sunlight, after you have compressed their tongue with the instrument we have described to you.
Then if you see the leech, extract it with small tweezers or with subtle gestures decently.
And if it's not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat, and after that place a hot iron in the cavity of the tube, and do this many times (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat) and the patient should abstain from water all day.
And afterwards, take a glass full of cold water, and open their mouth in it, and wash the mouth with it; and don't let them swallow any of it and move the water from one hour to the next with their hand, because the leech as soon as it feels the water falls.
But if it is not expelled with what we have described, then fumigate the patient with bugs or with asafoetida through the instrument which we have described for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, because it will fall.
And there is another way of operation in fumigation of it, which is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole; and after that the fumigation should descend; and the patient should put their mouth on the end of the tube, and close their mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, because the leech will fall immediately.
And if it doesn't fall, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and the patient should abstain from drinking, and eat salty things and garlic, and not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps as you see, except that there is some curvature in it: which is put to the throat, and the end of it is similar to a stork's beak, which is wide, in which there is roughness of a file, because, when it compresses something, it doesn't let it go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the discourse on the opening of abscesses and on their cutting.
Abscesses are of many types, of many kinds, according to what comes to memory of them, of each in this book.
And they are diverse in perforation and cutting of them in two ways, of which one is by the type of abscesses in themselves; and by what they contain of humors.
And the second way is near the places in which they occur in the body, because the abscess occurring in the head is different from the abscess occurring in the buttocks; and the abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from the abscess occurring in a joint; and each of them has its judgment for operation.
And more of abscesses are some which should not be perforated except after maturation of the pus in them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be pierced when they are raw, and are not matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near to the joints, because when an abscess comes near a joint, its being is prolonged until what is in its circuit rots, and very easily the ligaments or the nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore it is made a cause of contraction of that member; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay the perforation of it until it is matured it will harm the principal member; or it is near the buttocks, so perforate it unmatured; and it is not fitting that you perforate it raw, of uncompleted digestion, except that which is near the buttocks, so that the depth of it does not rot and is elongated to the inner parts of the buttocks, and a fistula is made, or that it is made in its end that it is not healed. It's important that you know the time for perforating the abscess, and this is when it has matured.
And know that it is mature when there is a cessation of pain and fever, and after a reduction in redness and pulsation, and the sharpness of the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And because it's necessary that you know the perforation should be at the lowest point of the abscess and most conducive to healing of the protrusion.
And perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in parts of the hands or feet, and in place of the muscles, the cords of nerves, of arteries; and, lastly, in all equal places where there is no reflection; because in those that are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you leave the perforation of it until the digestion of it is confirmed according to completion, as we have said; because if you perforate it before this, the course of its pus and much filth is prolonged, and very easily its lips and its depth are hardened.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body towards necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is appropriate that you administer small perforation, and in large abscesses large and wide perforation or many incisions according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are of the abscesses in which it is appropriate that the skin be hollowed out and incised, when it is made like bread and made at a term that it already remains, as that which occurs in many boils and buboes.
And of those that are incised with a section having three angles; and of those is in which it is incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf, as in the abscesses of the groin.
And of them are those in which round incision and lunar incision are administered, and similar to those.
But that which does not have a head as are superficial lion-like abscesses, it is appropriate that you perforate it with superficial perforation, or flat only.
And it is appropriate when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, that you perforate it and do not hurry to extract all the pus at that time; rather extract a part of it, and then seal the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger part, and then do this many times gradually until it is expelled.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or small child, or old in decrepitude, because the animal spirit is often dissolved with the egress of the pus, and very easily the patient dies, and you lose them.
Therefore guard this chapter with great caution, and after your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, it is appropriate that you clean the wounds and consider then if the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then you administer the lint of flax or old cotton; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then it is appropriate that you place a wick in each hole until they come together.
And if in the abscess a part of the skin is cut off or you have hollowed it out in the circuit, then it is appropriate that you fill it with old cotton or thread made of linen cloth, as when you have drawn out bread or roots, without moisture; and tighten it until the third day; and then proceed with it and treat it with what is appropriate of the ointments until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs at the time of your operation, then you administer cold water and vinegar in which you soak a linen cloth, and place it over the site of the blood flow many times.
But if the blood flow persists, then it is appropriate that you administer treatment and cure and powders which I have recounted in many places of this our book and of division.
But if it is in winter time, and the place of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then it is appropriate that you infuse compresses in hot wine and oil, and place these on the areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then it is appropriate that you place compresses soaked with water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then it is appropriate that you unbind the abscess and clean it and administer in its treatment what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already spoken of every abscess in what manner is the way of its cure by chapters individually.
Chapter 41.
On the incision of abscesses which come in the skin of the head.
In the skin of the head small abscesses, and they are of species of nodes which contain tunics that are made for them; and their species are many.
And of them are pingas, and of those are those which contain moisture similar to lima. And of those are those which contain moisture similar to dust, and similar to them; and of those are those which are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all, and in their extraction there is no fear, as towards their extraction there is no contrary artery.
And the operation in their incision is that you examine them with an instrument the form of which comes after this, which is called entromes, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is moisture, then you cut it with an incision according to the length by superficial incision according to this form:
And begin in the incision of the line of B until the line of G. Then when the moisture is evacuated, then you excise that skin that contains that moisture, and do not leave any of it at all, for many times it returns, if any of it remains.
And after submerge cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is present, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it and leave it until another day, for with this it corrodes all that remains of that little skin.
And after iterate over that cotton with Egyptian ointment secondly and thirdly if you need it, until you are confident that no part of that little skin remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then you incise it according to this form: with an incision having the form of a cross, and put hooks between two lips of the incision and peel it from every part, and exert your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if an artery opposes you, then do what I have recounted to you first.
And similarly do in the petrified abscess of incision and of treatment according to what we have told you; and incision on a petrified abscess is easier, for it is of little blood and of little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head in some men with whom there is no moisture at all; which thing is because I incised on an abscess in the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a deaf stone, rough, white, which could not be broken, and if someone were struck with it, it would wound them.
But in the other abscesses occurring in the head besides this this chapter is not, such as the abscesses that come to the heads of children and towards the roots of the ears, but the incision of all these should be superficial incision.
And place their perforation on the lowest part of them so that the body of the pus is relieved to the lower parts.
And after treat those with what is suitable to that of treatment.
Chapter 42.
On the cutting of scrofulas which occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and in the armpits and in the groins; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And these are those that occur in the neck, and there are many that are generated among them, and every scrofula of these is within its own tunic, and just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for of these some are stony, and of these are those that contain moisture, and of these are malignant ones which do not yield to treatment.
Therefore in that which you see of good disposition in the Touch, of which the color of what is manifest is close to the color of the skin, and is moved to all parts, and is not attached to the nerve of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, it is fitting that you incise it with a superficial cut from the upper part to the lower part of the body from the line of G to the line of B, and flay it from all parts, and extend the two lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is more cautious that the vein or nerve not be cut.
And let the spatula not be very sharp, lest it join your hand with a cut, or that it disturb the patient, and that you cut what is not necessary to cut; for if you cut a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from your operation, then place crushed vitriol or other powders which stop blood flow in the wound, and tighten the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess rests and the wound is softened and begins to be putrid. When the blood is stopped.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And afterwards inquire with your index finger if any of the other small scrofulas remain, then cut those and clean them.
And if there is a large vein in the root of the scrofula, it is then fitting that you do not cut that scrofula from its root. Rather, it is fitting that you tie it with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls by itself without harm.
And afterwards fill the wound with old cotton which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and afterwards treat it.
And if you cut that whole scrofula, then it is fitting that you join the two lips of the wound and sew immediately since no superfluity has remained there.
But if you see that superfluity has remained due to the size of the scrofula, it is fitting that you intend to cut its root, and extract it and administer suture and what is said.
And that of the scrofulas which contain moisture, perforate it with superficial perforation there where the place of its maturation appears to you, and place perforation of what follows the lowest of the body, as we have said.
And afterwards administer perforation, cauterize with ointment and similar to those, so that it may corrode what remains of the corruption. And afterwards when you see the wound already to be cleaned, then you treat it with flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
On cutting the growth of an abscess occurring inside the throat.
The ancients spoke of this incision in the epiglottis, and no one in our land has been seen to have done it.
And this is the transcription of their discourse.
But in men having quinsy, it is fitting that the extraction of the epiglottis be avoided, as it does not provide help because all the veins and major ones of the patient are there.
