But if the other members are removed and the head returns, then let the midwife insert the left hand; but if the mouth of the womb is open, then let her insert the hand into the depth of the air, and search with it for the head, and draw it with the fingers to the mouth of the womb, and afterwards insert into it one hook, or two hooks, and draw it with those.
And if the mouth of the womb is constricted by hot swelling which happens to it, then it is fitting that it not be troubled over; rather it is fitting then to administer an effusion of fatty moist things. And administer plasters; and follow the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if it is possible, let it be needled: and let that which we have said be administered in the living fetus.
And if that is not possible, then let it be cut into pieces, and let it be extracted.
And it is fitting that you do not leave anything inside of the afterbirth.
And when you will have completed all your operation, then administer that which we have said of the cure in the hot swellings which happen to the womb.
And if flux of blood happens, then descend into styptic waters and administer the other cure.
And I have already seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and afterwards upon that she was again pregnant, and afterwards the other fetus was dead. It happened to her after a long time a swelling in her navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to the cure of it, and medicated it for a long time, and the wound was not consolidated.
Then I placed upon it some things, a plaster of strong attraction, and a bone came out of it, and after some days passed, and another bone came out; and I was very amazed, as in the belly there is no bone.
And then I thought that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.
Therefore I widened the wound, and extracted from it many bones, the woman existing in her best dispositions, and already lived in such a manner for a long time, a little pus ran from it.
And I do not affirm this to be strange, except that in this is science and help to that which the skilled physician administers with the hand for cure.
On the forms of instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of a speculum with which the womb is opened:
This is the form of a clamp in which the books are equalized, to which equalization are two screws at the two extremities of two pieces of wood. However, this screw should be more subtle than a writing instrument, and be made either of yew or boxwood.
And the width of each piece of wood is about two fingers and the margin of each both about one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle place of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same wood which are already fastened, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions should be inserted into the mouth of the womb so that it is opened with them when you turn the two aforementioned screws.
Another form similar to that one, more subtle and lighter:
It should be made of yew wood or boxwood according to the likeness of forceps. But at the extremity of it are two additions as you see, and the length of those additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the womb with them, then you make the woman sit on the bed with feet hanging, opening what is between her thighs. And afterwards open your hand with the instrument as you do with scissors equally according to the quantity you want of the opening of the mouth of the womb until the midwife does what you want.
The form of speculum which the ancients described:
Form of impeller with which the fetus is impelled:
Form of large amisidat with which the large head is crushed
And it is similar to forceps which have teeth at the extremity, as you see, and sometimes it is made long like forceps according to this form, as you see, which have teeth like the teeth of a saw with which it is cut and crushed. Form of small amesidat:
Form of hook having one spine:
Form of hook having two spines:
Form of spatula having two extremities with which the head is cut off:
Form of two wide spatulas:
All these instruments are exercised, and the more the species of it are multiplied and are prepared by the craftsmen, the easier it is for its operation and the higher it is for men by its quantity.
And do not work with these instruments even if it is prepared for you, because the necessity of them is not excusable.
On extraction of the placenta.
When the placenta is retained after childbirth, then you should command the patient to hold her breath, and afterwards make her sneeze with condizi, and press your hand over her mouth and over her nostrils, and if by this it has come out, it is good.
And if not, then take a pot and perforate a hole in the lid and put in it herbs opening the mouth of the womb, such as calamint and rue and anise and chamomile and Arabian sticados and cassia lignea and centaury. All these herbs or some of them should be submerged in water in the pot, and place it over the fire.
And afterwards place a reed tube in the mouth of the womb, and hold it until the vapor comes to the cavity of the womb.
And afterwards make her sneeze as we have said, and the placenta will be expelled with velocity.
But if it remains after this cure and has not come out, then you command the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or in marsh mallow mucilage, and afterwards insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta with it, and when you have found it, then take it and extend it little by little. But if it is attached in the depth of the womb, then insert her hand as we have recounted, and when she has found the placenta, extract it little by little according to straightness so that the womb does not fall towards the vehement attraction. First, it is necessary that it be moved to the right and left sides with ease.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already recounted the cure with sneezing and with medication of the pot with herbs.
But if it is not expelled with all the things which we have recounted, then be careful not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction, but instead it is necessary that what has come out of it to the mother's mouth be tied to the woman's thigh, and afterwards apply an ointment and tetrapharmacon to her; for that will cause it to rot after a few days and it will be dissolved and expelled.
But when it rots, a bad odor rises from it to the head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then it is necessary that fumigations suitable for this be administered.
But some of the Ancients have already tried fumigation with watercress and dried figs.
Form of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and of the afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or copper.
And the thin end should be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation should be placed on the embers of the fire.
And hold it until that fumigation is consumed and repeat with another.
On the cure of an infant's unpierced anus.
Many infants are born and their anuses are not pierced, which have a thin and blocked membrane. Then it is necessary for the midwife to pierce that membrane with her finger; and if not, pierce it with a sharp spatula, and be careful not to touch the muscle.
And afterwards place wool soaked in wine and oil on it; and afterwards treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear it may be blocked, then place a cannula in the hole for many days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And obstruction of the anus can occur through healing of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this healed area be cut open, and afterwards be treated with what we have said about the cure and the lead.
On the cure of fistulas that occur below.
The fistulas that occur in the lower part are nodules and swellings which occur near the anus outside or in the space of one part.
And it is one fistula or many.
And when that nodule is old and a white watery fluid or thin pus flows from it, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known by what comes out of them through excretion and flatulence; and sometimes the patient experiences excretion, and very easily a worm comes out of them.
And some of these fistulas are sometimes in the space penetrating to the bladder, and to the urethra; and some of them penetrate to the joint of the thigh and to the tailbone.
And that by which a fistula penetrating to the anus and a non-penetrating one is known, is that you put your index finger in the anus, and insert a thin probe of copper or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger.
And if you don't feel it at all and nothing comes out of the hole like excrement or worm or flatulence, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then hasten to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating to the bladder or to the urethra, then its sign is the exit of urine from it and its prevention from being healed with medicine.
But if it is penetrating to the joint of the thigh or to the bone and there is pain around it, then it is a sign that the probe reaches there if there is no twisting to the bone, and there is pain in the area around the part; and that pus always comes out of it; and that medication does not help, nor is it healed with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the cure of these is the work and vanity of foolish doctors on which they are fixed.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is expected as I will tell you, and that which I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises his legs up, the thighs are inclined to his belly, and then insert a lead or copper probe if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe reaches.
And if the patient feels that around the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger the probe already uncovered penetrating by itself, beyond what you feel between your finger and that membrane or flesh, then know for certain that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no healing in it, as we have said.
But some said that it is rarely healed in some men.
And of the medication by which help is expected in it is that you heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been done before, and insert that heated instrument into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those joined fistulated flesh that are similar to a bird’s feather quill have already been burned.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in cloth until all that burned flesh comes out, and then treat it with medicines that generate flesh and consolidate, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it is not healed at any time by another cure.
And if the probe enters, and does not penetrate to your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a thick veil of flesh or membrane, and you see the fistula in what follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you go cutting with the probe, and let it be in the fistula until the cut reaches the place where the end of the probe reaches, and by the probe it is freed and falls.
And then clean those fistulated flesh which are similar to a bird’s feather quill and let nothing of those remain.
But if you see that a flow of blood comes between you and your operation when you cut those flesh, then the best and lightest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with sharp medicine, for cautery with fire adds two good effects: that is burning of those joined flesh, and stopping of the blood and drying of the humidities.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in cloth or in crushed lime until the place discharges pus, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate hard flesh and consolidate.
And these are medicines in which there are astringency and drying; when then it is restored it is already healed and you do not fear repetition.
But if the fistula already reaches to the depth of the anus and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
And then if you feel the probe and between you and it is a veil of membrane or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation in it except according to the way of trust and hope.
The matter is to administer in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or cut it until you reach its cavity so that it is possible for you to introduce the instruments and the proper cure; and do not proceed much with cutting so that you do not cut the muscle containing the anus, and that accidental discharge of excrement without will occurs to the patient; and afterwards cure it with what we have said because it will be healed very quickly; or that you elongate that veil and clean it more than you cut it until near the anus, as we have said, or with a probe or with another instrument with a sharp end, and afterwards purify those fistulated flesh which are in the cut up to where you can with encrustation; and afterwards strive to make the restoration of the whole wound with cutting, and if the upper orifice is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the anus, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula is finished in this way: it is when you introduce the probe into the fistula, and it is on the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the skin and on the edge of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle of those who prepare leathers according to this form:
and introduce in it a thread wrapped with five threads or around those; and afterwards introduce the probe with thread into the fistula until it reaches its cavity.
But if it is penetrating in the edge of the anus near inside, then you extract the thread from that hole so that you introduce your finger into the anus; and extract the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and when the thread cuts more into the flesh, tighten it well until those flesh are cut which are between two ends of the thread and that fall.
And afterwards cure the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the orifice of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way it is prepared for you, unless it is very deep, because it is not for you to do that so that you do not cut the muscle; and afterwards do with it until it is healed.
The form of the spiny spatula with which the fistula is split: the curvature of which is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
About the suture of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, because they are either between the anus resembling as if it were coals, or almost as if they were grape seeds. And of those are small and large, and blood always flows from them and it is one, and they are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
But however those that are outside the anus are usually of little humidity, from which flows citrine water or a little blood with continuous body, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the cure of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and to squeeze until the anus comes out and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you hurry and suspend with hooks or that you hold those with your nail, and afterwards cut those towards their roots.
But if the hooks are not held in those because of their humidity and softening of those, then take those with rough cloth or with cotton or with wool blanket, and extract those with your fingers, and afterwards cut those and sprinkle over them some hot powders after the incision, so that they are to those in place of cautery; or cauterize those as it is preceded in the chapter on cauterizing. And afterwards cure those with the other cure until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey to excretion, then administer an enema to the patient with a mild irritant, and wash with that what is in the anus so that it obeys to excretion easily when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is easier, and it is that you take them with your nail, or that you take them with a hook, and cut them, and afterwards treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And for those who avoid cutting, it is suitable to administer sutures to them in this manner: take wrapped thread and insert it into a needle and then extract the hemorrhoids to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle into the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and this laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong tightening, and afterwards tie the thread and extract the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them and do not sew it, so that the excess blood flows from it.
And afterwards place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or in wax; and place that on the anus and command the patient to rest. And afterwards leave that until it falls; and when it has fallen, treat those with ointments or with other treatment until it is healed.
And when they are old and medicine does not work on them, then it is suitable that you scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your nail until they are made moist and the uppermost layer is removed from them, which prevents them from healing; and afterwards treat them until they are healed.
Then you remove them with more vehement scraping than the first until they bleed a lot and are excoriated; and afterwards treat them until they are healed.
Know that alamermar, that is wart, and it is nothing but round knots according to the color of the body similar to the head of a nail, and it is made in all the body and mainly in the parts below the feet and on the toes, and it causes pain when walking.
And then it is suitable that what is in the circuit of the wart be cut, and be held with tweezers or with a hook, and be eradicated from its root; and afterwards treat it.
And if you want, cauterize it according to what is preceded in its chapter.
But veruca is a small rough fistulated round prominence on the surface of the body, and many times they come on the hands of children.
And its treatment is that you stretch and cut it, or that you tighten it with thread or with hair until it falls. And if you want, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or with sharp medicine.
But formica is a small felted or fistulated large prominence on the surface of the body going very deep, and when the cold finds it, it is felt in it similar to the bite of an ant, and it is made around the body.
And its treatment is that you take a tube of goose feather or of vulture feather, and it should be strong, and place it on the formica until the circle of the tube contains the parts of it; and afterwards revolve your hand with the tube until you cut what is in the circuit of the formica, and place it in the depth of the flesh, and that you eradicate the formica from its root.
And if you want, do this with a tube of copper or of iron according to this form:
Let the upper part of the tube be drawing to subtlety, deaf (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that on the finger the grasping of it is facilitated and its revolution.
And if you want cauterize the formica according to what is said before in the cauterization of atheyl in the chapter on cautery.
On the form of instruments used to administer enemas.
Sometimes enemas are made of silver, brass, or melted or hammered copper; and sometimes these instruments are made small and large according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose bottoms are weak or painful, their enemas should be small.
The form of large and small enemas:
The upper head of the cannula to which the bag is tied should be wide in this manner; and there should be a distinction where the bag is tied as you see.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the bottom, should be smooth (that is, not open) very slender, tending to fineness; and on one side of it should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the width of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays and a little thicker.
And the bag in which the medicines are placed should be of animal bladder or sheep skin, and its root should be a palm long.
And it should be perforated by many holes as it is rolled; and if the space between the holes is the thickness of a finger.
And then insert into those holes strong thread of raw thread which should be of ten threads, or thereabouts; and join the bag with it; and when the bag is placed in it, tighten it at the head of the enema between two distinctions with strong constriction, and then administer the enema through each hole: But the quality of administering with it is that you fill the bag with medicine, which should be heated according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and be careful that it is not too hot or too cold.
And then the patient should lie on his back, and raise his legs up and place underneath him a leather on which men have already lain or a sheet; and then anoint the extremity of the enema with oil or egg white or with mucilage of fenugreek or similar; and then insert it into the bottom with ease, and then the minister should take the bag and squeeze it with both hands and together with force until all that is in the enema is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the enema and the patient raises his legs to the wall.
And if the medicine forces him to evacuate, let him hold it as long as he can; and if he sleeps with it for a whole night, it is more beneficial.
But if you administer an enema to him for illnesses of looseness of the belly and the evacuation hurries him, then you repeat the enema until he is healed.
And if in the bottom there are hemorrhoids or painful swellings, then let the enema be very fine and slender, of the enemas with which children are treated.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have already said in the division of the treatment of wounds what was appropriate to the book, and I say here the treatment of those by hand and with medicines according to the completion.
Therefore I say that wounds are diverse according to what the wounds are made in and according to the place on which the wounds fall.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are such as throwing of stone, or cutting with sword or knife, or striking with lance, or with sticks, or with arrow, or similar to those of many things.
But the wounds that are in the places of the body are such as a wound which falls on the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the belly, or the liver, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, for the remembrance of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
So I say that when you encounter a simple wound on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or similar, and has only broken the skin and is a large wound, and you fear a hot abscess may form on that patient, then you hurry and bleed the cephalic vein immediately, and do not delay this; and extract blood from him according to the strength of his virtue if there is nothing prohibiting bloodletting, especially if the blood that has come out of the wound is little.
And let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded part; and let the patient guard against repletion and wine and heavy foods.
But if something prohibits the bleeding, then let the food be reduced and do not approach meat or wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or in wine in which it is styptic.
But if you are sure of a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent with its blood and the air has not changed it, the powder which I will recount shortly.
And if the air has already changed it, then place some ointments on it until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is from a sword cut and similar, and the lips of it are not joined by small stitches, then join those with sutures according to what I will recount about their suturing.
But if the wound is already uncovered to the bone and hanging, and there is no retention of it except a small suspensory, then cut it and afterwards cure the wound with medicines and restore the flesh afterwards in place of skin, and command the patient to be fed with foods in which there is thickness: heads and cooked wheat and similar to them, until it is composed.
And if you find putrefaction in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, then you cut all that is rotten and throw it away, and afterwards cure it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flow can occur, and it is not cut off by medicines, then you inquire about the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you cut it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity provokes you that what we have said does not confirm, then you cauterize until the blood is cut off.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is light; so that you powder over them this powder, for it consolidates them before the air changes them.
And the manner of the powder is this: take one part incense oil, two parts dragon's blood, three parts unslaked lime; let all be very well ground and sifted, and let the wound be filled with it, and be constrained by good constriction until it adheres well to it, and be coagulated with the blood.
And afterwards tighten it above with small cloths and leave it, and do not untie it while it remains so that it is not abscessed, for the medicine is not eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does this, when you do not have incense and dragon's blood present.
And this same operation is already done in large wounds when it is convenient that it be placed on them and they are tightened, and they are recent wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is in summertime, then there is no intention to the position of this powder, but then cure them with what we have said, so that you place on them some digestive ointments, or that you place on them powder of barley flour with water and honey until they throw out rottenness; and afterwards cure them with the other cure until they are healed.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with gestures.
And I will say the cure of the fractures of the head in what is to come.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound happens in the neck, then there is no difference in medication, and between the wound of the head when it is simple.
But if already the nerve of the neck is cut or an artery, then if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with that which causes strong stypticity, such as zinc and vitriol which are similar to them because they harm the nerves and contract them. And do not put on the wound anything completely cold, because the substance of the nerves is cold and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is that which is cured with light medicines, such as lime washed with sweet water, sometimes infused in rose oil or non-rose oil, and tutty when it is washed with sweet water and the other mineral stones according to this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or bring together its lips, and afterwards cure it with pledgets until it is healed.
But if the wound has depth or rot happens in it in the lowest part, then perforate it in the most depressed place.
But if in the wound an artery is already cut and a flow of blood follows, then separate or dry it, or tie it, or cauterize it if necessity provokes you to do so.
But if it has already cut the vertebrae of the throat or all, and the veins of the neck are saved, then you join the lips of the skin with sutures over the throat pipe and do not touch it; instead, equalize it and reduce it to its natural figure, and afterwards tighten it with decent tightness, and leave it for some days; and afterwards cure it with ointments in which there is stypticity and stimulation until it is healed.
But if the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then examine it, and then if there are fragments of bone in it, extract all those that you can of them; and that which is not prepared for you, leave it until the wound rots, and be separated and its severity be alleviated.
And if the wound is recent then sprinkle over it the powder which I have recounted to you. But if in the wound a fistula is made, then cure it with that which the remembrance will come after in its chapter.
If it is a blow from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider more if from the wound comes windiness when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if in it there is no depth and the wound is recent with its blood, then you put in it from the first hour powder, and do not tighten it so that the blood is not retained in its depth and that it returns to the heart and kills the patient, but put in it attractive ointment, and untie it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not present to you, then you put in the orifice of the wound old cotton so that it sucks that which comes out of the humidities; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that runs that which is gathered in it.
But if already three days or more have passed from the wound and spasm does not happen to the patient nor tremor of bad heart nor tightness of breath, and you see the other good dispositions of it, then know that the wound is safe; and then cure it with poultices and with the other cure until it is healed.
But if its healing is excused, and it is already always open, then know that a fistula has already been made; cure it then by its chapter.
And if the wound is by incision of sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then cure it with that which is preceded by suture, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone, and pieces are already cut from it, then you clean the wound and hasten to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound rots, so that their extraction is alleviated.
And of the other wounds happening in the other members, the help in curing is the help of that which we have said.
On the wounds of the belly.
The discomfort which occurs in the belly is either great, or small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and it follows that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not hurry to introduce them to their place immediately, because it opens and swells so that its entry becomes difficult.
And so the best rupture is moderate, for in that one the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in the two species mentioned before.
And know that suturing of the belly is done in four ways, but two sutures are common that are suitable for suturing other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures specific to communicating with other wounds, but they are more specific in suturing the belly.
And I recount to you each with exposition and declaration.
So I say that when the wound is small and something of the intestine has come out through it, and it is made difficult to reduce it, then it is in one of two ways: either by smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when swelling has come to the intestine due to the coldness of the air.
And so when it is like this, then it is necessary that you warm it so that you submerge a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water alone, or that you cook in water squinant, and cyperus, and spike, and you bathe with that the intestine until the swelling is resolved.
And sometimes wine does this in which there is astringency, and it is better than water alone in resolving the swelling.
And so when you see the swelling being resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which althaea and mallows are cooked, for by this its entry is eased with little effort.
But if its entry is excused after this treatment, then cut the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate than cutting is not, and there should not be occupation to dry after necessity and failure.
Of the instrument, its form follows:
One part of it curved and sharp, and the other part not sharp, and the extremity subtle, and not with the subtlety of a spatula, rather it should be a little blunt.
And so when the wound is enlarged and the intestine comes out, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form, in its proper place, if it is possible above this, for it is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and is in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary that you make the patient lie on his back, and place his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you place his head and chest higher than the lower parts.
And similarly if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your intention and your consideration always on this so that the part in which the wound is is higher than the other.
And so it is necessary that you administer this in great wounds and in moderate wounds.
And in small wounds, place what is prepared for you, and afterwards make the minister be between your hands carefully, who holds the whole rupture with his hand and joins its lips, and afterwards uncover from it to the one who follows the suture something after another.
And this is the manner of common suture: one where you take the needle, or count of needle according to the quantity of the width of the wound; and after leave from the extremity of the disruption of the ring finger, and tighten the needle one, other that you do not put thread in the two extremities of the skin, with two extremities of the peritoneum which is under the skin of the inner parts, until you make it penetrate from that part; and you have already gathered two extremities of the skin and two extremities of the peritoneum, and so made four orders.
And after tighten with double thread in the circuit of the needle, and sometimes from two parts together, and after gather the two lips and the wound by decent gathering; and after leave the thickness of the finger and stick another needle in it, and after tie it with thread as you did with the first needle; and do not cease to do this if it is necessary with the needles until you have completed all the contraction of the wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness, for very thin needles easily cut the flesh; and the thick ones are difficult to enter the skin, and therefore for this it is suitable that they be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not suitable that the needle be stuck in two extremities of the skin very close, so that the flesh is not easily cut and that the wound opens before its consolidation.
And they should not be prolonged from suture so that the wound is not prohibited from consolidation.
And after cut the extremities of the needles so that they do not hurt the patient towards his sleep; and you will put over the suture expanded pads from all parts, which hold the extremities of the needles; and leave those until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And these species of suture with needles in which manner is more convenient in small wounds, for it suffices in the suture of them one needle or two or around those.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you administer with suture the four extremities, of the skin and two extremities of the peritoneum, in one time, with needle in which is thread wrapped equal in thinness and thickness.
And after when with the needle you will have penetrated these four extremities, reduce the needle from that same part from which you began, so that the retracted thread falls from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture is according to suture of cloth with which the cloths are tightened.
And put between each suture the quantity of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture men know.
And with such suture I sewed the wound which had happened to a man in his belly who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than a palm, and about two palms of the middle intestine had come out and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced that after the intestine had been outside the wound for 24 hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of 15 days or around, and I cured that and it is healed; and after that he lived for many years, and is conversed in his dispositions.
And the other doctors had judged that he would not be healed; and by miracle it was, for I did not cure that with ointment, for it was in a place in which ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I put over the wound old cotton twice a day and assiduous ablution with honey water until it was healed.
But I will say two species of proper suture according to narration of Word.
And it is that you begin with suture of the skin, and introduce needle from the outside part to the inside part.
And so, as the needle penetrates the skin and into the same muscle according to the straightness and length of the whole body, and leaves the extremity of the peritoneum in which you have inserted the needle; and make it penetrate from the inside to the outside at the other extremity of the abdominal wall.
And when you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate again at that same extremity of the abdominal wall from the outside to the inside; and leave the extremity of the peritoneum which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate at the other extremity from the inside to the outside.
And make it penetrate with your penetration with which you make it penetrate the peritoneum at the extremity of the abdominal wall which is on the side of it until it all penetrates.
And afterwards begin from this same side and sew it with the extremity that is of the peritoneum on the other side, and extract the needle from the skin which it penetrated, and afterwards return it to that skin and sew the extremity of the peritoneum which is on the other side with this extremity of the abdominal wall; and extract it from the skin which is on the side of it.
And do this once and again, and do this once after another time until you sew all that which is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a suture of skins with which they sew skins, so that you sew the peritoneum once from the part of the extremity of the skin, and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second species of proper suture which Galen also said, and this is from his discourse and recounted by him, is that you sew it according to the similarity with which some doctors sew it, so that they aggregate every part to its comparable part convenient to it by nature; and applying the extremity of another to it and the extremity of the abdominal wall to another extremity of it, and this is done according to what I recount to you.
It is appropriate that you fix the needle in the extremity of the abdominal wall closest to you from the outside, and make it penetrate to the inside in that sole, and leave two extremities of the peritoneum; and afterwards turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in two extremities of the peritoneum.
And afterwards turn each of those, and make each penetrate from the inside to the outside in two other extremities of the abdominal wall that are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common light suture, and is a suture of which remembrance preceded.
This is the discourse of Galen
And moreover some said, suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is that you insert in these to each a thread, and begin with insertion of needle from what is towards you, and make it penetrate until the other side according to suture of those who sew leathers equally.
And know that when disruption is in the middle place of the belly, then the suture of it is more difficult than of the other places of the belly.
But the judgment of medication of wound is judgment of the other wounds.
Which thing is because when you follow the recent wound with its blood before the air alters it, and reduce the intestine and sew it and fit it decently, then place incarnative powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place on it some ointments which are present to you until they make a hard stone and the threads fall, and peritoneum and abdominal wall are consolidated; and afterwards cure it as is the cure of other wounds until it is healed.
But if the medicines are not present for you, then place immediately on it, as soon as it begins to discharge pus, old cotton, and change it twice a day, as has been taught to you, until it is healed, for you do not need other medication in many cases when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound might communicate injury to the main members, then it is appropriate that you submerge sheep's wool in oil of moderate quality, or in rose oil, and place it around the areas that are between the groins and under the armpits.
And if he feels pain or putrefaction in his bowels, for this happens many times, then give him an enema with lukewarm black astringent wine, especially if the rottenness has reached the intestine and has made a penetrating exit to its cavity.
And know that that which is of the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is of difficult healing because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing of a wound that falls in it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of the magnitude of those that are in them, and because of the subtlety of its body, and because of its proximity to the nature of nerves.
But if what proceeds from the wound is the membrane, and you catch it fresh, reduce it according to your straightness with which you equally reduce the intestines.
But if it has passed to it space and has already become green or black, then you should tighten it with thread above the place that is blackened of it, so that blood flow comes, for in the membrane there are veins and arteries.
And after, cut what is beyond that binding, and place two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it, so that the attraction of it towards the falling of the membrane and the emission of pus from the wound may be alleviated for you.
Remembrance of the wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, because I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a blow from a lance and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had become a fistula, and discharge and gas came out of it.
Then I pursued his cure although I was not confident in his healing, but I continued to treat him subtly until he was healed and the place was consolidated.
And I, after I saw the place already consolidated, feared for the patient that a bad accident might happen to him in his belly; and no bad accident happened because of this and he remained in his best dispositions eating and drinking, and afterwards he entered a bath and exercised in his sayings.
And some experienced ones have already said that when a wound happens in the intestine, and it is small, then it is appropriate that it be bound according to this manner: and it is that you take ants with big heads; and after join the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants that has its mouth open over the two lips of the wound; then when it takes hold of them and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and is not dissolved.
And after place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do this with ants one after another according to the quantity of the whole wound.
And after reduce it and sew the wound; and let those heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm happens to the patient.
And it is already possible that the intestine be tied with subtle thread which is made from the intestine of an animal attached to it, after it is put in a needle; and it is that you take the end of these intestinal threads, and let it be very lean, and after tie its end with flax thread, wrapped, and after introduce that thread into needles and sew with it the intestine, and reduce it in the disruption.
And this manner of suture with ants is only according to the way of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, for then there is no device except subtlety, and there is no healing of it at all.
On the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it becomes old and chronic, and turns into an ulcer, and is not healed and constantly discharges pus and is not cut out, then, in whatever part it may be, is universally called a fistula and we call it a zukem.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, felted knot which is not painful and which has a cavity like the hollow of a bird’s feather; and for this reason some call it rizatz, that is, a feather.
And in some people it is moist, discharging pus; and very easily the moisture is cut off at certain times. And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and it is scarce, and it is thick, and it is thin.
I have already shown all these causes, and their signs and their cure with medicines in the division.
And it is appropriate here that I make known to you the cure of fistula which is done by way of operation with the hand.
And know that fistula occurs in all the parts of the body; for of these are fistulas that reach to the great veins and arteries, or to the nerves, or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints composed by composition such as of the hand and of the foot; and of these are those that give to it proximity of the principal member and of similar ones.
And therefore that of these fistulas which is according to this manner is of the diseases of difficult healing, and does not receive cure, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
And if there is of the fistulas one the depth of which does not reach to these places that I have said, and you hope and trust in the cure of it, and you want to come to knowledge of it, then take a probe of copper or of iron, and if the fistula goes according to straightness, and you investigate it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuosity, then investigate it with a probe of subtle lead, for lead with the lightness of its body is lightened and curved towards the tortuosity.
And if the fistula is having many orifices, it is not possible for you that you signify upon it with a probe.
Then irrigate the orifice through its orifices, for the moisture with which you irrigate it goes towards the other orifices and flows from them.
And afterwards inquire exquisitely, according to which manner it is possible for you that you know if there you have bone or nerve, and if the cavity of the fistula is far or near, or if it is one fistula to which there are many orifices and it stands upon this all with the sum of your strength, with your interrogation, with which you interrogate the patient, and the invention of the pain towards your compression with the hand upon the place, and similar of the significations and of the causes happening to the abscess.
And when you will have stood upon all these causes with true standing, then go to the cure according to confidence: and it is that you consider then if it is an apparent fistula near or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula according to what is said before of the narration of it, and remove that which is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and that which is born in the orifice of the flesh, or of skin and of similar things, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you cut it as deep as possible, and afterwards clean it of all its corrupted flesh; and then administer some wrappings with strong medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have probed with iron.
And do this with it several times until the strong medicine corrodes all that remains of the corruption in the cavity of the fistula.
And afterwards restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have described.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you have confirmed this, then you investigate it and dry it as we have described, if you do not prohibit any cause of vein or nerve or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and afterwards treat it with that which makes it flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healthy and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption; and then you uncover it again and carefully perform the scraping and cleansing of it with your utmost strength, and afterwards restore it.
And so if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then know that this corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which has not been reached, and then for you in that case there is no solution but to leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to extract it, then you extract it with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then carefully perform the extraction of all of them, and of those do not leave any with your strength.
But if any cause of moisture creates a problem for you, and in their incision there is no fear or error, then cut those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then place on the wound that which will rot that wound, and leave it until that is around those bones of flesh and ligaments, and guard the wound so that it is not consolidated, and let the section which you have cut in your operation be constricted.
Before, if you fear, place on it cotton soaked in sulfur ground with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in one of the green ointments, because when those ligaments rot, and the bones are separated from them by removal, and their extraction is eased.
But if it is a large bone such as the bone of the leg or thigh or similar to those, and that which is corrupted of it is only its surface, then scrape it with only the last scraping until that blackness and corruption is removed, and afterwards restore the wound.
But if that which is corrupted of it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to enclose it and cut it all until the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is consolidated.
And I tell you that which happened to a man in his thigh so that you may use it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man about 30 years old, who experienced pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts it moved over the matter inside the body until the matter reached the thigh, and it is the matter of an abscess with a large swelling, and it was not caused by something external.
And then the time was extended with that due to the error of the doctors until the abscess opened, and many materials flowed from it, and its cure was postponed until the leg became fistulated, and many orifices were made in it which all discharged pus and bodily fluids.
And then a multitude of doctors treated him for about two years, and there was no wise hand in the art among them, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the materials that were flowing from those orifices in great quantity; and already the body of that man was all emaciated and his color was yellow.
And then I inserted the probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And afterwards I examined all the orifices and found that they reached their destination from all parts of the leg.
And then I hurried and cut over one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already earthy and corrupted and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted until it had penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed what was uncovered and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I thought that there was nothing in the bone but that corruption which I had cut away and sawed, and I had already uprooted it.
And afterwards I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicine for a long time, and it did not heal.
And afterwards I repeated and uncovered the bone a second time over the first uncovering, and I found continuous corruption with the bone.
And I sawed what appeared to me of that corruption, and afterwards I was forced to make the cure and restoration of it, and it was not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to cut the bone little by little, bit by bit, and forced its restoration, and it was not restored until I cut about a palm's length of it, and extracted it with its marrow, and afterwards I restored it with medicines, and it healed easily and was cured.
And repetition in its operation and cutting was not necessary except for the disposition of his weakness and for the scarcity of his tolerance and his fear of death, for he would faint at all hours due to excessive evacuation.
And thus he is healed by complete healing, and hard flesh was born in place of the bone; and his disposition in his body was made good and his strength returned; and he went about his affairs and it did not cause him any difficulty in walking or harm in any way.
But if there is a protruding bone in a place of the body already pierced, then you should cut it in this manner: take a binding and tighten it at the end of that protruding bone, and command someone to extend it upwards; and place that binding of wool thicker than the first binding; and afterwards tie it over the flesh that is below the bone, and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh to the lower parts, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you want to saw so that the saw does not harm the flesh.
And place wood or a board below the bone decently, for when you do this the saw is not prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And the sawing should be done over the corrupted place little by little, for if there is corruption in the concavity of the bone and it does not appear in its appearance to the senses, it forces another sawing of it.
If the bone is corrupted and not prominent, before one part is continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or in part of it, then you uncover all the flesh from all parts; and afterwards place wood underneath, and then saw that from the first part where the corruption is until the cut is continuous with the other part.
And let the cut be according to the location of the corruption a little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason in it except ingenuity.
And if the corruption is in the chest of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, it is necessary that you investigate the corruption in whatever manner it appears to you, and that you abrade it, and clean it according to each disposition which is possible for you, and with each ingenious method it is rectified for you, when vein or nerve does not pose a challenge to you.
And know that the cutters and saws to cut these bones are many according to the situation of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their subtlety and their size and their smallness, and their hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument suitable for that operation.
And know that this operation indicates to you about the kind of instrument which you need; when you have great study and long and knowledge of the manners of this art, and testimony of manners of illnesses, for then you already have for yourself what is suitable of instruments for every illness.

On the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
The extremities rot either due to internal or external causes, or when you have treated that corruption with the cure of those medicines and the cure does not work, or you see the corruption spreading to the limb which you did not strike for any reason, then it is necessary that you cut that limb up to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may escape death or malice which is a greater weakness of the limb.
And the sign of that which happens is that the limb becomes black until it seems that it was already burned, or it rots after the blackness until that rot spreads to the limb that follows it and takes over the whole body; and then hurry with the incision of that.
And similarly if the cause of the corruption is the strike of some venomous things such as sea scorpion or viper or spider and similar ones.
And if the corruption or the strike is at the extremity of the finger, then you cut the finger and do not give space to the corruption to spread to the hand.
And similarly if it reaches the hand, then you cut it from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption until it takes over the bone of the arm.
And if it invades that, then cut the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that is the death of the patient; and administering anything else for cure is a matter of virtue.
And similarly do with the foot when corruption invades the toe, then you cut it up to one of the knuckles.
And if it takes over the instep of the foot, then you cut the whole foot. But if it rises up to the knee, then you cut the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no solution but dismissal, and leaving the patient to death.
The manner of cutting the limb or sawing it is that you tighten the binding in the lowest part where you want to cut, and tighten another binding above the place; and the minister extends a binding to the parts below, and the minister extends another binding above; and you separate the flesh which is between two bindings with a wide spatula until all the flesh is uncovered; and afterwards it is cut or sawed.
And it is necessary that a linen cloth be placed on all sides so that it does not touch the healthy place, and that no added injury and hot swelling come to the patient.
But if blood flow occurs when you work, then you cauterize the place quickly, or place on it some powders that restrict the blood; and afterwards return to your medication until it is complete.
And afterwards bind the wounded limb with a suitable binding, and treat it until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have told you about, which is that blackness occurred in his foot with a burning that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe until it spread to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, as he saw the treatment going to the limb with the vehemence of what he found of pain and burning of it, and he cut that foot up to the joint, and was healed.
A long time later, that same corruption occurred in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that excess with what I placed on the hand of medicines, after the purification of his body; and the excess is not repelled and began to go to another finger until it began in the hand.
And he called me to cut his hand, and I did not want to do that hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and more that I feared towards the cutting of his hand death, because the strength of the patient was declining.
And after he despaired of me, he returned to his land and it came to me that that man prepared and cut his whole hand, and is healed.
And I have not brought this narration except that it be help on what falls from this disease, and that it be a sign by which it is signified and according to which it is worked.
On the cure of sinuses and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When a swelling occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the swelling is prolonged until it makes a collection of pus, and afterwards it is broken and perforated, and the pus has come out of it, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is above it as if a thin cloth, and has not reached to the last corruption, and does not impress on the bone or on nerve or on ligament, it is called a sinus, and is not named fistula.
But however if the space is prolonged until the corruption impresses on some member, then it is called fistula and not sinus.
And the cure of it is that you cut all that skin just as it is revolved, and especially when it is thinned and is made like cloth, and you trust that it does not adhere to the place by its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin adheres and that that corruption has not achieved by last achievement, and in the skin there is thickness of face, then you cure that with clyster.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the pus that comes out is of fetid odor, then clysterize that with Egyptian medication which is of oil and vinegar and ziniar receiving equal parts, and let them be added in one vessel, and let them be cooked over the fire until the medication becomes red and reaches the thickness of honey.
And afterwards you take from it what is necessary and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize with that the sinus, and tighten the orifice of it, and leave the medicine in it for the quantity of two hours.
And then extract that with a probe; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleaned and the odor is removed.
And sometimes it is cleansed with ash water, when this medicine is not available to you; and it is that you take vine ash, and put it on water; and then strain it and cleanse it with it until you are confident that the sinus is then removed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor the ash water, then cleanse it with water and honey and as if mixed with ground ginger, and cleanse it with wine and mixed honey, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes the sinus adhere, and especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you do this sometimes and you are confident that the sinus is already removed, then cleanse it with that which makes flesh grow in it.
And it is that you take diaphoenicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you cleanse it with some other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written in the treatise on ointments.
And if the orifice of the sinus is narrow in which the cannula of the enema is not received, then you widen it little by little with iron, and put in it something wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment until it is widened.
And similarly if the orifice is wide, then you bring together its lips with suture and leave of it a quantity in which the enema enters without addition.
And if the orifice of it from which the pus flows is elevated to the upper parts, then dry it in the lowest place that is in it so that the pus flows down to the bottom, for when the pus is confined in the depth of the sinus the flesh is generated in it.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus to around the lowest bone as you wish, then force yourself to prepare the member with a preparation by which the pus flows from it easily as is prepared for you and that nothing of the pus is retained in it.
But that which is over the orifice of the sinus, which you remove every time so that what is gathered in it of the pus comes out. And so that you know if what flows from it of the pus is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this you consider the place of that sinus, if the patient feels pain in it or not, and if there is an abscess in it.
And when your medication is prolonged in such a manner, and you see the matter that is thrown on the orifice of the sinus already adhering, or is approaching adherence, then in the morning you unbind it or on the third day, and when you unbind the wound, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus after a long space thin virus comes out, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is approaching, for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is delayed, and of those there are in whom the contrary of it is done.
And if from the sinus after many days undigested pus comes out, then know that the sinus does not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you add in drying of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dry like dyafinicon ointment, when there is added to it excess addition of zinc.
And the last way with which it is cured, is that you take dyafinicon ointment and it is dissolved in rose oil, and old wine equal in its substance is poured over it, and afterwards mix that with it, and use that.
And it already makes similar in that action, when there is no pus, dyafinicon ointment, honey when it is cooked until it is thickened, and use it; or that you take myrrh and aloe and incense, and grind all and pulverize over the honey when it is on the fire, and afterwards spread that on a cloth and tighten it over the sinus; and spread that with the honey which you have cooked until it is thickened, and pulverize on cloth and tighten it.
And sometimes they are administered with it long aristolochia and root of celestial lily (and it is celestial lily) and flour of vetch and centaury, and use those simples or combined, grind those and sift and pulverize over the honey which I have recounted; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this cure, then know that the corruption is in the depth of it, and already makes inquiry in the bones or in the other hard members or to the ligaments; then you cure it with fistular cure, as is said above.
On the cure of whitlow and bruised nail and incision of added finger and section.
Whitlow is an abscess which is born under the nail of the thumb of the hand or of the foot, and very easily born in the other fingers, and when it is prolonged and its cure is less prized, or it is abscessed from hot abscess, or it is corrupted, and throws pus until it corrodes the root of the nail, and very easily corrupts all of it, and very easily its corruption reaches the bone until it has a fetid odor, and the extremity of the finger is made wide, and its color is made dark.
And so when you will have that with what we have said in the division and you do not complete your cure, then it is fitting that you cut with iron all the superfluity that is remaining of the nail and afterwards cauterize the wound after that, for cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the empty additions of the nail already compress the flesh to the parts within, and begin to prick it and injure it, then it is fitting that you place a subtle ray under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and push it up and cut that flesh with ease, and place over that which remains of the flesh burning corrosive medicines until it is all removed; and afterwards cure that with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption already impresses on the bone, then it is fitting that you cut that bone and extract it, for at no time is it healed that the corrupted bone be in it.
And if you see the corrosion and the corruption going into the finger, then you cut it at one of the knuckles according to what is promised its remembrance, and afterwards cure that until it is healed.
But if to the nail happens percussion or attrition, and happens in it vehement pain, then it is fitting that you at the beginning bleed the patient, and afterwards dry the nail with sharp spatula with transverse section from above downwards, and guard yourself that the section does not reach to the flesh that is under the nail, for you will make happen to the patient vehement pain, and it will be cause to birth of superfluous flesh added in the place; and afterwards cure that place with that which yields pain.
But it is said together, which is born in the hands of some men, very easily it becomes all fleshy, and very easily there are bones in it, or very easily it is in the nail.
And some of them are born in the root of the joint, or their birth is in some knuckles of the fingers. But that which is born in some knuckles of the finger does not move, and this which is born in the joints very easily is moved.
And of those that are born fleshy, the cutting is easy, and it is that you cut that towards its root with a wide spatula.
But of that which is born in the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of cutting it off.
But in that which is born in the finger towards the knuckles, it is fitting that you cut at the beginning its flesh with a round incision up to the bone, and afterwards saw with one of those saws suitable for this, and afterwards cure it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers among themselves many times either by birth of the man who is born thus, or by consolidation of a wound or by burning of fire, or by similar things, then it is fitting that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And afterwards place between them linen cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not easily consolidated, and separate between them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as it should be.
And similarly if consolidation happens in some fingers with the palm, then you dry that consolidation according to what is prepared and suitable to the shape of the member.
On the incision of varicose veins.
Varicose veins are swollen and twisted veins full of melancholic superfluities which come to many parts of the body, and most commonly occur in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and porters.
Thus it is appropriate that you administer a purging of the body of black bile sometimes with strong expulsion, and afterwards bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But their cure with iron is according to two methods: one is that they be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be elevated and extracted entirely.
The section of it is according to this method: first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick blood is dissolved, which is the impurity of the leg, and then tighten the patient's leg from the uppermost part of the thigh to the part below the knee with a bandage, and then cut the vein in one place, or in two, or in three with a wide incision, and then elevate the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg up to the top, and from top to bottom, until the amount of blood you want has come out and that you see is sufficient, and which the patient's strength can tolerate.
And then bind it, and command him to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the cure by phlebotomy when the veins are filled and this harms the patient.
But the elevation of them is according to this method: you who shave the patient's leg, if there are many hairs on it, and then put him in a bath and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are swollen; or let him be exercised by strong exercise, if a bath is not available to you, until the limb is heated.
And then cut the skin in line with the vein by an incision lengthwise or behind it towards the knee, or in the lowest part of it towards the heel.
And then open the skin with hooks and flay the vein from all sides until it appears to the touch, and it is towards its appearance that you see it red inclining to black.
And then as you remove it and see it from the skin, you see it white almost like a cord.
And then you put the probe under it until it is elevated and thrown out of the skin, and suspend it with a blunt hook.
And then make another incision after that incision by three fingers, and then flay the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears.
And then elevate it and cut it in the last incision towards the heel, and then extract it and elevate it until it has come out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above that.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or uppermost of all the incisions, until it is all out; and then cut it.
And if it does not obey you to extract and elevate, then insert a needle with strong double thread and tie it and extract it, or insert the probe under it and turn your hand in all directions until it is thrown out. And be careful that it is not cut; for if it is broken, it becomes very difficult for you to elevate it and harm has come upon the patient because of it.
And when you have thrown it all out, then place over the site of the wound wool soaked in wine and rose oil and non-rose oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is contracted having tortuosity to which there is convolution to the parts, and is not straight as we have said, then it is appropriate that you split it over all parts of its tortuosity and at the places of its convolution, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate all of it.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not wound it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and maintain your prudence in this.
Form of the cutting instrument with which the vein is cut:
It does not have sharpness like the hooks nor is it of sharp extremity so that it does not wound the vein; and it is of thick flexibility, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein by its thinness; rather it has excessive thickness as we have said.
On extracting a vein from the leg.
This vein is formed in the legs in hot lands such as in Ategi in the land of the Arabs and in the hot and arid lands of India with little tenderness and where no grass or trees grow.
And it is easily formed in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its gestation is from putrefaction that comes under the skin just as serpents and worms and mites and worms are generated inside the body between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of its coming is that this vein causes severe inflammation in the leg; and then the area becomes blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the site of that blister almost like the root of a plant.
And then when its extremity appears, it is fitting that you wrap over it a piece of lead weighing from one to two drachms, and tie it and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as something of it comes out to the outer parts, wrap it in lead and tie it. And if it is much prolonged, then you cut a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not cut it from the root until it has come out completely, because if you cut it, it contracts and enters the flesh and causes an abscess and rotting in the place and bad ulceration.
Therefore it is fitting that it be treated gently and be extracted little by little, until the last of it is thrown out and nothing of it remains in the body.
And from this vein has come out in some men that which is in its length of 5 palms and ten, and it has even come to me that one came out of 20 palms.
And if it is cut at the time of its medication, then introduce the probe into its hole and perforate it with a long hole with the body, until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and administer putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and afterwards cure it by the cure of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices come to it, and a branch has come out of each orifice.
And then cure it as we have said in the division and with what is said above.
On cutting over worms generated under the skin and called bovine disease.
This disease is named in some land near us bovine disease because it often happens to cattle and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and it goes throughout the whole body rising and descending, and it is shown to the senses by its movement from member to member until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by putrefaction of some humors just as worms and serpents and roundworms and mites happen in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, because when it goes in the body and is elevated to the head, and has reached the eye, then it very easily opens into it and enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
And so when you want to cure it and extract it, then it is not done except towards its movement and appearance to the senses, and then it is fitting that you tighten what is above it and what is below it with a binding and strong constriction, and afterwards cut over it and extract it.
And if it is deep in the flesh and you do not find it, then you place a cautery with fire over the place until it dies, and the main thing that is feared is its corruption with which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see that it has reached the head near the eye, then tighten over the eyebrow with strong constriction, and afterwards cut over it, and extract it; and it is fitting that the patient use cleansing of his body with medicines evacuating putrid bad humors, and guard himself from foods generating putrefaction.
On the treatment of the illness called nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some body parts, and then moves from one part to another.
And I have seen it as I will tell you.
