I was called to a sick woman in a village, and uncovered her arm and saw a small swelling in the main vein of the arm, and after an hour, I saw that swelling move like a worm climbing up it, but more lightly than can be, as if it were quicksilver running from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and settled in the muscle, and stayed there for an hour.
And then it ran through the other body until it reached the other arm.
And then she told me that it moved all over her body as I saw; and I was very amazed at its movement and the speed from one part to another. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain moving from one part to another. But I did not see it with my eyes as I did in this woman.
And I do not think this is except because the woman was old, where there are dry bodies and exposed veins, and in her this moving wind appeared to the senses.
And it is necessary that this does not appear according to this backward-running in those who are delicate and with moist bodies of hidden veins.
And when you want to cure it, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on it and below it lightly and dry it until that constrained wind has come out, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you cure it with shaking of the body and with that which expels winds, and treat those with fetid pills and with pills of serapium and similar things.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types, and according to the places of the body where they fall, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small, and hollow, and solid ones; and some that have three angles, and some that have barbs; and some that have splinters.
And of the signs of the brain is when an arrow falls in it, and passes through the bone, and has come out of the membrane which is over the brain, for it causes by it violent headache, and dizziness, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of understanding and vomiting of bile, and very easily the speech is cut off and the voice is destroyed, and white moisture like dust comes out of the place of the wound, and something like watery flesh comes out of it.
And so if these signs appear to you, then you abstain from the cure and from the extraction of the arrow, if it is not outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls in the heart, are that it is near the left breast, and one feels as if it is fixed in a hard thing, and it is not an empty thing; and very soon the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood runs from the wound, and that follows coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting; and then know that death descends without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are the exit of foamy blood from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are swollen, and the color of the patient is changed, and he exhales great high spirit, and demands attraction of cold air.
And if the arrow falls in the membrane which is in the chest, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and the breath is made great with vehement pain and with sigh, and not all the members of the muscles are moved.
And if the arrow falls in the stomach, then very soon some undigested food comes out of the wound.
And if the arrow falls in the belly, and is fixed in it, some crack comes out of the wound, or the skin, or bowel which is broken, and there is no hope in the cure of extracting the arrow.
And if the arrow falls in the bladder, and urine has come out, and some thing proceeds from it to the outer parts, and very soon there is pain over the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the upper arm, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, and the thigh, and the leg and their similar members, are saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not find vein or artery and is not a poisonous arrow.
And I will recount to you what I have seen of the being of these arrows, so that you signify by this about your cure.
Which thing is, that in a man an arrow fell in the tear duct of his eye in the root of the nose, and I extracted it from him from the other part, below the fat of his ear, and he is healed, and no harm occurred in his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which fell in him in the fat of his eye, below the lower eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and nothing was achieved of it except a small extremity with which it adheres to its shaft, and it was a great arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with lion iron in which there were no ears.
And the Jew is healed, and no bad accident occurred in his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen in the throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and it is that to which there are ears; and then I cut over it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I was subtle with it, and afterwards extracted it, and the Christian is healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen in his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and then, after thirty days had passed and nothing had changed over him of his dispositions, I cut over the arrow and contrived over it, and he is healed, and no bad accident occurred to him.
And I saw more a man in whom an arrow fell in his back, and the wound is consolidated over him, and after it was seven years later, the arrow came out in the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow fell in her belly, and the wound is consolidated inside her, and nothing is changed in her dispositions at all, and the woman found no harm in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow fell in his face and the wound is consolidated and the arrow remained, not finding much pain there, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the man of the princes of the king that had fallen in the middle place of his nose and had already declined to the right side a little, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to treat him after the arrow's fall for up to three days.
And then I found the arrow wound very narrow; and then I probed it with a subtle probe, and did not feel it; but he felt a prick below the right ear, and then I hoped that this prick was from the tip of the arrow.
And I applied a plaster to the place with a plaster in which there is attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the place would become inflamed so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could cut above it.
And no accident occurred in the place indicating that the arrow had reached that place; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no accident occurred; and the wound healed meanwhile, and the patient remained without the extraction of it for the space of many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with the probe I tested it, and found the subtle tip of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And afterwards I enlarged the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared visibly to me, and time passed with this for the space of time up to four months.
And after the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert the forceps into it, and remove it, and it did not yield to exit, then I did not cease to refine and devise upon it with ingenious ways, until I began one day upon it with ways of devices to take it with properly made forceps, according to the form of those that will come at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and afterwards I restored that wound.
And some doctors judged that the cartilage of the nose is not restored, and I restored it and the wound is healed, and the patient is healed with complete health, and it did not harm him in any way.
And I recount to you the quality of extraction of some arrows so that you may set this as a rule and indication about what I have not said, for the parts of this art and its definitions are not comprehended with narration nor does the book comprehend them.
And the wise craftsman compares with little over much; and with what is present over what is absent; and finds new operation and new instrument towards the descent of strange causes of the art when they occur.
And so I say that arrows are not cast out of the members in which they are fixed except according to two ways: either by attraction of the place in which it entered; or by the contrary of the other part.
And that which is cast by the place where it entered, either is an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place, and is drawn and cast out.
And if it does not yield to exit at the time in which it falls, then it is fitting that you leave it for some days until the flesh that is around it rots, and its attraction and its egress is eased.
And similarly if it is fixed in bone and does not yield to you to exit, then leave it for some days and repeat that with attraction and motion each day, and it is already cast out.
And if it does not yield to you exit after some days, then it is fitting that you perforate in the circuit of the arrow in that bone from every part with a subtle auger until the place of the arrow is enlarged, and afterwards extract it.
But if the arrow is already hidden in the body, and is hidden from sensation, then you should probe for it with a probe, and if you feel it, then pull it out with some instruments which are suitable for its extraction.
And if you cannot reach it due to the constriction of the wound and the depth of penetration, and there is no bone or nerve or vein there, then you should cut open the wound until it is widened and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
And if it has barbs with which it is held, then you should free the flesh in which it is lodged from all sides with all the skill you can muster; and if you cannot manage the removal of the flesh, attempt to break or twist the barbs until it yields.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow in whatever place it may be, then you should use a twisting motion of your hand with forceps on all sides until you make it yield; and be extremely careful that the arrow is not broken and that its extraction does not become difficult for you.
And if you cannot manage it at that time, then you should leave it for a few days until the parts around it begin to rot, and then try again for then it is loosened. But if you are faced with the problem of blood flow, then you should administer what we have said about treatment in its chapter.
And guard your skill from cutting a vein or nerve, or cord, and use all possible means to remove the arrow; and do this with ease and tranquility and slowness and firmness as I have described to you.
And therefore make the arrow retract so that the patient is in the same position as when the arrow fell into him, for this is more helpful. But if this is not possible for you, then you should do what is possible for you according to the circumstances.
But for arrows that are ejected from the opposite side, or from which some part already protrudes to the outside, or if the tip of the arrow causes a bump that can be felt just under the nearby skin and you see it protruding; then you should cut above it; and let the cut be according to the size that the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it is made easy to remove.
But if it is retained in the bone, then you should twist the blade in the opposite direction until the arrow makes an impression in the bone, and widens itself, and then extract it.
If not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is still attached, then you should push it out with it.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you want to push it out, then you should insert into it, if it is solid, a hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow and then push it out with it.
But if the arrow is hollow, then you push it with a strong instrument entering into it, because the arrow is lightened by it.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you hollow out all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and afterward cure it with what is appropriate.
And if the arrow falls in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side, and is close so that you touch it with the probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it, then cut above it and be careful of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it, and afterward sew the wound if needed, and cure it until it is healed.
Form of forceps with which arrows are drawn:
Let their extremities be like a bird’s beak that are made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they don't let it go.
And of these are made many types, large, small and medium, according to the quantity of the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and the constriction of it.
like a ray so that the entry of it into the hollow arrow is lightened and it is pushed with that.
On bloodletting of the veins.
The veins for bloodletting from which it is customary to draw blood are 30, of which 16 are in the head, that is two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two herbs that are in the two visible temples; and two veins that are in two tear ducts of two eyes that are called looking; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein that is at the tip of the nose; and two guidegi that are on the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins that are called algelic —; and there are two veins that are under the tongue. But the veins that are bled in the arms are 5; for of these one is cephalic, and is towards the wild side; and the people call it "the vein of the head", and muffled, and it is the middle vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people name it "vein of all the body", and basilic is a vein which is placed on the domestic side, and is named "citillar", and the common people name it "vein of the belly"; and funis of the arm, and it is placed on the forearm, and it is that in which is bled, and it is that which appears on the wrist by manifest appearance; and salvatella, and it is the vein that is between the ring finger and the smallest finger, and there are two branches in it.
And in the foot and the leg there are three veins; one of these is that which is below the curve of the knee on the wild side; and the second is sophena, and its place is below the leg on the domestic side; and the sciatic vein, and its place is below the leg on the wild side; and in the leg are other veins similar to these.
The benefit of the veins that are near the ears is for ancient catarrhs and migraine and sahafati and bad ancient ulcerations of the head.
And the quality of bleeding those is as I tell you: the patient's head should be shaved, and the patient's head should be rubbed at the occiput in the place of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; afterwards, the patient should tighten their neck with a band until both appear and their places are after the ears in two depressed places of the head; and seek that with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those with your finger, mark there with ink.
Afterwards, take the knife-like lance, which is the cutting one, and then insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone. And then raise your hand with the vein and the skin to the upper parts and cut the vein with an incising cut. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two joined fingers or thereabouts; and let out as much blood as you want.
And afterwards, tighten with compresses and leave it until it is healed.
And I have already preceded in the beginning of the book the incision of those and their cauterization.
But of the two arterial veins that are at the temples, the benefit of bloodletting is for ancient migraine and sudden evil and persistent eye inflammation and flow of acute superfluities poured into the eyes.
And the quality of bleeding those is as I tell you: let the patient tighten their neck very much until the two veins appear to the touch by clear appearance and let their pulse be demonstrated under your finger; and then you mark with ink.
And afterwards, you raise the skin above the vein to the upper parts with your index finger, and insert the cutting lancet underneath, and raise the vein upwards; and cut it as you have done in the other two veins; and let the blood run according to the quantity of your need.
And afterwards, untie the patient's constriction, and place your finger over the vein for an hour, and afterwards place cotton and compresses on it and tighten it above with strong constriction, and leave it until it is healed.
And the remembrance of them has already preceded, and of those the incision, and the attraction at the beginning of the book.
And so the benefit of bloodletting from the vein of the forehead is after the bloodletting of the cephalic for ancient diseases of the face and for migraine and redness and fetid ulcerations.
And the quality of the bloodletting of it is as I tell you; that is, that the patient tightens their neck with a band until the vein appears; and afterwards take the instrument which is called a fossori of which this is its form:
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the fossori on that vein and strike it with a comb or with another thing in its manner, and let the blood run according to the quantity which you want; and afterwards untie the patient's perforation and tighten it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide lancet, however it is not suitable that the lancet be of sharp extremity as the other lancets, rather it should be of wide extremity, and bleed it according to the transverse, because the bone is near and the lancet would very easily break in it when it is thin.
But the benefit of the two veins that are at the tear ducts of both eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for redness and onion in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the quality of the bloodletting is that the patient tightens their neck with a band, and afterwards bleed those, and you should be standing over their head.
And let the bloodletting be done according to the transverse to a little length with a small lancet, because in the place there is no flesh; and if the lancet is thin, its extremity is very easily broken.
And afterwards let the blood run as much as is necessary for you, and place cotton on those, and tighten those for one night, and afterwards untie those.
But the bloodletting from the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and severe headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and redness that occurs in the face, and especially when it is chronic.
And the method of bloodletting from it is that the patient tightens his neck and then you hold his nose with your left hand, and take the long, thin lancet and insert it in the middle of the tip of the nose in a straight line, for the vein does not appear to the touch there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
It is necessary that you guide your hand with the lancet little by little and make the blood flow according to your need; and afterwards bind it for a night, for it is easily restored.
But the benefit of bloodletting from the two guidegui [possibly jugular veins] is for constriction of breath and for the beginning of leprosy and for many diseases and impetigo and bad corrosive ulcerations such as herpotromeno [possibly herpes].
And the method of bloodletting from these is that the patient tightens below each one on his neck with a binding; and the practitioner stands over the head of the patient, and the patient is sitting on a chair.
And then bleed the vein lengthwise with a wide lancet little by little; and then extract blood according to a moderate quantity or according to what you see is necessary for this; and then do the same for the other vein.
And then untie the binding and tighten the two veins with moderate pressure so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave until morning for the wound is healed.
And the benefit of bloodletting from the algerich veins [possibly sublingual veins] is beneficial for small abscesses and pustules of the mouth and corruption of the gums and bad ulcerations and fissures, and for bad ulcerations that are in the nose and in its surroundings.
And the method of bloodletting from these is that you make the patient sit before you, and tighten his neck with a band, and then roll back his lips and look at two veins of which one is on the right of the lip and the second on the left; and the indication of each is by the blackness of each of both, and it is more so because in their surroundings are small black veins; and then incise each with an incising incision.
But if there is doubt under you, and you don't know which of these it is, then you intend to incise the larger of these, and the more visible of these.
And do the same for the two veins that are in the upper lips.
But of two veins that are under the tongue, the benefit is after the bloodletting of the cephalic for suffocations that are in the throat, for the disease of the uvula, and for diseases of the mouth.
And the method of bloodletting these is that the patient follows between your hands before the sun, and elevate his tongue, and look under the tongue, on one side of it a vein and on the other side a vein, and the color of each is tending to blackness.
And then bleed each; and be careful that you don't extend your hand in bleeding these, for under these are arteries.
And your veins that are bled in the elbow are those in which custom runs in all men, and their bloodletting is done according to two ways, either puncturing with the myrtle-shaped or olive-shaped lancet tending to subtlety; or cutting with a knife-like lancet, and it is called alnessil, and this is its form:
let its side be as you see, and it is suitable to open the hollow full veins proceeding apparent thick, and for those that contain thick turbid blood.
And this lancet is of lesser width and of more subtle extremity, and is suitable for bloodletting of subtle veins that contain thin citrine blood.
But the benefit of bloodletting from the basilic vein which is one of three veins: for it extracts the blood from the diseases that are below the throat and neck, and from what follows the chest and the belly.
And it is advised for the phlebotomist, when performing phlebotomy on it, to watch it carefully and be very cautious with it, because below it there is an artery, and if he errs and hits it with the puncture of the lancet, he will cut that artery and cause a flow of blood, so it is better not to perform phlebotomy on it with a puncturing lancet, but rather with a cutting one like an alnessil.
And if the basilic vein does not appear clearly visible, then it is better that you leave it and move to the other one, or that you look for one of its branches; or that you perform phlebotomy at the end of the arm, because that one is visible; and cut it with an alnessil lancet as we have said.
But if you want to perform phlebotomy on the same one, then it is necessary before tightening the arm to touch the area until you recognize the location of the pulse, and then mark it with ink; and after that tie the arm and cut the vein with a concave cut using an alnessil lancet as we have said; and be careful that the puncture falls some distance from the location of the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, a swelling in the place that you have marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, and therefore avoid it.
And if you see the blood flowing in the phlebotomy like the urine of a child, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is blood from the artery; and then hurry and place your finger over it for a long time; and after removing your finger, if the blood has stopped, because many times it stops, then you tighten the arm and leave it.
And guard the patient from moving it, and be very cautious with it and do not move it for some days until it is healed.
But if the blood does not stop, and you see that you do not have medicine present for this, then you cut the artery, if it appears to you; because its extremity is contracted, and the blood is stopped; or take bark of pistachio, and split it, and take one half and tighten it over the location of the vein with a descending tightness with a bandage and with compresses until the next day.
And then if the blood has stopped, it is good; and if not, then treat it with that which has been mentioned before in the section on powders that stop the flow of blood.
And the stopping of blood from it is difficult in many situations because of the small size of the wound and the possibility of tying the arm.
But for the dark vein, the phlebotomy is beneficial as it extracts blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we have said.
And it is necessary for the one performing phlebotomy on it to be very cautious in the phlebotomy of it, because below it there is a nerve; and if he hits it with the puncture of the lancet and finds the nerve, it will cause numbness, and its healing is difficult, and very often it is not healed completely, And this nerve many times appears to the touch; and if it is hidden to some men and is subtle and not shown, then it is necessary that you place the lancet with which you perform phlebotomy with an alnessil cut, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
And if the vein is between two nerves, then you split the vein lengthwise.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and is beneficial for diseases of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this specifically, if you want to perform phlebotomy by puncturing with an olive-shaped lancet or with a myrtle-shaped lancet; because it is the safest of all the veins, as there is no artery or nerve below it.
However, it is very easily infected in some men, when it is not bled in the first strike, but that infection does not harm anything.
The first of them is that you should know that bleeding is not administered except for the preservation of health and for the hope of it and caution of the occurrence of diseases, and that bleeding should be done in one of the veins that are in the elbow, that is: cephalic, fuscous and basilic; and that bleeding should be done at the beginning of spring, on an empty stomach in spring and autumn, and in winter after eating, when signs of repletion appear.
And bleeding should be done on the third day or on the first after three hours have passed from the day.
But bleeding that is administered, but when it provokes convenience and necessity for this, either by night or by day, at all hours and at all times.
And it is appropriate that a child should not be bled until he is 14 years old, and a woman who is pregnant should not be bled except from the fourth month until the seventh, and the old should not be bled who have already passed 60 years.
And when someone wants to be bled in whatever manner, it is appropriate that he clean his bowels before the bleeding with a gentle enema, if there is much retained waste in them, so that the veins do not draw the superfluities in the bleeding from the bowels, which are putrid, and that they do not harm the principal members.
And patients with nausea and indigestion should not be bled, nor those recovering from illness or drunkenness until that is removed from them, nor those sick with dropsy, and moreover bleeding should be feared after colic passion and after vomiting, and after bowel movement, and after multiplication of coitus and of work and of exercise of fasting or of vigils and of any cause that dissolves the virtue, whether it be corporal or animal.
And afterwards consider in the thinning of humors before this if the blood is thick, with foods and drinks, and with medicines if this is possible for him to do.
And afterwards enter into a bath if nothing prohibits it, or be exercised by some exercise so that the blood may be thinned.
And place his bleeding in the best part of the day, that is around the hour of terce, as we have said.
And strive that his chest be guarded on that day from all evil animal accidents, such as anguish, anger, fear, and from all corporal accidents, such as excessive work and pain and coitus and similar to them.
And at his session let there be present the things in the use of which runs the custom of men with aromaticity and odor and with balsams and similar to those according to the quantity of possibility.
And let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion that is higher than the cushion of the one who is to be bled.
And afterwards let him extend his arm, and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand two or three times; and afterwards tighten the binding with a strap and wrap it twice.
And let the tightness be tempered, for when the tightness is not equal, or the tightness is with superfluity, then it prohibits the flow of blood; or it is with softening, and then it prohibits the course of blood likewise.
And after the tightening, it is appropriate that the one who is to be bled rub his hands together between them until the veins are swollen, and are declared to the feeling.
And afterwards let the phlebotomist anoint his lancet with a little old oil properly; and afterwards place his index finger of the left hand on that vein, below the place from which he wants the bleeding, so that the vein may flee a little, and the percussion may be ceased for of the veins is that which you find thus as a cord fleeing towards the bleeding, and of those it is that is full of windiness and when you place the lancet on that it is contracted towards the lancet, and the phlebotomist is deceived; and the lancet does not open the vein, and if it opens, that is narrow.
And so, for this reason it is appropriate that the phlebotomist be steady and be careful in all these things.
And after that, perform the phlebotomy, but if the vein is opened in one go, then it is good; and if not, you repeat this again a little below that place, or above it with haste, if the place is not swollen.
And if the place is swollen, or the patient is frightened, then you leave it for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage, because it will quickly attract hot swelling, nor should he enter a bath.
And afterwards repeat the phlebotomy if it is necessary.
And if the opening is small and blood flows thinly, and you fear that the amount of blood you want will not come out, then you repeat the phlebotomy in that hole in the same direction and enlarge the opening a little, and do this quickly before the place becomes swollen, because the place becomes swollen in many men from a small opening.
But if you see it already swollen, then do not repeat it there, because nothing excuses you; and put on it some part of the oil compress, because it eases the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than oil and other oils.
And similarly administer an oil compress in every phlebotomy with which you bleed the veins to stop the flow of blood, and they do this with theriac alfadoc and sagzenea when one of these is placed on the spot, because the blood is thinned and is revolved when it is thick.
And if in the place of the phlebotomy a large swelling is formed, because it often happens, and especially to one who has not been bled except that time, or in the small opening of the vein, and then you hurry, and put on the place a sponge soaked in water and salt, a little heated, and tighten it for an hour, because it is dissolved.
And it is appropriate to do this after the blood comes out of that vein completely or from another vein.
And if some blackness or greenness remains in the place for some days, it does not harm; and if you want, put on it something of aloe and myrrh dissolved, or something of the juice of wild mint or calamint or similar to it.
And often swellings and protrusions occur from phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And so put your hand on it, and if then you find softness from your compression on it, then that protrusion is malignant and bad; and then be careful that you put on it any of what we have said, because blood will flow very quickly from the artery.
And make a plaster with something that has astringency, so that the place becomes hardened, and afterwards treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
And it is appropriate that you extract blood from every man according to the quantity of his strength and according to the color prevailing in the blood as it appears.
Because if the blood is black, then you let it come out until it is red; and similarly if you see it thick, then you let it come out until it is thinned; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is appropriate for one who is full and strong and needs blood extraction in one go, that the phlebotomy of the vein be enlarged and the phlebotomist be wide.
And in one who is weak, the opposite of that should be done, and it is appropriate that he extract that in many times, and that the hole be narrow.
And the best that is administered in phlebotomy of a vein is that the cut be transverse, not a puncture.
And this manner of phlebotomy is safe from blood flow and nerve incision, and is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy according to width and length.
And for the one who is accustomed to fainting from phlebotomy, it is appropriate that you treat him before the phlebotomy with a little bread soaked in pomegranate wine or simple oxymel heated; and extract blood from him in three or four times.
And if there is a coldness of complexion, then take before the bloodletting bread soaked in myrrh syrup or in honey syrup flavored with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger and similar, or in aromatic and very fragrant syrup.
And if fainting occurs during the bloodletting, and it is caused by too much blood loss, then it is advisable to drink meat broth and very fragrant, subtle syrup; and use aromatics with algalia, and anoint his chest with it.
And use the other things that we have mentioned in the division in the chapter on fainting due to evacuation.
And who wants to ventilate his arm and dissolve his blood a second time, it is advisable for him whose bloodletting is for much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, to evacuate the blood little by little, that is according to the quantity of strength in the continuous days.
But who wants to ventilate his arm, and remove his blood a second time, and his body is strong, then do this after 7 or 9 hours from the first bloodletting.
But who wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite of the part to which it inclines, it is advisable that it be ventilated on the second or third day.
But that body in which there is much blood already heated and is acute and causes fever to occur, it is advisable that blood be removed from it in one go and a large quantity be removed from it and the opening be widened until fainting occurs, after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and that you place your hand on his pulse towards the body of blood, so that death does not occur instead of fainting, for this often happens when the bloodletter is ignorant and negligent.
And it is not advisable when you want to untie the arm and evacuate the blood a second time, when the orifice of the vein is already closed and the exit of blood has become difficult, that you press on it with vehemence and bend it to be extended lightly, for this is very bad.
Rather, leave it until it is bled secondarily; or remove that with the sharpness of the lancet that is coagulated from the blood in the orifice of the vein; or place on it some theriac alfarach, or sagzenea, and compress it with light compression until the blood has come out.
And if the vein is swollen, then leave it and do not touch it until the swelling has subsided.
And if necessity compels you to evacuate the blood secondarily, and there is no excuse, then you bleed him above that place or in the other arm in another vein.
And the vein called "rope of the arm" is bled in place of the dark one or the basilic, when they are not found or are hidden, for it is composed of those.
And the quality of its bloodletting is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly.
And afterwards it should be tightened above it with a binding with medium stricture; and afterwards bleed according to the small transverse, not latitudinally nor according to the length.
And let the opening be wide and let your bloodletting with which you bleed it be above the joint of the hand a little.
And if the flow of blood is prohibited, then you repeat the hand in the hot water and let the blood run in the water until you achieve your necessity.
But if you were in the summer days, then you are excused from the repetition of the hand in hot water, and the month is set the course of blood in hot water in winter time.
And the bloodletting of this vein is safer than all the other veins, for below it there is no pulsatile vein, nor nerve.
And the bloodletting of the cephalic vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver diseases.
And the quality of its bloodletting is that you tighten the hand with a binding or with your hand, after you have put it in hot water, until the vein is swollen and is well demonstrated to sight; and afterwards bleed it transversely; and if you cut it completely it will not harm anything.
And be careful not to open your hand with the phlebotomy, because below it are the nerves of the fingers exposed without flesh.
And afterwards put the hand in warm water and let the blood flow into it, because if you don't put it in warm water, the blood will coagulate in the orifice of the vein, and the flow is blocked.
And when the blood is extracted according to the necessary quantity, then you put oil and salt on the vein, so that it doesn't heal too quickly; and similarly you should do this for any narrow branch.
But the benefit of phlebotomy on the left hand is that it is helpful for diseases of the spleen.
And do similarly in the phlebotomy of it as you have done in the other.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is for diseases that are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb, and for retention of menses, and for diseases of the kidneys and old ulcers of the thighs and legs, and for similar diseases.
And the method of phlebotomy of it is that the patient puts his foot in warm water and rubs it until the veins appear well; and tie above the joint of the foot with a strap; and the place of the vein is the apparent heel towards the thumb, and from it are branched many branches on the face of the foot.
Therefore, perform phlebotomy on the widest branch or below the leg towards the heel towards its aggregation, because it is better and safer.
And if you perform phlebotomy on it on the face of the foot, then be careful of the nerves that are below it on the face of the foot, and place your phlebotomy with which you perform phlebotomy across, as if you wanted to cut it.
And let the phlebotomy be careful.
But if the blood flow is blocked; then you put his foot back in the warm water and let the blood run into it until it is evacuated.
And if the person performing phlebotomy errs on the first try, then repeat with the phlebotomy a little higher up, because the place is safe; and don't fear harming it, but be careful of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly do the same for the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
And for the sciatic vein, the place as we have said is towards the heel below the ankle on the outer side.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of it is for hip pain when it is due to hot blood.
And the method of phlebotomy of it is that the patient enters a bath, and hurries and tightens his thigh on the side of the hip down to above the heel above four fingers with a long thin bandage, because it doesn't appear except by this.
And so when it appears, then you perform phlebotomy on it according to each method that is possible for you and across, and it is better; or cut it by incision or section it by section, because its location is safe.
And it is very hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it doesn't appear to the touch at all, then perform phlebotomy on some branch of it, and it is the one that appears on the back of the foot towards the little finger and the ring finger.
And be careful of the nerves, and let the quantity of blood you want flow.
And afterwards untie the stricture and place cotton on the site of the phlebotomy and tighten the place, because it heals easily.
On ventilation and the qualities of using them.
Ventouses are made of horns, or brass, or glass.
And ventilation is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is ventilation without scarification.
And this ventilation without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for the ventouses that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood, there are fourteen places on the body, of which one where ventouses are placed is the occiput, and it is the back part of the head; and the middle place of it on both sides of the neck together; and ventouses of the chin below the jaw lower than the mouth; and ventouses of two shoulder blades; and ventouses of the coccyx, which is above the end of the tail; and ventouses of two forearms, which are of two arms; and ventouses of two ankles; and ventouses of two thighs.
And ventilation does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for this reason virtue does not fall through them, as it does through phlebotomy; nor should ventilation be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for ventilation due to illness or continuity, we administer it at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and at whatever time it may be.
And so when you see any of these things, and especially if it is in the third or middle of the month, then we command ventilation to be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of ventilation of the nape is that when it helps with heaviness in the head and with what is poured into the eyes; but it should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this ventouse is in place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not allowed to use it on one who is of cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh, for it gives them great harm.
And for this reason it is not suitable for the elderly to use it, and in those whose heads have cold illnesses.
But for one who continues it over time, forgetfulness is caused; and for this reason you should command the ventilator to place his hand a little with the ventouse to the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
But ventilation of the back is in place of phlebotomy of the dark vein and the basilic vein: and for this reason it helps with asthma, and with constriction of breath and with tearing of the breathing instrument, and with cough, and with repletion.
And the ventouse should be pushed a little on the back, for if it is placed below, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And the ventilation of the temples helps with pains occurring in the head and with eye inflammation, and migraine, and suffocation, and with pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
It is advisable to instruct the cupper not to press his hand with scarification so as not to cut the artery and cause blood flow.
And cupping below the chin helps with throat ailments in the mouth, and with corruption of the gums, and similar diseases that are in the mouth, and stands in place of phlebotomy of the lips that are in the two lips.
But the cupping that is done on the two shoulders helps with headaches, which is caused by excess heat.
And the cupping of the two inner parts of the arms helps in place of phlebotomy of three veins, that is the basilic, median, and cephalic, because it draws blood from all those smaller veins that are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too much with scarification, because the place is devoid of flesh, and below it are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cup with which one cups over the bones and it helps with hemorrhoids of the anus and ulcerations below.
And it is advisable that you instruct the cupper to use a large cup, and that it be made of copper, because the place needs strong suction; and very light cups are easily pierced and should be scarified with scarification.
But cups on the legs reduce by manifest reduction, because they draw blood from the whole body, and help with old pains of the kidneys and of the womb, and of the bladder; and provoke menstruation, and help with boils and swellings; and stand in place of phlebotomy of two saphenous veins, and the sciatic nerve, and of two ankles.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause fainting in many men.
And of the cups on the sciatic nerve, the benefit is close to the benefit of the cups on the two legs.
And the quality of the positioning of the cups is that you place the cup at the beginning empty and suck with moderate suction, and do not prolong the positioning of the cups, but place them lightly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place by equal arrival. And do not cease to repeat this and continue until you see the place already reddening and swelling and the redness of the blood appears.
And then you scarify and repeat the suction little by little, and afterwards consider the disposition of the body.
For that man who is of tender flesh with rare pores, it is advisable that you scarify him once and no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to amplify the scarification and deepen a little, and temper the suction with ease.
And if in the blood there is thickness, then it is advisable to scarify twice, because in the first time the way of subtlety to the blood and its wateriness is opened. But in the second to the acquired scrutiny of the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very feculent, then scarification should be done a third time to achieve the last.
And lastly, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we scarify with much scarification; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is advisable that we scarify with deep scarification.
And the term of scarification in which is the depth of the skin only.
And if the one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is advisable that the places of the cups be anointed with lenitive, aperient, resolvent ointments; if it is in summer time, such as aloe oil or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil; and if it is in winter time, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or blood oil, and those similar to them.
And if the superfluity is thick, cold, then the oil should be amaranth, or costmary oil, or aloe oil, or dill oil, and similar to them.
But if the cupping area has wide pores of tender flesh, then it should be prohibited from oil, and it is necessary that for this the cupping sites be washed with rosewater or with cold water of nightshade, or with water of gourd, or with water of purslane, and similar to these.
And for the one whose blood has much humidity, wash their cupping sites with vinegar, or with water of myrtle and sumac and similar to those.
And for the one whose superfluities are thick, wash their cupping sites with old wine, or with water of marjoram, which is majorana, or with a decoction of dill, or of chamomile, and similar to those.
And it is necessary that the cupping be guarded before bathing; it is better that it be administered after leaving the bath for an hour or two, and it is necessary to sleep after the cupping.
What is it that one must govern the cupped or bled person before cupping.
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: then if the cupped one or the one who must be bled is choleric and wind overcomes the acute blood and inflammation, then it is necessary that they take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, and endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secanabin, and julep, and similar to them.
And give them foods such as chickens, and meats of wild birds, and foods seasoned with verjuice, and similar to them.
And for the one whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that they drink syrup of almonds, or syrup of alsecanabin seeds, and sumac, and very fragrant wine between old and new; and that they be commanded to use little drink and food, and give them foods of chicken and hemp and fish and small birds and young pigeons.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bleeding, the drink be more than the food.
And it is necessary that at certain times some men be given to drink theriac alpharac, or musk medicine, that is nutmeg, or alcelita before cupping or before bleeding or after, so that the principal members are comforted and the blood is thinned; and it is not necessary that they take this heated.
But the cups that are without scarification are cups that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the knees, and the hip joint, for these members do not suffer scarification on themselves.
And it is not sought by these except the attraction of blood from a member, as we place a cup on the breast in illness of blood flux from the nostrils; and we use it to dissolve cold viscosity from the member, as we place on the belly and navel; for that rarefies the member and warms it and removes the pain because it resolves windiness.
And it has already been placed on the kidneys when obstruction or stone occurs in them, for by virtue of strong attraction it soon opens obstruction and eradicates the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when it is placed on the liver and spleen towards windiness enclosed in them.
And these cups are administered empty with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cup may be filled and be larger, that is hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this have been cooked.
Form of cup administered with fire: let the width of the orifice be two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and the quantity of the depth be half a palm; and let there be on its side, in the place that is in the circuit, half of it, let the hole be small according to the quantity that enters in it a needle.
Let it be made of red or citrine copper, with thick margins, smooth, equal and polished so that it does not harm the member towards its position on it.
And there should be in the middle of it a transverse rod of copper or iron, where a candle with fire is placed.
And although this cupping glass is much larger than the one we have described; and the small one is smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for children and the emaciated are different from those for young people and those with robust bodies.
And the method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend the wick with wood fire decently before a small wax candle, and place it on the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the lower part so that the body of the patient is not burned.
And after it is placed on the limb of the patient, and the finger is on the hole we have mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses and we do not need to remove the finger and let the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass will be quickly dissolved.
And then ignite the wick according to its manner, and repeat it if you need it.
But in a cupping glass that is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod or hole, and is not administered unless it is filled with water, and is placed on the limb only.
And this is its shape: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better, so that it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered on many limbs except on those limbs where the placement of cupping glasses is not possible, due to their small size, such as the lip and gum and similar to them; or on a limb stripped of flesh, such as the finger and the nose and similar to them.
And the method of using them is that if you have found leeches that are in fresh waters clean of putrefaction.
And afterwards they should be left for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies; and afterwards the body should be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and with cupping glasses; and afterwards rub the limb of the patient until it reddens; and afterwards they should be placed on it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if suction of the place with a cupping glass is possible, it is more beneficial afterwards.
And if not, then wash the place with vinegar and afterwards with much water; and it should be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged after the falling of the leeches, and this is oozing, then you soak a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top until the oozing is stopped.
And if the blood is multiplied, then sprinkle crushed vitriol on it, or galls and similar astringent substances, until the blood is stopped; or place halves of peeled beans on the place, and leave them until the beans adhere to the place, and the blood is stopped.
And it is appropriate, if necessary, to repeat the leeches, when it is possible to change them.
And if the leeches are prevented from attaching, then rub the place with fresh blood or prick a needle in the places until blood comes out; and afterwards place them, for when they feel any blood they quickly attach.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little crushed aloe on them, or salt, or ash, and they fall off immediately.
Here begins the prologue of the third part: on restoration. This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine; it is about the restoration of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Oh, son, know that foolish doctors and common people boast about this chapter, and those who have never looked at the books of the Ancients nor read a letter from them.
And for this reason, this manner of knowledge in our land is private; and I have not forgotten anything about it at any time; and I have not added what I learned, except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study of their understanding, until I extracted their knowledge.
And afterwards I directed myself to experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and on which my experiment has passed; since I had abbreviated it for you others and delivered it from the occupation of elongation; and I abbreviated it in the end of abbreviation; and I showed it in the end of declaration; and I formed in it many forms of instruments that are administered in it, as is done by addition of declaration, as I have done in two preceding chapters.
First Chapter.
Summaries and collections from the essence of bone fractures, from those whose introduction is necessary, before we begin the recollection of broken limbs and the different ones if of each one. It is appropriate then that we say in the preface of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters to the understanding of which at the beginning the craftsman is forced and to station over the truth of those; that is you and those who are ready to know or learn this noble art from you.
And so I say that when someone suffers a fracture or dislocation or sprain or fall, then it is appropriate that you hasten at the beginning with bloodletting or to relieve him, or both together, if nothing prohibits it, such as weakness of virtue, or if the one to whom it happens is a child, or old in decrepitude, or it is a time of vehement heat, or of vehement cold.
And afterwards be content in his food with cold herbs and meats of birds and goats; and we prohibit fatty meats and filling oneself with foods and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are assured against hot apostema, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then let the patient return to his first regimen with which he usually runs.
And so when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is appropriate that the patient be fed with nutritious foods of much and thick and heavy nourishment in which there is viscosity, such as foods made of wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and cow bellies, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar to those, and with this regimen the coagulation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that broken bones when they are in men who are in the first old age and in the decrepit, it is not possible that they be continued and be consolidated according to their nature because of the drying out of their limbs.
But however, nature adds and connects over the broken bone from every part something similar to glue in which there is thickness that adheres and tightens it until they are together conglutinated and bound together, until they come to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at the beginning, until nothing prohibits it from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that you set aside the foods of the sick, foods in which there is heaviness and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone; and similarly all the other limbs are different from each other.
And I come with remembrance of every type of fracture, explained in its distinct chapter from the others.
Many different types of fractures in themselves. And the types of bone fractures are diversified in themselves; for an arched fracture is made beyond what happens in the stars; and sometimes a fracture is made along the length of the bone: and these are fractures of splinters and chips and protrusions proceeding and not proceeding, as is done in the pen when it is prepared to write, and it is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and it is a small fissure fracture.
And for every type there is a proper device or treatment in restoration of them according to what will come to memory of them distinctly in their places.
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: it is the tortuosity of it, and its prominence and appearance to the touch, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
And so when there is no apparent tortuosity in the place nor is sound made towards your touch when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture there.
Rather it is possible that there is tortuosity or a small fissure, then it is not appropriate that you move it with your hand and with compression of all; rather place on it the medicines of which remembrance comes after that moment which is suitable for the place; and afterwards let it be tightened by subtle tightening.
And know that when the place is arched and is not split in two or beyond what happens in splinters, when every part is removed from its companion, then it is appropriate that you begin from your hour to rectify it before a hot swelling occurs to it.
And if a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for some days until the swelling ceases; and afterwards straighten it according to each manner that you can with lightness and skill.
And know that its restoration and joining is easier than of the bone in which splinters occur.
And afterwards tighten it according to what will come to memory of it.
But if there are splinters in the bone, then extension of this broken limb is necessary from two parts, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two ropes or with one rope and the hand.
And let your position with which you place the limb be an equal place according to its natural figure, while the parts of the broken limb are extended, then strive to make those protrusions return with skill and according to each manner that you can to their places.
And guard yourself according to your strength that pain or injury does not occur to the patient by your operation; and strive according to your power that you apply one bone with its companion according to their best forms.
And it is appropriate that in that hour you profitably touch it with your hand, and if you see anything diverse there, rectify it and straighten it according to the quantity of your virtue.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish doctors do who many times cause hot swelling to occur by their operation, or cause contraction to occur in the limb as we have seen this by their operation many times.
And the quality of the tightening of the broken limb is that which I recount; so that you know that broken limbs are diverse in their smallness and in their greatness and in their form.
And of those that are small such as the arm and the finger and the forearm and similar to those, then it is necessary that the wrapping be of soft, thin cloth.
But those that are large such as the thigh or the leg, it is necessary that for those there be a wide and hard wrapping, because the wide binding connects the large limb and tightens it from all sides with equal tightness, so that no blemish enters it.
And after beginning, after your evacuation of equation, place a binding suitable for this, over the place of that fracture with three or four wrappings according to what the limb deserves; and tighten your hand a little with the binding.
And after go with that to the uppermost part of the fracture site, and tighten less than your tightening with which you have tightened the fracture site. And after elongate yourself with wrapping from the fracture site a little, and soften the tightness little by little, until you take a large part from the healthy site.
And after take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with wrapping, and after go with the wrappings to the lower part of the fracture; and let your operation in the tightening of the wrapping and in softening of it be according to what we have said in the first wrapping above.
And after place between the windings of tow lion or cloth with which the tortuosity of the fracture is evened out, if there is tortuosity in it; and if not, do not place anything in it.
And after place another bandage over it, and after even out over these wrappings stars decently made immediately, if there is no swelling or hot abscess in the place, and then place over it that which calms that abscess and removes the swelling; and leave that for some days; and then you tighten the stars over it.
And let the stars be of the half of a wide, smooth cane, and well formed wisely; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each star be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over that fracture be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to what is allowed to the limb in magnitude and smallness; and after tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and after tie above with thread decently according to what we have said about tightening, and it is that your tightening should be according to the fracture site more, and the further it is elongated from the fracture, the lesser the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be medium in thickness and thinness, and be of soft linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of some fools placing their threads of twisted linen ropes, and it is a great error, when the tightening falls outside equality.
And likewise thin threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve by tightening with those what you want, and it is not appropriate that between splint and splint there be less than one finger.
And if the patient is injured in the extremities of the splints near the constriction in healthy places, then place beneath them oakum or carded wool until it does not cause him injury.
Sometimes a fracture is with a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, and I will come with a reminder of that in the following chapter specifically.
And know that not every broken limb is tightened with splints on the first day; which is because the limb, when it is large, should not be placed on splints except after 5 days or 7 or more, according to your certainty of the onset of hot swelling.
And this is the manner of plasters with which the Ancients restored, which are placed on fractures and dislocations and sprains.
Method of common abridged plaster for restoring fractures, suitable for many complexions and especially for women and children, for it is of that upon which neither heat nor cold prevails: and it is that you take mill powder, and it is the core of the flour that hangs towards the movement of the millstone on the walls of the mill.
And prepare it as it is, except that you sieve it with egg white, and place the preparation of it neither thick nor thin; and afterwards use it.
Another method of plaster restoring fracture and dislocation and sprain: take asafoetida, labdanum, acacia, elecampane, and migrach, which is wild pomegranate root, of all 10 drachms each; of myrrh and aloe 5 drachms each; alochil 20 drachms; let all be ground and sieved and mixed with alochil water or with egg white if the patient's complexion is heated; and afterwards use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores broken bones easily, and is beneficial to many men.
Another method of plaster which is beneficial for fracture and sprain: take migrach and put white marshmallow 10 drachms each; of myrrh and aloe 5 drachms each; of acacia 6 drachms; of Armenian bole 20 drachms.
Let all be ground in a mortar with much crushing, and be sieved, and be prepared with water or egg white and be administered.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and it soothes pain occurring in them and for fissures and fractures: let unwashed wool be taken and be submerged in vinegar and cooked oil and be placed on the spot.
But in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, of hot swelling and it expels pains specifically.
Another method of plaster that restores broken bone: take leaves of deaf fig and leaves of wild poppy; crush all and make a plaster of them.
Method of abridged plaster administered for the restoration of bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remnant of swelling: take roots of marshmallow, of chamomile and violet flowers, of vetch flour, of all 1 part each; let all be crushed and be prepared with wine, if the limb is not heated.
But if it is heated, then prepare it with water of moist coriander or with water and use it.
Another manner of plaster stronger in resolution than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs near the restoration of the limb: take root of marshmallow and flaxseed, and pomegranate flower, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of all equal parts; let all be ground and mixed with willow water or fresh water or wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the rest of its color.
But Hippocrates did not say in his book that anything should be placed on the broken limb for its restoration except a ceruse made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recounted that it should be between thickness and subtlety.
But in Gaul it is seen that they place on the broken limb for its restoration things in which there is drying with a little heat such as myrrh, incense and similar things to those that we have recounted.
Manner of plaster that confers to lesion and pain: take crumb, chickpeas, cut human hair or bird feather, and marshmallow, and salt, equal parts; let them be ground and sifted and mixed, and let a plaster be made.
But the quantity according to which it is convenient that the bandage remain and afterwards be untied, is that you consider if there is no pain or itching and the broken bone has not moved from its place, then you do not untie for many days. And if there occurs at the place vehement pain or itching or swelling, then you hasten or untie that at the hour, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and soft wet sea sponge, and submerge that in lukewarm water, and with that wash the place until that itching ceases and the pain is sedated; and afterwards let the limb rest for an hour; and afterwards place on it unwashed wool submerged in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bind it for a night until you are sure of hot abscess, and that the swelling of the limb rests, and its pain is removed.
And afterwards repeat that subtle constriction and small plaster and do not tighten it with your first constriction, and refine with that until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess and the redness and the pain and the swelling and all the accidents are already removed completely, and you need plaster and constriction, then you repeat that as you have done at the beginning equally.
And if there does not occur to the limb any of those things that we have said, then do not untie that except after three days or four or five or seven; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days according to what appears to you by the untying of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh over it.
And then increase the constriction more than in your first entire constriction, and increase in the thickening of the patient's food, according to what is preceded by the remembrance of it.
But if you see the place of the fracture already drying and thinning more than is convenient, then know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching it; and then bathe that with lukewarm water every time that you untie it; and let this be in all three days; and lighten the constriction a little, and by this operation nourishment runs to the limb, and it is healed easily.
But what the foolish restorers of the fracture of the limb do another time when it is not restored at the beginning, as is convenient, and is restored according to tortuosity, is error according to their operation, and great fear, and if it were righteous this the Ancients would have enlarged in their books and would have worked with this.
And I have not seen any of them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable that this not be done.
Chapter Two: On fracture when it occurs in the head.
There are many types of fractures, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are made by the blow of a sword, and the blow removes all the bone down to the membrane, which is below the bone, as an axe does in wood, and for this reason this type is called axial; or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts the flesh of it but does not pass through to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small, and of fracture that is crushing or bruising; and its cause is impact with a stone or falling on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating that already approaches the membrane which is below the bone; or it is on the face of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And of fracture there is one that is diminished in the bone, in the fineness of a small hair, and for this reason this type is called capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture that is made either by falling or by impact of stone and similar things, which causes some layer of the bone to enter the inner parts, and a concavity is made in the place as happens to copper cauldrons when they suffer an impact and part of them is pushed inwards.
And this mainly happens in bodies with moist bones, such as the heads of infants.
And to all these types of fractures are attached fragments, that is pieces like splinters separated. And I will come with a reminder of all this in its place.
And we know all these types of fracture by detection upon them and by investigation of them with probes and removal of corrupted flesh that is over them.
And the capillary type is known by this that is uncovered over the bone, and is cleaned, and smeared over it with ink, for the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of fracture consider at the beginning the symptoms of the patient, and then if you see by the symptoms that indicate by clear indication about fear, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes and redness of them, and similar to them of the symptoms in the major dispositions, without doubt he is not healed.
And if you see non-recurring symptoms you hope for health, then you begin the cure of him.
Which thing is that if the wounded has come to you at the beginning when he is wounded and this is in the days of winter, then it is fitting that you strive in the removal of the bone before the 14th day according to every disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is fitting that you hasten to remove the bone before the 7th day, before that which is below the bone is corrupted from the membrane, and that the symptoms occur that we have recounted.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain, and is with minute parts and bruising, then it is fitting that you cut the comminuted and bruised part, according to what I will recount to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the wounded patient, and uncover over the bone according to custom and manner possible to you according to the shape of the wound, and that it be light on the patient.
And if it happens to you towards the detection of the bone, flux of blood or hot abscess, then you oppose it with what is fitting, and it is that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and make the wound rest until the abscess is settled, and you are sure of the flux of blood; and afterwards begin in the circumference of the bone and in the removal of it.
And this be according to one of two manners of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a narrow chisel, and this is its form: And then use another chisel, a little wider than that one; and this is its form:
And then use a chisel wider than the second one.
It's advisable that you have a number of different chisels, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are very sharp at their ends, and are made of Indian or Damascus steel.
Use them with care in striking the chisel so that you don't shake the head and harm it.
If the bone is very hard, then it's advisable to drill around its circumference before you use the chisels, with drills that are called non-penetrating drills.
They are called this because they don't pass through the entire thickness of the skull bone, because the drill has a round end or edge above the sharp part, like a small circle, which prevents it from submerging and passing through the thickness of the bone.
It's advisable that you have a large number of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have one for all skull bones, the end of which should be sharp, the length according to the thickness of this skull.
And this is the form of three types of drills: The method of drilling around the broken bone is that you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; and then move the drill to another spot, and place it between each hole at a distance of about the thickness of a needle.
And then cut with chisels what is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as you can with great care, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other tools which you have prepared for this, such as pliers and fine forceps.
It's important that you take great care that the chisel doesn't touch any part of the dura mater.
And when you have drilled around the bone, and it is separated from the dura mater if it was attached to it, and you have extracted it, you should scrape and smooth its rough edges with another tool similar to a chisel, but it should be thinner and finer than the other chisels.
If any small pieces of bone remain and are stuck, then remove them carefully with the tools you have; and after that, treat the wound with lint and the ointments we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is easier, less frightening, which Galen described and praised highly; and this is his description: it is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and most visible, until when you have uncovered that part and have placed under it the end of this gentle and pleasant chisel, of which this is the form:
And it is that the lenticular part is smooth and doesn't cut anything, and the sharp part of it is on two sides extending in length, as you see; that the lenticular part should be placed against the dura mater with its sharp cutting face on the bone.
And then strike the chisel on one side with a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone easily as it is turned, and you can be sure that no accident will happen to the membrane that you might fear in any way, even if the physician was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; indeed, even if he were sleeping.
Then if something remains attached to the bone of the membrane in some places of the bone, cut it from it with the extremity of that lenticular incision tool, and free it from it with ease, for it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the membrane, and what is cut or removed from the bone is its face, and roughness and subtle fragments remain in it, then it is necessary that you scrape that roughness and eradicate those fragments with subtle razors for which you have prepared a number, of diverse quantities so that it is possible for you to administer from them in every place the most convenient of them and the most direct of them according to what your operation leads you to that broken bone and its shape.
