And it is necessary that you administer in your scraping with which you scrape the bone at the beginning the widest of those razors, and afterwards use after it the most subtle of it, and do not cease doing this continuously until you come to administer the thinnest and most subtle of all those.
But in each of the other small capillary fractures and subtle fracture, it is necessary that you administer care according to what leads to its rectification.
And it is a matter that is not diminished over all those who have studied in that art. And I stood on what we have written shown in great fractures.
And if the membrane is retained when you eradicate the bone, then it is necessary that you take linen cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge that in rose oil, and place that over the orifice of the wound; and afterwards take another cloth doubled or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place that over the thin cloth and do this as lightly as you can over that, that you do not burden the brain; and afterwards administer over that wide bandage, and do not tighten it except by the quantity that holds the wound only.
And leave that with this care for a day or for two until you are sure of hot apostema; and afterwards untie that and afterwards administer medicines with which there is superfluous dryness, such as flax root and vetch flour, and powder of incense and birthwort, and similar to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that is pulverized over the wound, as they are dry; and universally, administer in that every medicine of property of which is that it cleanses and does not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and let there not be in it filth of ointment or other; nor do not let virulence aggregate in it; for when it is gathered over the membrane of the brain, it corrupts that and rots that, and befalls the patient after that evil malignancy.
And it befalls in the brain of the head, when of it the bone is cleansed and especially when the care of it is despised, and there is blackness in the surface of it; and then consider then if there befall the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious without any doubt.
And if blackness does not befall except from the medicine that is placed above, and it is in virtue of that medicine that it makes that blackness, then it is necessary that you take of honey one part and of rose oil three parts, and with those anoint a cloth, and afterwards place that over the brain; and afterwards cure that with the species of cure that is convenient until it be healed.
Chapter 3
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that only two parts of the nose are broken together at the top, or sometimes one of them, because in it there are two bones, as the lower part is cartilaginous, and is not broken and does not suffer anything but abrasion and twisting and bending.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert the little finger into his nose, and straighten that fracture inside with your index finger, and with the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and be careful that no pain falls upon the patient through your operation.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it should be evened out with the end of a stick which has a small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same as this.
And hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then after the 7th or 10th day you should rest the hot swelling.
And afterwards insert into the end of the nose a wick of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or put two tents, if the fracture is in two parts, and let there be in the tent a thickness of such quantity that it fills the hole of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the tents should be soaked in butter, and should be changed every day, but it is not seen by me; rather, the wick should be soaked in egg white mixed with mill dust; and afterwards leave the tent until the bone is firm and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes a goose feather should be inserted into the nose, instead of a tent, after it is wrapped in soft linen cloth; and this is when the fracture of the nose is more severe and the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary for you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, make tents.
And if a hot swelling occurs again, plaster the nose with a wax ointment or with cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if no hot swelling occurs, then you should plaster it externally with sesame flour and incense powder mixed with egg white; and afterwards place soft tow on it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then you should cut over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for them; and afterwards sew the incision, and treat it with what incarnates and consolidates, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with tents and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4
On the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not with a wound, then observe if the fracture is only external, and not broken in two and is curved towards the inner parts, then its recognition is easy.
And so it is suitable, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with it the bulge of the fracture inwards with ease to the outer parts, with your other hand on the outer part of the bone; with that decently equalize it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated in two, then it is suitable that you administer extension of two parts according to straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if tooth movement occurs to disruption, then tighten those which you hope will remain with gold or silver thread or silk; and after place wax on the broken jaw, and after place a doubled cloth over it, and place over the cloth a large, decently made splint, or a piece of sole equal to the length of the jaw; and after tie it above according to how the binding of it is prepared for you and that suits the application, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and place his food as light broths.
And if you estimate that the figure is already altered in some way, then you hurry and untie it on the third day; and after rectify what is already altered of it and plaster it with mill powder, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the wax from it, and place light wool over the plaster.
And then as that plaster stays attached and adjusted over it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not untie it until it is healed and tighten the fracture; for many times this fracture is tightened in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of bone is separated or splintered, then you subtly remove those splinters with what is suitable for that in the removal of those from the instruments.
And if the orifice of the wound is narrow, then you amplify it with the phlebotomy according to the quantity of your necessity.
And after when you have removed those splinters, and of those nothing will be left, then you sew the orifice of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that are suitable for that and incarnate it, until it is healed.
Chapter 5
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Many times the collarbone is broken on the front part towards the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
For either it is broken, or separated, or it is separated in two parts other than that splinters do not occur; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that splinters occur in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation on it, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the upper arm and follows the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
And after equalize the fracture with your fingers until its form is made as it should be, and there is neither small protrusion nor concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the patient's armpit a roll of cloth or wool, and let its size be according to the quantity of your need; and extend and elevate the collarbone and tighten the roll with your hand until you align the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the extremity of the collarbone to the outer parts, because it has sunk deep, then it is necessary that the patient be laid on his back, and place under his shoulder a medium-sized cushion.
And the assistant should press his shoulder downwards until the collarbone bone that is deep down is raised up, and then you rectify the fracture and align it with your fingers.
But if you feel fragments of the collarbone and they are moved, then it is necessary that you cut over it and extract those fragments with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should make an incision of it with one of the cutting tools which you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the collarbone the instrument that protects the peritoneum; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is its form:
And it is similar to a spoon of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to what you need of it, according to the size of the bone or the smallness of it.
But its length should be according to what is possible for you in the operation; that it has two extremities as you see, and one extremity should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have cut when you have extracted the fragment from the bone is wide, and you are sure of hot inflammation, then you join the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear hot inflammation, then you fill the wound with cloths and small pads according to the size of the cut of the wound.
And if hot inflammation occurs, then soak a cloth in vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it over that.
And all, that the splint should not be moved from above the broken bone; and for this it is necessary that you see the patient every day, that when the binding is softened or you see the splint has already been moved, then you rectify this and tighten the binding.
And afterwards place the patient's sleep on his neck, and place under his armpit towards his sleeping side at night a small cushion; so that with it his upper arm is elevated on that side, and thus the fracture of the collarbone is elevated by the elevation of the shoulder; or let his arm be tied to his neck.
And do not untie the binding unless itching or inflammation occurs in the place, until 12 days.
And afterwards renew the plaster if you have seen its face for this, and increase in the tightening and restore it and leave until the fracture of the collarbone is restored and coagulated.
And it is tightened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6
On the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The shoulder blade is rarely broken in its wide part and only its edges are broken.
And so when the wide part is broken - and it is broken in the middle of it - and it is not known except by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture, strive to prepare it and reduce it to its natural shape by any means possible to you.
And after, place on the spot powder of mill with egg white and soft tow; and after place a double cloth compress; and after place on it a wide splint of thin board according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if under the splint there is a concavity in any of the places of the shoulder blade, then you level that concavity with soft tow, and after raise the splint evenly.
And after tighten above with a long bandage with decent constriction and with firmness of the splint until it is not moved from its place.
And check the binding every day; and every time it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved from its place.
And let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 days or in 25.
And so when this number is completed, then you undo the binding, and you should be assured that it is one of the bones whose comminution is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if a piece of bone protrudes from the place and pokes under the skin, then you cut over it, and remove it, and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the clavicle about treating a hot abscess if it occurs there.
Chapter 7
On the restoration of a fracture of the chest.
The chest is sometimes broken in its middle part and this rarely happens; but its extremities are very often cracked and comminuted.
And of the accidents when its middle is broken is that it declines to the inner parts and great pain occurs, and difficulty in breathing, and cough, and very easily vomits blood; and a cavity is declared in the broken bone and is not hidden to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient be laid on his back, and place a pillow between his shoulders; and after press on his muscles, and join his ribs with the hands on both sides, and be subtle in its operation and in the adjustment according to every manner that is possible and that is convenient to it, until the shape of the bone returns as it should.
And after place on it a plaster and tow and place above a splint of thin board of willow or of reed and similar to them in lightness, after you wrap it in cloth; and after be subtle in binding it over the broken bone so that it is not moved; and go with the binding according to roundness to the back sometimes, and tighten it with decent stricture; and after check the binding every hour, and when it is loosened tighten it.
And if necessity provokes to unbind it due to corrosion or itching, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the place, then you hasten and unbind it and eradicate the plaster and rectify what occurs by this with the manner of curing it; and after replace the plaster, if you see fit; and add to it stricture until it is healed.
Chapter 8
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that fracture of the ribs only occurs in thick places that follow the back; and from their extremities of the front part, contraction does not happen to them except because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense towards the inquiry of it with the fingers.
And the restoration should be thus that you equalize the fracture with the fingers according to the possible manner, until it is aligned as it should be, and afterwards plaster and tighten the broken bone with a splint, if it needs that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining to the inner parts, then severe pain and puncture occurs to the patient, just as puncture that occurs to one who has pleurisy, and because the bone pricks the membrane; and difficulty of breathing occurs to him, and cough, and vomiting of much blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the Ancients have contrived in that with many contrivances.
And of them is what they say: it is fitting that you set foods for the patient that generate inflation and windiness, so that the belly may be inflated and extended, and the fracture may be impelled to the outer parts.
But we abhor this because it may not be a light cause to generate a hot abscess, if it does not occur; and if it has already occurred, then it already helps with this.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed above and afterwards that it should suck with strength; and it is similar to reasoning, but nevertheless it is feared that the cupping glass attracts the superfluities to the place due to the weakness.
And some of them said: it is fitting that the place be covered with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and that cushions be placed between the ribs until it is filled, that the bandage be equal when you wrap it according to roundness; and afterwards the patient should be cured with treatment of pleurisy of food and medicine.
And if a vehement cause constrains the patient which is not tolerated, and the bone pricks the membrane with a harmful puncture, we fear for the patient, then it is fitting that we cut over the place, uncover the broken rib, and afterwards place over it an instrument that preserves the peritoneum, the remembrance of which is preceded, and that we cut the bone with ease, and extract it.
And afterwards that we join the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suturing, and cure it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you hasten and soak cushions in rose oil and place on the location, and that the patient be cured with that which is suitable for the inner abscess.
And he should lie supine on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and rarely contusions occur to them, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will heal or not, then consider, because if you see their hands already softened and numb and dead and they cannot move them or extend or tighten them and when you squeeze them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and they don't feel pain in them, then know that they will not heal, and in most cases it is fatal.
And if they move them and feel constriction with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if something similar happens to the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if they will heal or not, then consider their feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, the same thing happens to them that happened to the hands, and after when he lies on his back, wind and excrement come out without his will, and sometimes he is lying on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is fatal, and then do not trouble yourself with his treatment.
But if none of those things happen, the case is better and easier.
And his treatment is that you strive to reduce the hot swelling by placing rose oil alone on the bruised vertebra, or with enough egg yolks; place that on it several times a day until the hot swelling is reduced.
And then place one of the strengthening and stimulating plasters, and tighten it with a bandage; and command the patient to rest and be tranquil and not to sleep on the part where he finds pain until he is healed.
And if near the bruise of the bone you find splinters or something that has already separated from it, then it is fitting that you cut into the skin and remove that bone; and afterwards join the two lips of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and afterwards treat it with some incarnative ointment, until it is healed.
But if the back of the bone is broken, and it is the end of the tail, then it is fitting that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and that you align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible for a second alignment; and afterwards place a plaster on it and splint, if you need those, and afterwards tighten it.
And if you feel broken splinters in it, then you cut over it, and remove that, and treat the wound with the treatment that has preceded until it is healed.
Chapter 10
On the restoration of the hip.
The bones of the hips are rarely broken, and if they break, then their fracture is nothing but a crushing in their extremities and they are cut in the length and decline towards the inner parts; and the patient experiences pain in the place and puncture, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And its restoration is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture which is its shape.
And if the fracture is in its extremity only, then you align it according to what is prepared for you by equal alignment until it is resembled to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the length or has already declined to the lower parts, then you make the patient lie on his belly until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And when you have evened that out, place it on the plaster, and then place a splint of wood or bark on it, and tighten it with a bandage over which you don't fear puncturing the fracture or removing the splint.
And even out the concavity with what fills it until it takes the bandage evenly, and order the patient to sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you stop extending it and restoring it until the hot abscess subsides, and place on it what calms it as previously stated; and then return to restoring it, and tighten it as appropriate.
And if in the bone there are splinters or it is comminuted, then you remove them.
Chapter 11.
On restoring the fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is what is between the elbow up to the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a flat wood of medium thickness according to this form:
And two bandages are tied at the two ends, and then it is suspended from an elevated place.
And the patient sits on a chair; and then throw his broken arm over the wood until it reaches its armpit in the middle of which the curve of the wood is attached.
And then suspend over it some heavy thing, or the assistant extends it to the lower parts; and then the physician evens out the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other way is that the patient lies on his back and suspends the hand from his neck with a bandage; and then order two assistants that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the lower part of it; and each of them extend to his side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten over the fracture with a bandage and over that with another bandage, and each of the two assistants extend to their two sides.
And so if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is appropriate that half of the bandage be placed over the armpit and the other below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is close to the elbow, then it is appropriate that the bandage be placed over that place and over that elbow.
Even it out with ease and without effort, and then when it will be evened out as it should be and is applied with good application, then tighten it if a hot abscess does not occur.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the tightening until the seventh day, and place on it dirty wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is that you place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then bring the arm close to that humerus, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place the cloth and tighten over the humerus the arm so that the arm is in place of splints, unless something from the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is that you place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten over the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your tightening over the place of the fracture should be tighter. And the further the fracture extends, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the stars and the structure as we have said at the hour in which you restore the limb to its position, then you do.
And if you fear a hot abscess, then you leave the structure and the stars until the seventh day as we have said.
And afterwards consider the binding in all parts for three days so that itching or swelling does not occur in the place; or food is prohibited from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness; and so rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage except after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hands be on his stomach.
And place on the aid a cushion full of wool evenly, and consider at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not untied or dissolved, or that the binding is softened, so rectify all this with your strength.
And set the patient's food according to the order which we have put before, so that the food is subtle at the beginning until it starts to tighten; and then it is appropriate that you thicken his food.
For it is customary for his aid and his leg to be tightened for 40 days; and then it is appropriate that they be untied and a bath be administered and be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is widely crushed, then you do not untie from it the binding and the splints until 50 days or until two months.
Chapter 12.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
An arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and is the one that follows the thumb, and the other is large and is the one which is placed below the little finger, on the lower part.
And very easily the major focil is broken alone, or the small one, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small focil above is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when its focil below is broken the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when two bones are broken together.
So if the bone which is broken is the small focil above, then it is convenient for the doctor, towards its restoration, to place a small extension with ease until it is evened out.
But if the large focil is what is broken, then it is appropriate that he place a more vehement extension.
And if the two focils are broken together, then it is appropriate that you place the extension much stronger.
And it is appropriate that you place the shape of the hand towards its restoration and its extension stretched out on a cushion, and let the pulse of the hand be to the parts above, higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger be lower than the other fingers, and let the patient be sitting on a square chair.
And let there be a cushion before him at his height or in front of him, so that the patient does not incur fatigue.
And afterwards the minister extends the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a binding, and another minister extends above similarly.
And afterwards the doctor evens out the bone until he reduces it according to the best shape he can.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then strive to reduce every piece to its place with your strength.
And if there appears in it a separated splinter that pierces the skin and you have no hope in its restoration, then you cut over it and remove it according to the manner which we have said above.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for this, and so take the cure from that.
And if a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you anoint a cloth with a cerot made of rose oil and white wax, and let it be between thick and thin, and tighten the cloth over it with a subtle tightening until the abscess has subsided; and then remove the cerot and apply a plaster prepared from mill powder with egg white, and then apply splints; and let the splint which is placed over that fracture be wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in the major dispositions, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
And then place your tightening over the site of the fracture more strongly and more vehemently, and the more the tightening will be on the parts above the fracture or on the lower parts of it, place the tightening a little softer according to what is preceded by the remembrance of it at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture should be soft, damp cloths, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is tightened should be of linen properly between thin and thick, as we have recounted.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any cause occurs for which rectification is necessary, such as itching which occurs in the limb, then it is advisable that you bathe the limb with hot water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstricted until it rests, and then return the tightening.
And if the tightening has already softened and the bone has already moved and similar to those, then rectify all this with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening, for then it is advisable that you soften it a little and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if none of those things we have mentioned happens to the patient, then it is not advisable that it be untied until after 20 days or thereabouts.
And afterwards let the patient's hand be suspended by its power of agitating movement, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or in 32 days, or very quickly it is restored in 28 days, and all this is according to the disposition of the complexions and the dispositions of strength.
Chapter 13
On restoring fractures of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures of the wrist and finger joints rarely occur, and what happens to them is usually bruising.
And when a fracture or bruise happens to the hand, then it's necessary for the patient to sit square and in front of you sitting equally, and then place their hand extended on it.
And then the assistant extends the broken bones and the doctor aligns them until they are joined with good connection.
And then it's necessary to apply a plaster and tow, if there's no hot swelling; and then place a splint on top according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts towards the palm, then make a semblance of a cloth ball, and order the patient to squeeze it with their fractured palm; afterwards it should be tightened with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather with cloth in which there is lightness, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as is appropriate.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it's necessary to place a splint on top and another splint on the part below in the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the bandage, as the hand is wrapped to the wrist between the fingers with bindings.
And if a fracture happens to any of the finger joints, then if it's the thumb, let it be aligned as is appropriate and then be tightened with the palm and if you want, place a small straight splint on it so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle one, or in the index, or in the little finger, or in the ring finger, then let it be aligned and tied with the healthy finger which follows it; or let them all be tied in continuation, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and after it, the disposition of hot swelling and oppose — with your operation — with what is appropriate when any of these things happen, according to what is repeated in the account of it.
Chapter 14.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and it is evident to the touch as it is turned to the front and upper part.
And its restoration is done in this way: the bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient is laid on his stomach and face.
And then the minister extends all the bandage to his part according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then the bandage should be close to the knee on the groin side, so that the extension falls upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly if the break is close to the knee, then the bandage should be made close to the knee, and the extension should be to the lower parts.
And then the doctor aligns the bone with both his hands until he reduces it according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by good connection; then it is appropriate to apply a plaster and tightening, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then you leave it for a few days until the hot abscess has subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The tightening of it should be done thus: it is appropriate that you wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, and double it two or three times, and remove excess from it; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the heel and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two sides, and then wrap over the leg and thigh what is left over from the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the location of that fracture, and place one of those splints on the leg bone.
And then fill the empty spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the tightening is consolidated.
And then begin with strong tightening from the middle over the fracture site with three or four wrappings, and the further you go from the fracture site with the bandage, your tightening should be less and lighter and softer.
And then extend to two ends of the thread to the lower parts until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not moved from its place.
And then leave the tightening on it, where it stays thus so that no corrosion or hot abscess or swelling and similar things occur to the limb; and if any of these things occur, then hurry to loosen it, and rectify what occurs all afterwards, as we have said many times.
But if in the bone there is a piece of bone which pricks, then it is appropriate that you align it as much as possible; and if not, cut over it and remove it, and treat with that which has been previously mentioned until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened beyond this so that splints are added to it, as we have said in the help and in the arm.
But however in its restoration done in such a manner, lameness does not occur to the patient; and if it is restored alone, beyond that the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he limps from which it is always.
And know that the thigh is tightened in 40 days or a little more or a little less, and all this is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
On the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that fracture of the kneecap rarely happens, but bruising often occurs to it.
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and above all this is with feeling.
And its restoration is thus: that what is broken of its parts be aligned with the fingers until it is joined and connected as much as possible by alignment, ease, and construction.
And afterwards apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need one; and tighten above with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the conditions which we have recounted in other fractures, such as hot swelling and similar to those, so that you oppose every accident with what is suitable for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
On the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is thick, and is named the leg bone; and the other is thin, and is named the fibula.
And each of the types of fracture that happen to the two bones of the arm can happen to these, and for this reason the restoration is done just as the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is turned to all sides.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is turned to the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find it below, and it is one of those things that are not hidden from you.
And then administer extension and alignment and binding of the splints accordingly equally.
But it is suitable, if the fracture of the leg is ugly having many fragments, that the extension be less and lighter, and alleviate with your skill.
And in the leg there is some operation above the arm, and it is when you align the splints and complete all your operation, then you will take pieces of pine wood which is placed under the things which are placed in shoes, and are also made of palm branches; choose from those which have little thickness, and not from the thin ones, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the bottom.
And then wrap cloth over each in two wrappings according to its length, and place one over the part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and let them be from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places at both ends and in the middle, for with this tightening the leg is prevented from bending to the right and left side, and stays in a good position.
And a wooden arm is already administered according to the length of the leg; and let the leg be placed in it so that it preserves it from movement.
And it is especially suitable that you do this in fracture, and when there is a wound with it properly.
And afterwards consider the leg each day and study it with the utmost care, and guard it from swelling and inflammation or other similar things; and when any of these things happen, then oppose it with what is suitable until it is healed.
Chapter 17
On the fracture of the heel, the bones of the foot, and the toes.
The heel doesn't fracture completely; but the bones of the foot fracture sometimes; and the toes rarely fracture, and only suffer abrasion in most cases.
And if the bones of the foot fracture and you see those bones elevated above each other, then the patient should place their foot on the ground prepared as if walking.
And then place one foot on what is elevated of those bones, and then press them, and push, and align them until they return to their places.
And then place a plaster and stop above, and place under the sole of the foot a small board which has two flat ends.
And then tighten it with the foot with proper binding, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things that you need.
And when three or four days have passed, then you untie the binding, because you will find the bones aligned, whether they are broken or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if any toes are broken, then restore them, and align them as I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. And then place on the broken toe a splint according to the length of the toe, and let it be a little wider than it; and then under the foot this board which I have described to you, and tighten it with proper binding.
But if what is broken of the bones are two or three or more, then you place on each toe a splint of its size wrapped in light cloth, and tighten the board at the lowest part of the foot, and let it be standing on one of the ends of the board outside the sole of the foot so that it holds with good retention.
And it is convenient for you not to forget to consider all the things which I have told you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have said; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is suitable.
Chapter 18
On the fracture of the woman's vagina and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the woman's vagina is broken, then make her sit in a square chair, and then incline her to the back part a little, and let her be held from behind.
And then the midwife fills the vagina with cotton, and let it be done in her vagina as a sphere; and then the woman should be shaken, and raise her back little by little, because all of this is thrown out until it is in front of the part of the vagina as a sphere and the broken bone returns.
And then place pillows on her back, and when she wants to urinate remove the cotton with ease and then urinate, and reduce and return it according to the manner in which you placed it at the beginning, and then return to her first pillow.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, because it is restored.
And if you want, take a sheep's bladder and tighten over its hole a reed tube; and introduce the whole bladder into her vagina, and then blow into the tube with strength until the bladder is inflated inside the vagina, because the fracture returns.
And then let it be filled with cotton and stay for some days as we have recounted, until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and binding what we have recounted for the hip bone.
And what is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures that rarely occur, especially to one who has some study and use and understands this book of mine well; because that fracture indicates to you in the major dispositions a straight way in its restoration and in its binding, and therefore understand well.
But in the man's penis when it is broken, take a goose's throat, and put the penis in it; and then place over it a wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and then let it be left for three days or thereabouts, and let it be healed.
Chapter 19.
On the restoration of bone fractures when there is a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large like the thigh bone or the arm bone, or similar to that, that you hurry and bleed them immediately if the conditions for bloodletting are testified to you as we have said above.
And if the wound bleeds, then it is necessary that you hurry to retain it so that you powder over it crushed zinc, if you have nothing else present.
And after begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you leave the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth day at all, for you will cause bad accidents to happen.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin uncovered, then it is necessary that you strive to reduce it and equalize it with your hands with ease and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the equation of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and it is an instrument which is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingers and the width of which is according to the size of the wound; and for this it is necessary for the doctor to take three or four of these according to the quantity that is needed for this in the cure in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let there be little thickness in it so that it is not doubled towards the compression on it at the time of the operation.
And let it be of sharp extremity to which there is a margin at its extremities; and let the top of it tend to thickness, and from the middle of it to the parts.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", that is a small spatula.
Therefore it is necessary that the sharp raised extremity of it be placed on the extremity of the prominent bone.
And push it at once until the bone returns, and is equalized with some equation.
And then strive to equalize the extremities of the fracture between them, and if the extremity of the broken one is subtle, and the instrument does not take it by good acceptance, then it is necessary that you cut the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone at all with what we have recounted, then cut it with what is convenient to it of the cutting instruments which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And after, scrape what remains in the bone of roughness and small scales.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement pain hurting, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do this for you will confer great help to the patient.
When your restoration which you do to the bone is completed, then submerge a cloth in styptic black wine, and properly if it is in summer, and do not put on the wound wax or other thing in which there is oil, so that it does not make putrefaction and corruption occur in it.
And after administer splints at the time of your completion of the restoration of the bone, and leave the wound uncovered so that you cut the hole with scissors and use wrappings according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not tighten the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish doctors have done this, and caused death or corroding disease or suppuration, that is sanies, to happen to their patients. And if your tightening is light, soft, different from the tightening of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you fear over it some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the place, then it is not convenient that you put splints on it, but make for it a wrapping of hard cloths in place of splints and tighten it with those.
And when after a day or two you see the wound beginning to form pus, then remove the cloth which you had placed over it with wine, and afterwards apply poultices, ointments with which we customarily treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is advisable that you unbind the bandage and examine the wound each day morning and evening until it is consolidated and firm, and it is appropriate that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows from it to the lower parts with ease.
If many days pass and the wound is not consolidated nor is the pus removed from it, then know that there are small fragments there, so it is necessary to probe the wound with a probe. And of those fragments, those that are separated remove and extract, those that are not separated on their own and are pricking the limb and causing pain, force yourself to cut those out as much as possible for you.
And if the wound develops gangrene or necrosis, that is, tissue death, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you apply to each of these what is appropriate for its treatment, of which an account has been given in its chapter.
And in this it is appropriate that my discourse remains and you keep your mind present: when a large bone is broken, protruding over the limb such as the thighbone and the humerus and similar ones of the large limbs, and that you do not attempt to pull it or extract it because many times death occurs because of this; rather, leave it until it rots, for it will fall off by itself after 20 or 30 days; and then treat the wound if it seems right to you, and if not, leave it.
Chapter 20.
On the treatment of nodules that occur in the site of some fractures.
Many times this nodule occurs in the site of healing of a fracture, and mainly near the joints, and the shape of the limb is disfigured by it; and it easily prevents the limb from its natural operation.
Then consider if the nodule is recent and apply to it medicines that have astringent properties such as aloe, incense, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar ones; so that you take some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar, and place this on the nodule in tow, and bind these over it with good pressure.
And leave the bandage so that you do not unbind it for many days; and afterwards unbind it and repeat another, until the nodule is removed; or tighten over it a lead plate decently, because lead has the property of removing all that is swollen in the limbs.
But if the nodule has already turned to stone and is tight and necessity urges to remove it quickly, then cut it at the highest part of it, and cut away the excess and protrusion, or scrape it with some razors until it is removed; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
On the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and afterwards the limb remains thin and weak not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and afterwards the limb remains thin and weak, then this is not done except for many reasons: of which one is the multitude of loosening of the bandage and of its binding not done as it should be; or by excess tightness of the bandages until the nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb; or by excessive fomentation; or by excessive movement not at the right time; or by scarcity of blood in the body of the patient and by his weakness.
And the treatment for this is to give food to the patient and make his heart tender until the blood is multiplied in him; and administer baths; and introduce joy and happiness to him.
And afterwards place pitch on the limb, so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or continue fomentation of it with lukewarm water, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural shape.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of broken bones when they have healed crookedly and prevent their proper function.
When it happens to a member already healed after its recovery from crookedness and protrusion of the broken bone and swelling, and because of this the shape of the member is deformed, but the member is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the word of one who thinks that member is newly broken.
But there have already been many foolish doctors and healers who have done this in our land; and this operation is very blameworthy, leading to great fear, the sign of which is deadly.
But if lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be fomented with lukewarm water in which softening herbs have been cooked, such as marshmallow leaves and roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and a softening plaster should be made for it such as well-made diachylon; or mucilage of marshmallow root should be taken and mixed with chicken fat or sesame oil, and plastered with that; or fat figs should be taken and crushed with pigeon droppings, and similar ones of the medicines that are named to reduce consolidation.
And sometimes it is dissolved with constant gentle friction with moist hands, and movement of the member should be administered to every part at all hours.
But if the crookedness has already gone before and has become strong and turned to stone and the need for its treatment with iron has arisen, then the uppermost should be cut and the continuity of the bone should be dissolved, and what is superfluous of its swelling should be cut away with the bone using subtle incisions, and ease should be administered to it with care and study.
Chapter 23.
On the discourse of dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of one of the joints from its place, whereby it is prevented from movement, and the shape of the member is deformed, and it causes great pains and injuries to come upon the patient.
So when dislocation happens to someone, then one should strive immediately to reduce it, and not delay that reduction, because if it is delayed, the place becomes swollen and very easily with this the reduction of dislocation becomes difficult.
And therefore it should not be moved or stretched at the time of swelling, because many times it causes spasm and harmful pains.
But when this happens, then you should hasten to bleed the patient, and afterwards let it be until the swelling rests; and afterwards the member should be fomented with hot water and oil, and afterwards reduced with ease.
And every member should be treated with that which remembrance will come in its place.
And I have already ordered the chapters of dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the uppermost part of the body to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Both jaws are rarely dislocated; and the dislocation of both happens in one of two ways: either they are slightly moved from their places, and are a little softened; or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they are softened at the joint area until saliva runs from the patient and he cannot hold it nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is impaired in his speech.
But when the dislocation is small, then in most cases it returns by itself with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete, then it is necessary that reduction be administered gently, and not delayed at all.
And it is that the assistant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the jaw dislocation inside his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or he should insert both his thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands should be outside with which he aligns; and he commands the patient to relax his jaws and let them go on each side; and the physician aligns the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easy and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And then when we return and they are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place cloth powders with cerate on them, and let it be made of wax and rose oil; and afterwards tie it gently with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his head be between two pillows, so that it does not move to the right side or to the left; and do not force him to chew anything; rather let his food be light broth, until when the pains are removed and the jaws are joined, then he may move what he wants.
And let this be administered with ease, and do not labor over the opening of the mouth towards eating and drinking and yawning, until the jaw is joined and healed.
And if the reduction of two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and are not converted to their places, then many times fevers and persistent headaches occur, and very soon the patient's belly is loosened, and very soon he vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is perdition; and many times he to whom this happens dies in 10 days.
Chapter 25.
On the reduction of dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the shoulder.
The clavicle is not dislocated on the inside due to its continuity with the chest; and sometimes it is dislocated to the outside part, and this is evident to the touch.
And its restoration is that the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms. And afterwards constrict the place with your palm with strong constriction, for it will return.
And afterwards place a plaster and powders on it and tighten it.
But the extremity of that which follows the elbow and is continuous with it, is not dislocated in any day; then it is necessary that it be reduced and aligned as we have said and as is prepared for you.
And afterwards place a plaster on it, and powders, and a bandage, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the shoulder is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
On the reduction of muscle dislocation.
Know that the muscle is only dislocated in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated from the armpit area to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this happens rarely; and it is not dislocated to the back parts because of the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front parts because of the nerves.
And it is mainly dislocated and comes out towards the lower parts near the armpit, and especially in those who have little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those who have much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And very easily it happens to some men by a blow or a fall, and their muscle becomes inflamed with a hot swelling, and it is thought because of this that it is already dislocated.
Then it is necessary that you examine this until you are certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts near the armpit; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, for you find between them a manifest difference; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity and below the armpit towards the touch of the muscle head as if an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all directions.
If it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards, and you then find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And from this dislocation, an easy reduction is made when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And its reduction is that the assistant raises his hand to the upper parts; and then place your thumbs below his armpit, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place, and the assistant raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then place it below for it returns easily.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and it is from the beginning when the dislocation occurred, many days, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in hot water and an embrocation be administered that softens and soothes as if roots of marshmallow, and fenugreek, and melilot are cooked in water, and be administered.
And then the patient should lie on his back and a sphere of wool should be placed below his armpit that is equal between lightness and tightness, and then the doctor should place his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the muscle with strength, and pull the patient's hand so that it is not moved to the lower parts, and another assistant should hold the patient's head, so that it is not moved to the lower parts, for it then returns to its place.
And if you want, reduce it according to this manner: and it is that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and he stands on the side of that side, and puts his muscle under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit to the upper parts until the patient is suspended in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's hand from the lower parts of his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that with him another thing be suspended, so that it weighs him down, for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced also in another way: and it is that a long piece of wood is fixed in the ground the head of which is round in shape, like a mortar pestle, that is neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the patient; after soft cloths are placed on the head of the wood, and the patient is standing according to the length of the wood, and then his hand is extended to the lower parts of the other side; and his body is extended on the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
And if it is difficult to reduce it for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this cure: take a piece of wood whose length is two arms' length, and whose width is four fingers, and whose thickness is reduced at the depth of the armpit according to this shape:
And then on the round end, let lion-colored cloth be tied so that the wood does not harm the patient.
And then let it be placed under the patient's armpit, and let the hand or arm be extended over the wood up to the lower parts, and let the wood be tied over the upper arm and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then let the arm be placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and let the hand be extended to the lower parts, and let the other body be suspended on the other side, for the joint will enter soon.
And when its entry is completed in whatever way is possible for you, then it is fitting that you place under the armpit a sphere of wool of equal quantity; and then place a plaster prepared from mill dust with incense and egg white over the entire muscle, as is wrapped above, and then tighten under the armpit with a proper binding; and let the bandage be wrapped over the plaster, and let his hand be attached to his neck, and let it be left, and let his hand not be moved for 7 days.
And it is fitting that the patient's food be small, until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier for its healing.
And after the food should be given in greater quantity after 7 or 5 [days] and let it be exercised with a movement; and then if it is fixed and not softened, then it is already healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity that befalls it or for another reason, then it is fitting that in that case cautery be administered with three holes, as is said before in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after 7 days the bandage is untied and the joint is not fixed, and the plaster and the binding are reapplied to it several times, and it is not fixed and falls and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that its nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are already cut or extended or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
For the dislocation of that which is made towards the chest and the breast or the back parts, the reduction is made with pushing and extension with the hands, until it returns.
And let the other binding be administered to it, and cure it until it is healed.
And if after the healing, heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use baths many times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it can be dislocated in all directions, mainly to the front and back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it falls under sight or touch depending on how it is dislocated, and when you compare the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is clearly evident to you, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you hurry, and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot swelling comes to it, because if a hot swelling comes to it, its reduction is difficult, and it may not heal completely; and especially when it is in the back parts, because it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and of greater pain, and many times death descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that his hand should be extended with both hands, and his arm should be expanded, and the doctor's hands should be above and below the elbow, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then return it so that he doubles the hand with his hand, until he strikes the elbow with the base of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey the reduction, then administer vehement and very strong extension.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and two assistants also hold it so that it is not moved away from the extension.
And afterwards, the arm should be rotated in all directions, after a long doubled cloth is wrapped over his hand, or a bandage.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that these are the things which help in lubricating the joint with ease; and afterwards push the joint with vehement force until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be placed on it in which there is astringency and drying with egg white, and it should be tightened with a descending stricture, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left for some days.
And afterwards it should be untied; and then if the joint is formed in its place, then you untie its bandage and leave it.
And if you see the joint not tightening well, then you repeat the plaster and the bandage, and leave it for some days until it is tightened; and afterwards untie it.
But if heaviness occurs to it after the reduction of the joint, and its disposition is in movement, then administer moistening in a bath and subtle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or place on the joint "aleliati", that is, fat mutton thigh; and afterwards let it be bound, and leave it on the bone day and night. And afterwards remove it, and put it in a bath; and when it sweats, then you rub the joint with moderate friction, and afterwards repeat on it "aleliati", that is, mutton kidney, once, and a second time, and a third, corruption has already come upon the... entry of bath, until it is smoothed.
And if you want, place on it a moist cow's split warmed with butter and tighten over it; and do this several times, because it is moist and reduces to its disposition.
Chapter 28.
On the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
The wrist is sometimes dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy, contrary to other joints, but it is necessary to act quickly in reducing it at the time it is dislocated, before the area becomes swollen or a hot abscess develops.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist on a table and the assistant extends their hand, and the physician places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns to its place.
However, it should be considered if the palm is already turned to the inside of the hand, then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of their hand should be intrinsically on the table, so that the physician's hand falls on that prominence of the joint.
And if it returns immediately, it is good; if not, then tighten it with a plaster to reduce the swelling and leave it; and do not repeat this, because otherwise, it will not be tolerated nor can you attempt the reduction after days have passed.
However, the joint remains according to its distortion; and it does not harm the patient in any way, except that the hand is softened and cannot grip anything; then know that the nerves are cut or crushed, and then there is no remedy except to tighten it with cautery near its fall, and this may confer some benefit.
Therefore, when the wrist is returned, then place on it the plaster which we have described, and let it be left and tightened for 5 days; and afterwards let it be untied and left, and let the hand be exercised.
But if its movement is impeded and some heaviness occurs in it, then soothe it with hot water and with friction sometimes, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in any direction.
And when any of those fingers is dislocated to the back of the palm or to its inside, then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
And afterwards support the tip of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And afterwards suspend it and extend it until it is straightened equally on that day.
And when night comes, bind it according to this description, and do not cease to untie it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for some days until it is tightened.
And do similarly with this, if it is dislocated to the inside of the hand, and bind it to that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated to other parts.
Chapter 30
On the treatment of dislocation of the spine vertebrae.
When a complete dislocation occurs to any of the spine or neck vertebrae, and many vertebrae are moved from their places, then there is no cure for this, as death hastens to the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excretion comes out involuntarily, and they cannot retain it; and often some of their limbs are softened, or both legs, or both arms, or one of these; or a vertebra is moved from its place, for often it is moved and often the removal of these is done in four parts.
Therefore, this has already moved to the back parts and is called hunchback.
But its treatment is that you then consider if the hunchback occurred from childhood, for then there is no cure for it nor is there any complete healing of it.
But for that which comes from falling or from impact or similar to these, the Ancients have already described the methods of treatment with lengthy discourse, of which most return with benefit; but I have already abbreviated that of which little excuses too much of it, due to my proximity to my intended exposition, with which I have explained this and formed from diverse instruments with which they showed this and explained it.
And of these, none is cured except that which occurs in the back properly with what I recount.
And it is that the patient should be extended on their face on a level hill near a wall, and a soft instrument should be extended under them so that their chest is not injured.
And after, a straight beam should be placed, fixed in the ground at the edge of the hill around their head, and another beam around their feet at the other edge of the hill.
And the minister should hold the beam, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another minister should hold the other in the same way.
And after, wrap a firm lion binding over the patient's head and under the armpits; and extend the end of the binding to the beam which is towards their head, and tie it to it; and after tighten the other binding over their hips, their knees, and towards their legs; and after join all the bindings and tie them to the other beam which is towards their feet.
And after, each minister should extend the binding to the part of the beam, and the two beams and the bindings should not be moved from their places in which they are fixed, but however they are not fastened as we have said.
And the doctor should place his palms on the vertebra with strength until it returns, or place a board over it, and after that add his feet on the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board of which the width or length is about three arms' length, and carve in the wall which we said is near the patient a place in which the end of the board enters. And after, place half of the board on the vertebra, and the doctor should place his feet on the other end, and press well until the vertebra is constrained and returns to its place.
And if you wish, work with a lathe which is turned by hand; and it is that you fix in the ground towards the patient's head at the back of the hill two beams, the length of which should be one arm's length, and towards their head at the back of the hill likewise two beams; and let the length between each beam be one palm, and already made in each beam a hole in which the lathe runs, and each should be very firmly fixed in the ground so that it is not moved.
And let a round beam be placed in which is the lathe, and let it be revolved in the two holes of the two beams.
And at the extremity of it is a hole in which the shaft is formed, the length of which is a palm with that which is revolved; and in the two other shafts it is similar to that one.
And after, tighten the bindings that you have tightened on the chest of the patient in the hole which is towards the head of it, and the bindings that you have tightened on the thighs of it in the turn which is towards the feet of it.
And after each turn, let a minister stand who twists the turn with his hand, and the doctor aligns the spine as we have said.
And after the spine is returned, and the place is aligned, then it is appropriate that you place a drying plaster with egg white, and after that, tow; and after place over the plaster a splint of board of which the width is three fingers or thereabouts, and the length of it is a quantity that covers the place of the hump, and over the healthy part of the spine.
And after it is bound with a binding which we have mentioned, and let the patient use subtle nourishment until he is healed.
And if some protrusion remains in the place at the end of the healing, then it is appropriate that medicines are administered that soften and smooth with the administration of the board for a long time, which we have recounted; and a lead plate has already been administered in that place.
And sometimes a protrusion occurs in the back of the spine, and it is thought to be a dislocation; and it is that bone which already protrudes because it is not appropriate that a presumption be made to it with this medicine, for it will very easily cause death to you.
Chapter 31.
On the cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the shoulder only suffer dislocation, and that which happens to the other joints of small removal and concavity does not happen to them.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated to the inner parts, or to the outer parts, or to the front parts, or to the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated to the inner parts, and little to the front parts or to the back parts.
And the sign of its dislocation to the inner parts is that, when you join the healthy leg of the patient with the sick one, it is longer and the knee is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot towards the groin, and the place that follows the groin is swollen with a hot swelling, because the head of the thigh has already arrived there.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs to the outer parts, is that his symptoms are contrary to this symptom.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs to the front parts is that he extends his leg completely, but however he does not bend it without pain that is in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk he cannot do so to the front parts; and his urine is retained and his groin is swollen, and his walking is on the heel.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs to the back parts is that he does not extend the knee, nor can he bend it before he bends the groin, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is towards that place.
But the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, that it has already settled with the one having it after you administer its reduction and it remains according to its disposition, then there is no cure for it at all; and then it is not appropriate that a presumption be made in it.
But in one whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types of dislocation, then hurry to turn the joint and extend it to the inner parts and to the outer parts, and move it to the right and left, for it will very easily return and you will not need another cure.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant and extend the leg of the patient from the lower part, either with his hands or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extends the hand of that person above so that he puts his hands below the patient's armpits; and afterwards tighten with a lion bandage above the root of the thigh, and let another third assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be of him or of the inner part to the groin to the part of the collarbone, or from the back part to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all these be at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this kind of extension is a common kind for all four.
And then if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every kind what I will say about the proper cure.
In the proper reduction of it, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is to the inner parts, it is necessary that the patient be laid on his healthy side; and afterwards place the bandages on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the groin; and afterwards extend the bandage upwards from the part of the groin to the highest part of the collarbone.
And afterwards another strong assistant should take his arms, and reduce the thick place of the sick thigh and extend to the outer parts by vehement extension, for that returns to its place.
And this kind is of easier cure with which this member is reduced than the other kinds.
But if it is excused upon you and does not obey the entry with this kind of cure at all, then it is necessary that the two feet of the patient be tied together with a strong, light bandage, over both ankles and both knees.
And let the length of each from its pair be the quantity of four fingers, and let the sick leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And afterwards let the patient be suspended over the head of the wood, and let it be in the house; and let the length of him from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
And afterwards command a strong young man to decrease the head of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended with the patient, and the other young man decreasing the thigh with strength push, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is to the outer part, it is necessary that the patient lie on a hill as we have recounted in the one having a hump; and let the bandage be tightened over the sick leg properly and over his chest.
And afterwards two woods should be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head; and afterwards let joined woods be placed in the middle of the hill fixed on which wet clothes are wrapped, so that the patient is not damaged because the wood is between his thighs, so that towards the extension to the lower parts it is not attracted.
And afterwards let each assistant extend to his part, and the doctor with his hand equalizes the reduction according to the art.
