And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended lightly, and it is according to this same narration on a hill; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand over the sick groin, and afterwards constrains it with the other hand, and with that constraint places that extended to the lower parts to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and is raised from the ground; rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard thing, as it is necessary the same if his hip is dislocated to the outer parts, according to what we have said of his lying on two hips; and it is on his face and the bandages are extended, as we have said, not long ago.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board over the place where the joint protrudes.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be — and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient stretches his leg and expands it without excuse, then know that the limb has returned as it should — and then you join the two thighs and apply a plaster and tighten with a bandage so that the hip is not moved in any of the parts; and the patient should adhere to rest for three or four days.
And afterward, untie the binding and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is already fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of softening, then you return and plaster it, and tighten it according to your first tightening, and leave it for three days.
And afterward untie it, and make him delay in walking on each for some days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the cure of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated to the outer parts, and to the inner parts, and to the lower parts but to the back parts; and to the front parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his leg with the thigh; then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its manners is that you make the patient sit upright, so that he extends his leg, if he can do so; adhere your back to his face, and place his foot between your feet; and afterward make his knee adhere with your palms, and hold them between your fingers over his knee with strength, and another minister extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to the thigh with effortless lightness.
And afterward plaster it and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and afterward tie them together with a bandage for three or four days, and afterward untie them.
And the patient should not use walking for some days, until it is healed and strengthened.
But if its reduction is excused to you, then administer strong extension with bindings of which in the cure of the hip preceded narration.
Chapter 33.
On the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle sometimes is moved by small movement, and sometimes is dislocated completely, and its dislocation is made either to the inner parts or to the outer parts.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of its removal is of easy reduction, and it is that it be extended with ease with the hands and be equalized until it returns.
But in its cure when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the patient sit upright, and a strong and valiant minister holds him from the back part of his back in the middle place of it.
And afterward you hold with your right hand the foot from the uppermost part, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot in the place of the ankle.
And afterward pull the foot to you with your right hand, and afterward with the left for two times, and afterward extend with the left; and push the chest of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice as we have counted.
And afterward push the chest of the foot to the leg the third time, and you are at the ankle.
Therefore it returns in one or two times according to this manner, and if you see the foot evened out, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for it will return.
But if the reduction is prohibited to you with what we have recounted, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and fasten a stake firmly in the ground, and let it fall between his thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth over him so that it does not hurt the patient.
And after, the minister of him should hold his thighs, and after another minister should extend the foot with his hands or with a binding that ties over the neck of the foot; and after extend each minister against the extension of his partner, and the stake should be standing between the thighs of the patient retaining him, so that his body is not drawn to the lower parts towards his extension.
And after the doctor evens out the dislocation with his hands, and another minister holds the healthy thigh to the lower parts, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore when the dislocation returns and the health of its reduction is declared to you, then place a plaster and tighten it with bindings strongly, and tie the foot with a binding to the leg.
And it is advisable that you fear or guard against the nerves which are behind on the heel, so that the binding on it is not violent and hurts it strongly.
And after leave that for two days or for three; and if the binding is softened, tighten it.
And after untie that on the third day or on the fourth, and prohibit the patient from walking for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no assurance that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted on him, and after that it will not receive healing.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, then it is advisable that you administer in its soothing that which preceded our narration, in another place of healing and of embrocation, until it is removed.
Chapter 34.
On the healing of dislocation of the toes.
It is advisable that that which is dislocated be evened out with little extension without trouble; and that which is not difficult, but is light.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the back of the foot, then it is advisable that you make the patient sit his foot on an even place on the ground or on a table, and be standing as if he were walking.
And after you stand on foot, and place your foot on that which protrudes from the joints, and after press that with your foot with strength until it returns, and that you see that already evened out and no protrusion appears in the place.
And after place under the sole of his foot a board that takes all the foot, to which are two ends, and after tighten that with decent and firm tightening for three days; and after untie that and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is tightened and assured of return.
Chapter 35.
On the types of dislocations that are made with wounds or with fractures or with both together.
When any of these things happens, and you strive in the cure of it and in the restoration of it, many times death follows.
And for this reason it is not advisable that anyone presume to cure it, except one who is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, cautious, not bold nor presumptuous; and who uses in the beginning medicines that only reduce hot swellings, and leaves the patient to divine judgment, except for those for whom you hope for salvation from perdition, with alleviation of the illness, and in which appears some hope. And then strive immediately to make the reduction of it at the beginning of the matter, before a hot swelling occurs.
And then if the limb returns as we want, then administer a regimen which reduces hot swelling and cure the wound with what is suitable for it from the drying ointments.
And if dislocation is with fracture and occurs in the bone with separate fragments, then you strive for their alignment, and do in this what we have said in simple illnesses about what has preceded in their places.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of entering into ways of fear according to what has preceded my preaching to you; for this is better for you towards your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutary to your body truly those in your time. Here end the words of Albucasim perfectly of which God be blessed. Amen.
